I Think With It.

This is how I actually use Claude — for investor emails, ad copy, shipping entire apps over a weekend, and running a studio where AI agents do real work. Not theory. Not prompting hacks. The whole operating system.

By Kayan Mishra — founder, builder, and the person who calls his agentic loops "Ralph Wiggum loops" with zero irony.

What's Inside

Module 1 — Lesson 1

Why Most People Get Bad Outputs — And the One Shift That Fixes Everything

The AI Prompting Course

There's a moment I think about a lot. I was sitting at my desk, staring at a response from Claude that was technically correct but completely useless to me. I'd asked it to help me write an email to an investor, and what I got back read like a Wikipedia article crossed with a cover letter from 2007. Stiff. Over-formal. Full of phrases no human being would ever actually say out loud.

And my first instinct — honestly — was to think, "This tool isn't that good."

I see this all the time now. People try AI once, get something mediocre, and walk away thinking they've seen what it can do. They haven't. What they've seen is what happens when you talk to something incredibly capable without giving it any real information to work with.

That's what this lesson is about. Not prompting tricks or hacks. Something more fundamental — a shift in how you think about the conversation you're having when you type something into an AI tool.

The Real Problem

Here's the pattern I see over and over. Someone opens up Claude or ChatGPT and types something like:

"Write me an email to a client."

And then they get back something generic. A perfectly structured, grammatically correct email that could've been written by anyone, about anything, to anyone. And they're disappointed.

But think about it for a second. If you walked up to a coworker — someone brilliant, someone you trust — and said "Write me an email to a client," they'd look at you and say, "Okay... which client? About what? What's the situation? What tone? What are you trying to get out of this?"

They wouldn't just start writing. They'd need context.

AI is the same way. The difference is that AI won't push back. It won't ask you those questions. It'll just do its best with what you gave it, which was almost nothing. And "doing its best with almost nothing" means defaulting to the safest, most generic version of whatever you asked for.

That's the gap. The gap between what you wanted and what you got isn't a technology problem. It's an information problem. You knew what you meant. The AI didn't.

The Shift

The one shift that changes everything is this: stop thinking of AI as a search engine and start thinking of it as a collaborator who just walked into the room.

A search engine needs a query. A collaborator needs context.

When I work with Claude now — and I use it constantly, for everything from investor emails to ad copy to analyzing market data — I don't start with what I want it to produce. I start with what it needs to know.

That's it. That's the entire shift. Before you type what you want, give it what it needs.

Let me show you what I mean.

The Example

Let's say I need to write a follow-up email to someone I met at a conference. They run a mid-size SaaS company, we had a quick conversation about a potential partnership, and I want to reconnect without being pushy.

Here's what most people type:

"Write a follow-up email after meeting someone at a conference."

And here's what they get — something like:

"Dear [Name], It was a pleasure meeting you at [Conference Name]. I enjoyed our conversation about [Topic]. I would love to explore potential synergies between our organizations. Please let me know if you would be available for a call at your earliest convenience."

It's fine. It's also forgettable, sounds like it was written by a robot, and could've been sent by literally anyone on earth. It's the email equivalent of elevator music.

Now here's how I'd actually prompt it:

"I met James Park at SaaStr Annual last week. He's the CEO of a mid-size SaaS company doing around $15M ARR in the HR tech space. We talked for about ten minutes at the after-party — casual conversation, not a formal meeting. He mentioned they're struggling with their outbound sales process and I think there might be a real partnership opportunity with what we're building. I want to follow up in a way that feels warm and natural, not salesy. Keep it short — five or six sentences max. I write pretty casually in emails, no corporate speak."

Now Claude has something to work with. It knows who James is, what his company does, how we met, the vibe of our conversation, what I'm hoping to accomplish, and how I actually communicate. The output is going to be completely different — specific, natural, and actually useful.

That's the shift in action. Same task. Wildly different results. The only thing that changed was the amount of context I gave it.

Why This Works

There's something going on under the hood that's worth understanding, even if you never want to get technical about it.

AI language models are, at their core, prediction machines. They're predicting the most likely next word based on everything that came before it. When you give a vague prompt, the prediction has to be safe. It has to work for the broadest possible interpretation of what you might have meant. So it defaults to the average — the most generic, least risky version of whatever you asked for.

When you load up the prompt with real, specific context, you narrow the prediction space. You're essentially telling the model, "You're not writing for everyone. You're writing for this specific situation, this specific person, in this specific tone." And the output snaps into focus.

Think of it like giving directions. "Go somewhere fun" could mean a thousand things. "Take me to that Thai place on 5th Street, the one with the outdoor patio" gets you exactly where you want to go. Same idea.

The Concept: Context Is the Input That Matters Most

I want you to walk away from this lesson with one belief locked in: the quality of your output is directly proportional to the quality of your input.

Not the cleverness of your prompt. Not the specific words you use. Not whether you said "please" or "act as an expert." The quality of the context. The real, specific, situational information that helps the AI understand exactly what you're dealing with.

Here's why this matters so much. Most of the prompting advice you'll find online is about tricks. "Use this magic phrase." "Start with this sentence." "Tell it to think step by step." Some of that stuff has its place — and we'll get into it later in this course. But none of it matters if the foundation isn't there. And the foundation is always context.

I've watched people spend twenty minutes engineering the perfect prompt structure when they could've spent thirty seconds just... explaining the situation. Just talking to it like a person. Telling it what's going on, what they need, and why.

That's the unlock. It's weirdly simple, and I think that's why people overlook it. They assume good AI outputs require sophisticated techniques. They don't. They require clear communication. And most of us are already good at clear communication — we just forget to do it when we're typing into a text box.

What Good Context Looks Like

When I say "give it context," I don't mean write an essay. I mean include the information that would matter to a smart person helping you with this task. Here's my general framework — and you don't need all of these every time, just the ones that are relevant:

Who is this for? Not just "a client" — which client? What's their role? What do they care about? What's your relationship with them?

What's the situation? What happened? What's the background? Why is this task happening right now?

What do you actually want? Not just the output format — the real goal. Are you trying to close a deal? Smooth over a conflict? Get someone excited about an idea?

What should it sound like? Your voice matters. Do you write casually or formally? Short sentences or long ones? Are you funny or straightforward? If you don't tell the AI, it's going to default to something that sounds like a LinkedIn post.

What should it NOT be? Sometimes the easiest way to guide the output is to say what you don't want. "Don't make it salesy." "Don't use bullet points." "Don't start with 'I hope this email finds you well.'"

That's it. That's the framework. Who, what's happening, what you want, how it should sound, and what to avoid. You don't need to memorize this. You just need to get in the habit of thinking about it for ten seconds before you type.

Try This Yourself

Here's something I want you to try right now, or the next time you're using Claude or any other AI tool.

Think of something you actually need to write today. An email, a message, a short document — something real. Don't make up a hypothetical.

First, type the prompt the way you normally would. Just your natural instinct. Send it and read what comes back.

Then, try again. But this time, before you type what you want, spend thirty seconds typing out the context. Who's involved, what the situation is, what tone you want, what you're trying to accomplish. Be specific. Use real names, real details, real constraints.

Compare the two outputs.

I'm willing to bet the second one is dramatically better. Not because you used some advanced technique. Because you communicated like a human being talking to another human being, instead of typing a keyword into a search bar.

Why It Works (The Deeper Layer)

There's a reason I'm starting the entire course with this. Everything else we're going to learn — how to iterate, how to manage context over long conversations, how to get AI to write in your voice, how to build systems and automations — all of it builds on this one idea.

If you internalize the habit of leading with context, you'll be better at using AI than ninety percent of people. Not because you know more prompting tricks. Because you understand what's actually happening in the conversation.

You're not giving commands to a machine. You're briefing a collaborator. And the better the briefing, the better the work.

I run my entire business with this mindset. When I need Claude to help me with a competitive analysis, I don't just say "analyze my competitors." I tell it about my company, my market, what I'm trying to figure out, and why it matters right now. When I need it to write ad copy, I don't say "write a Facebook ad." I describe the audience, the product, the emotional angle, the constraints, and what's worked before.

Every single interaction starts with: what does this tool need to know in order to help me well?

And then I just... tell it.

The Common Mistakes

Before we wrap up, let me hit the three biggest mistakes I see people make, now that you know what the shift is.

Mistake one: being vague because you think AI should "just know." It shouldn't. It can't. It's working with what you give it. If you give it nothing, it gives you nothing back. This isn't a flaw in the technology — it's the nature of how it works.

Mistake two: over-engineering the prompt structure instead of just providing information. I see people spending more time on prompt formatting than on actually explaining the task. They'll write "Act as a world-class marketing expert with 20 years of experience who has worked with Fortune 500 companies" and then give zero context about their actual situation. The role-playing can help in some cases, but it's never a substitute for real information.

Mistake three: giving up after one bad output. This is probably the biggest one. You try it once, get something generic, and conclude that AI can't do what you need. But you haven't actually tried. You've asked a vague question and gotten a vague answer. That's not failure — that's a starting point. Give it more context, tell it what was wrong, ask for a revision. The conversation is the product, not the first response.

What's Next

In the next lesson, we're going to break down the anatomy of a great prompt — the specific components you can include and how to structure them. But I want to be clear: structure is a tool, not a requirement. The most important thing you can do is what we talked about today. Lead with context. Communicate like a human. Treat the AI like a smart collaborator who needs to be briefed, not a search engine that needs a keyword.

Start there. Everything else gets easier.

Module 1 — Lesson 10

Why You Should Always Specify Audience in Your Prompt

The AI Prompting Course

I learned this one the hard way. I was working on a product explainer for Official AI — our platform that helps people build AI-powered tools without code. I asked Claude to write a description of how our semantic search feature works. What I got back was technically accurate, well-organized, and completely wrong for my audience.

It read like documentation for a developer. References to vector embeddings, cosine similarity, embedding dimensions. My audience was founders and operators who've never written a line of code. The explanation was correct. It was also useless.

The fix took five seconds. I added one line: "The audience is non-technical founders who want to understand what this does for them, not how it works under the hood." The next version was perfect. Clear, benefit-focused, zero jargon.

That's when I realized: audience isn't optional context. It's the single most important piece of context you can give.

Why Audience Changes Everything

Here's the thing. When you don't specify an audience, Claude has to guess. And its default guess is basically "a generally educated adult who wants comprehensive information." That's fine for some things. It's terrible for most real-world communication.

Think about how different these audiences are:

  • An investor who's seen 500 pitch decks this year and skims everything
  • A potential customer who's never heard of your product category
  • Your engineering team who needs precise technical specs
  • A journalist who needs a quotable soundbite, not a whitepaper
  • Your mom, who you're trying to explain what you do for a living

Same product. Same company. Completely different communication. The words you choose, the level of detail, the examples you use, the tone, the length — all of it shifts based on who's reading.

Claude knows this intuitively. But it can't apply it if you don't tell it who the reader is.

The Specificity Spectrum

There's a range of how specific you can be about audience, and more specific is almost always better.

Vague: "Write this for a business audience."

Better: "Write this for SaaS founders."

Best: "Write this for a non-technical SaaS founder who's doing $1-5M ARR, has a small team, and is evaluating whether to build AI features into their product. They're smart but they don't know the technical details of how LLMs work. They care about cost, speed to market, and competitive advantage."

See the difference? The third version gives Claude a complete mental picture of the person reading. It can now make hundreds of micro-decisions about word choice, detail level, and framing — all calibrated to that specific reader.

The Audience Prompt Pattern

I've settled into a simple pattern I use almost every time I'm asking Claude to write something that someone else will read:

"The audience for this is [who they are]. They [what they know / don't know]. They care about [their priorities]. The tone should be [how it should feel to read]."

Let me show you a real example. I needed to write a Slack message to our team about a change in how we're handling client onboarding. Here's what I told Claude:

"The audience is our internal team — five people, all remote, all pretty casual with each other. They know the current onboarding process. What they don't know is why we're changing it. They'll care most about how this affects their day-to-day workflow. Keep it brief and direct — we communicate in Slack, not memos."

The output read exactly like something I'd actually post in our Slack. No preamble, no formal structure, just a clear explanation of what's changing and why. Because Claude knew who it was writing for.

When Audience Matters Most

You don't need to specify audience for every prompt. If you're asking Claude to help you brainstorm or think through a problem, the audience is you — and Claude already knows that from the conversation.

But any time the output is going to be read by someone else, audience is non-negotiable. That includes:

  • Emails and messages
  • Marketing copy and landing pages
  • Presentations and pitch decks
  • Documentation and SOPs
  • Social media posts
  • Job descriptions and offer letters
  • Investor updates and board materials

For all of these, the question isn't "what do I want to say?" It's "what does this person need to hear, and how do they need to hear it?"

The Two-Audience Trick

One technique I use a lot: when something has multiple audiences, I tell Claude both and ask it to optimize for the primary while not alienating the secondary.

"This landing page is primarily for non-technical founders evaluating AI tools. But technical co-founders will also read it. Make sure the technical claims are defensible without making the page feel like documentation."

This is way more useful than just saying "write a landing page." It gives Claude the nuance to make smart tradeoffs.

Try This Now

Take something you've recently asked Claude to write — an email, a post, anything. Look at the prompt you used. Did you specify who would read it? If not, re-run the prompt with a two-sentence audience description. Compare the outputs.

I'd bet the version with audience context is sharper, more natural, and actually sounds like something that belongs in front of that reader. Because the single fastest way to make AI output feel less "AI-generated" is to tell it exactly who it's generating for.

That's Module 1. You now have the foundation — context, structure, tone, format, and audience. Everything from here builds on these basics. In Module 2, we're going to talk about something that unlocks a whole new level: making Claude remember who you are across conversations.

Lesson 10 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 2 — Lesson 11

Building a Personal Context Document Claude Always Knows About

The AI Prompting Course

Here's something that changed how I work with AI more than any prompting trick I've ever learned: I wrote a document about myself, my company, and how I operate — and I make sure Claude has access to it at the start of every meaningful conversation.

I call it my context document. Some people call it a "user manual." Whatever you call it, the idea is simple: instead of re-explaining who you are and what you do every single time you start a new chat, you write it down once and paste it in.

This sounds obvious. It is obvious. And almost nobody does it.

Why This Matters

Every time you open a new conversation with Claude, it knows nothing about you. Zero. It doesn't know your name, your company, your role, your preferences, your communication style, what you're working on, or what you care about. It's meeting you for the first time, every time.

So what happens? You ask it to help you write an email, and it writes something that sounds like a generic consultant. You ask it to brainstorm marketing angles, and it gives you ideas that have nothing to do with your actual market. You ask it to review a strategy doc, and it gives you MBA-textbook feedback instead of feedback grounded in your specific situation.

The context document fixes all of this in one shot.

What Goes In It

Here's roughly what mine includes — and yours doesn't need to look exactly like this, but this gives you the framework:

Who I am and what I do. I'm the founder of Mudita Studios. We're a venture studio that builds products fast using AI-native development. I'm also building Official AI, a video content generation platform. I work with a small team — Ben handles product engineering, Bree runs operations, Cyrus is on the business side.

How I work. I move fast. I prefer shipping something rough and iterating over planning for weeks. I code with AI agents. I think in systems. I'd rather see a working prototype than a perfect spec document.

How I communicate. Casual, direct, no corporate speak. Short paragraphs. I say what I mean. If something sucks I'll say it sucks. I don't need things sugar-coated and I don't want to sugar-coat things for other people either.

What I'm currently focused on. This changes every few weeks, but right now it might be "rebuilding our onboarding flow" or "preparing a pitch for a potential client" or "launching a new product line." This is the most dynamic part of the document.

Preferences and pet peeves. Don't use bullet points unless I ask. Don't start emails with "I hope this finds you well." Don't give me five options when I asked for one recommendation. Don't hedge — if you think something is bad, tell me it's bad.

How I Actually Use It

In Claude, there's a feature called Projects. You can pin files to a project, and Claude will reference them in every conversation within that project. I have my context document pinned to my main workspace. So when I start a new chat within that project, Claude already knows who I am.

If you're not using Projects, you can also just paste the document at the top of any important conversation. I know — it feels weird to paste 300 words of context before asking a question. But the difference in output quality is massive. You go from generic AI responses to responses that actually sound like they came from someone who works with you.

"Here's my context document: [paste]. Now, given all of that, I need to write a cold outreach email to the head of content at a mid-size DTC brand. We want to pitch them on using our video generation platform for their social content."

That prompt, with the context document, produces something ten times better than the same prompt without it. Because now Claude knows your company, your product, your voice, and your style — before it even starts thinking about the email.

Keep It Updated

The biggest mistake people make with context documents is writing one and never touching it again. Your context changes. Your priorities shift. Your team grows. Your product evolves.

I update mine roughly every two weeks. Not a full rewrite — just tweaking the "current focus" section and adding any new context that's become relevant. It takes five minutes and saves me hours of re-explaining things across conversations.

The Format That Works Best

Keep it under 500 words. Seriously. You don't need your entire life story. You need the information that would help a smart new hire understand your situation on their first day. Write it in plain language, not formal documentation style. Use headers to break it up so Claude can quickly reference specific sections.

Here's the thing — this document isn't just for Claude. Writing it forces you to articulate how you work, what you care about, and what your current priorities actually are. I've found that the act of writing my context document has clarified my own thinking more than once. It's a forcing function for self-awareness.

Try This

Open a blank document right now and write your context document. Start with these five sections: who you are, what you do, how you communicate, what you're currently working on, and what you don't want. Keep each section to two or three sentences. The whole thing should fit on half a page.

Then use it in your next three conversations with Claude. Paste it at the top, then ask whatever you were going to ask. Compare the output to what you'd normally get. You'll never go back to starting from scratch.

Lesson 11 of 100

Module 2 — Lesson 12

How Claude Memory Works — And How to Correct It When It's Wrong

The AI Prompting Course

Claude has a memory system, and it's both more useful and more flawed than most people realize. Understanding how it actually works — not the marketing version, the real mechanics — is one of the highest-leverage things you can learn early on.

Let me break down what's actually happening.

The Two Types of Memory

Claude has two distinct ways of "remembering" things about you. The first is conversation memory — everything said within the current chat. Claude can reference anything you've told it in this session. If you mentioned your company name in message three, Claude still knows it in message thirty. This works well, with one caveat we'll get to in a later lesson about context windows.

The second is persistent memory — things Claude stores about you across conversations. This is the newer feature, and it's the one people misunderstand. Claude will sometimes notice patterns in your conversations and save notes about you. Things like "Kayan runs Mudita Studios" or "prefers casual communication style" or "working on a video generation product."

You can see these saved memories in your settings. And here's where it gets interesting.

When Memory Goes Wrong

I see this all the time — Claude latches onto something from an old conversation and treats it as current truth. A few months ago, I was exploring a completely different business idea. I mentioned it in a couple conversations. Claude stored it. And then for weeks afterward, it kept referencing that idea as if I was still working on it, even after I'd moved on entirely.

Another common issue: Claude infers things that aren't quite right. Maybe you mentioned a team member in passing and Claude decides they're your co-founder. Or you described a product feature you were considering, and Claude stores it as something you've already built. The inferences are usually in the right ballpark, but the details can be off in ways that subtly degrade your outputs.

The worst version of this is when you don't notice it's happening. Claude is using outdated or incorrect context about you, and you just get outputs that feel slightly off without understanding why.

How to Check and Fix It

Step one: go look at what Claude has stored about you. In Claude's settings, there's a memory section. Read through it. I'd bet money you'll find at least two or three things that are outdated or inaccurate.

Step two: delete the wrong stuff. You can remove individual memories. If Claude thinks you're still working on a project you abandoned three months ago, delete that memory. If it has your role wrong, delete it.

Step three — and this is the one most people skip — explicitly tell Claude what's correct. Don't just delete the wrong memory and hope Claude figures it out. Tell it directly:

"I want to update your understanding of my situation. I'm no longer working on [old project]. My current focus is [new thing]. My team now includes [updated list]. Please use this as your current context going forward."

Claude will often save this corrected information as a new memory. Now you've replaced the wrong context with right context, instead of just creating a void.

The Proactive Approach

Here's what I actually do: I don't wait for Claude to infer things about me. I tell it what to remember. At the end of a significant conversation — one where decisions were made or context shifted — I'll say something like:

"Before we wrap up, here's what I want you to remember from this conversation: We decided to pivot the pricing model from per-seat to usage-based. Ben is taking over the frontend rebuild. Our launch target moved from April to June."

This is way more reliable than hoping Claude picks up on the right details and stores them correctly. You're curating your own memory, not leaving it to inference.

Memory Isn't Magic

I think people expect Claude's memory to work like a human assistant who's been with you for years — someone who deeply understands your business, catches nuance, and updates their mental model automatically. It's not that. It's more like a notebook that Claude checks at the start of each conversation. A useful notebook, but one that needs to be maintained.

The people who get the most out of Claude's memory are the ones who treat it as a tool they actively manage, not a feature that runs in the background. Check it monthly. Correct it when it's wrong. Add important context when things change. Delete outdated stuff.

The Combination That Works Best

In practice, I use both the context document from the last lesson and Claude's built-in memory. The context document handles the big picture — who I am, how I work, current priorities. Memory handles the smaller details that accumulate over time — specific preferences Claude has picked up, recurring patterns, particular formats I like.

Together, they give Claude a pretty solid understanding of my situation without me having to re-explain everything in every conversation. But neither one works well if you just set it and forget it.

Try This

Right now, go to your Claude memory settings and read through everything it's stored about you. Delete anything outdated. Then start your next conversation by explicitly telling Claude three to five things about your current situation that you want it to remember. See how the quality of its responses changes when it's working with accurate context instead of stale inferences.

Lesson 12 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 2 — Lesson 13

Projects as Persistent Workspaces: Setting Up Per-Client or Per-Company Threads

The AI Prompting Course

If you're using Claude for anything beyond casual one-off questions, Projects are the feature that makes everything click. And I think most people either don't know they exist or aren't using them the way they should be.

Let me show you what I mean with a real example from how I run my business.

The Problem Projects Solve

At Mudita Studios, I'm juggling multiple things at once. There's Official AI — the product we're building. There's client work. There's internal operations. There's marketing and content. Each of these has its own context, its own terminology, its own set of active decisions and constraints.

Before I started using Projects, every conversation with Claude started the same way: me spending the first three or four messages re-establishing context. "Okay, so we're working on Official AI, here's the current state of the product, here's the team, here's what we decided last week..." Every single time. It was like having a new employee start fresh every morning with total amnesia.

Projects fix this by giving you persistent workspaces. Each project has its own set of pinned instructions, attached files, and conversation history. When you start a new chat inside a project, all of that context is already loaded.

How I Structure Mine

I have a separate project for each major workstream. Here's roughly how it breaks down:

Official AI — Product. This project has our product spec pinned, our current sprint goals, the tech stack documentation, and notes about architecture decisions. When I open a conversation here, Claude already knows we're using Next.js and Supabase, that Ben is handling the frontend, and that we're focused on the onboarding flow this week. I don't have to say any of that.

Mudita Studios — Operations. This has our team structure, our current client list, our pricing, and our processes. When I need to draft a proposal or think through a hiring decision, the context is already there.

Marketing and Content. This has our brand voice guide, our audience descriptions, examples of content we've published that performed well, and our content calendar. When I'm brainstorming social posts or writing ad copy, Claude is already calibrated to our tone.

You can see the pattern. Each project is a self-contained workspace where Claude has the specific context it needs for that type of work. No re-explaining. No context-setting. Just straight into the actual task.

What to Pin in a Project

The project instructions are the most important part. This is a text block that Claude reads at the start of every conversation in that project. Think of it as the briefing memo. Here's what I typically include:

  • A two-paragraph summary of the project or workstream
  • Key people involved and their roles
  • Current priorities or active decisions
  • Constraints or non-negotiables ("we're committed to this tech stack," "budget is X," "deadline is Y")
  • Communication preferences specific to this context

You can also attach files — documents, spreadsheets, previous outputs. Claude will reference these when relevant. I keep key docs attached: our product roadmap, our pricing sheet, whatever's most referenced in that workstream.

The Per-Client Setup

If you're running a services business or working with multiple clients, this is where Projects become essential. Create one project per client. Pin their brand guidelines, their preferences, their past deliverables, their specific requirements.

When a client emails you and you need to respond, you open their project, and Claude already knows their business, their tone, their history with you. The response it helps you craft is specific to that relationship, not generic.

I've seen people try to manage five different clients in one Claude conversation. It's chaos. Context bleeds between clients. Claude starts mixing up details. You end up spending more time correcting it than you save. Separate projects per client solves this completely.

Maintenance Is Key

Here's the thing about Projects — they need to be maintained like any workspace. If your project instructions still say "Q1 priority is launching the MVP" and it's now Q3 and the MVP launched months ago, Claude is working with outdated context and your outputs will reflect that.

I do a quick update of my project instructions every two weeks. It takes ten minutes across all my projects. I update priorities, remove completed items, add new context from recent decisions. It's a small investment that keeps everything running smoothly.

When One Project Isn't Enough

Sometimes a single project needs sub-contexts. For Official AI, I have the main product project, but I've also created a separate project for our investor communications and another for technical documentation. The test is simple: if the context for two types of work is different enough that Claude would benefit from a different briefing, make it a separate project.

Don't go overboard though. I've seen people create fifteen projects and then never use most of them. Start with three to five, organized around your actual daily work. Add more only when you feel the need.

Try This

Create your first project today. Pick the one workstream where you use Claude most frequently. Write a project instruction document — 200 to 400 words covering the summary, key people, current priorities, and constraints. Attach your two or three most-referenced documents. Then start your next conversation in that project and notice how different it feels when Claude already knows what you're working on.

Lesson 13 of 100

Module 2 — Lesson 14

The Brain Dump Technique: Voice Memos and Raw Notes into Structured Context

The AI Prompting Course

Some of my best work with Claude starts with the messiest possible input. I'm talking about voice memos recorded while driving, notes scribbled during a phone call, half-formed thoughts typed into my Notes app at midnight. Raw, unstructured, stream-of-consciousness brain dumps.

And then I feed them directly to Claude.

I see this all the time — people think they need to organize their thoughts before talking to AI. They think they need to present clean, structured prompts. They spend twenty minutes organizing their input so it looks "professional." That's backwards. One of the most powerful things AI can do is take your messy thinking and structure it for you.

How the Brain Dump Works

Here's my actual workflow. I'll be walking or driving or just sitting somewhere, and an idea hits me — a product feature, a strategy shift, a problem I need to solve, an email I need to send. Instead of trying to think it through linearly, I pull out my phone and just talk. Stream of consciousness. No editing, no organizing, no self-censoring.

A typical voice memo sounds something like this: "Okay so I've been thinking about the onboarding flow and I think we're overcomplicating it, like the user doesn't need to see the character sheet setup until after they've seen what the tool can do, we should flip the order, show them a wow-factor demo video first and then walk them through setup, also Ben mentioned the API latency thing and I think that's related because if the first thing they experience is a loading screen that's terrible, we need to pre-generate demo content so the first experience is instant..."

That's messy. It jumps between topics. It has tangents. No human would read that and immediately know what to do with it. But Claude can.

The Prompt That Makes It Work

I transcribe the voice memo (or just paste the raw notes) and use a prompt like this:

"Here's a raw brain dump from a voice memo I recorded. It's unstructured and jumps around. I need you to: 1) Extract the key ideas and decisions, 2) Organize them by topic, 3) Flag any action items, 4) Note anything that seems contradictory or needs clarification. Here's the dump: [paste]"

What comes back is structured, clear, and actionable. Claude separates the product decision (flip the onboarding order) from the technical concern (API latency) from the action item (pre-generate demo content). It catches the connection between the latency issue and the onboarding experience that I mentioned but didn't fully articulate.

In ten seconds of talking and thirty seconds of pasting, I've turned scattered thinking into an organized brief. That brief can then become a Slack message to my team, a ticket in our project board, or the starting point for a deeper conversation with Claude about implementation.

Why This Is Better Than Organizing First

When you try to organize your thoughts before giving them to Claude, you're doing two things at once — thinking and structuring. That's cognitively expensive. Your brain is good at generating ideas. It's less good at generating and organizing simultaneously. When you force yourself to be organized up front, you actually lose ideas. You filter things out prematurely because they don't fit neatly into the structure you're building.

The brain dump technique separates generation from organization. You generate freely, without any structure. Then you hand the raw material to Claude and let it handle the organization. You end up with more ideas, better ideas, and a cleaner structure than if you'd tried to do both at once.

Meeting Notes Are Perfect for This

After every important meeting, I do a quick brain dump of what was discussed, what was decided, and what I'm thinking about as a result. Sometimes it's notes I took during the call. Sometimes it's a voice memo right after I hang up. Either way, it goes straight to Claude with a prompt like:

"Here are my raw notes from a meeting with [person/team]. Extract the key decisions, action items with owners, open questions, and any follow-ups I need to do. Format it as a clean summary I can share with the team."

This has replaced my entire meeting follow-up process. I used to spend fifteen minutes after every call writing up notes. Now I spend two minutes dumping raw thoughts and thirty seconds reviewing Claude's structured output.

The Transcription Pipeline

If you record meetings or take voice memos regularly, set up a simple transcription pipeline. I use tools that auto-transcribe my voice recordings. The transcription goes straight into Claude. Some people use the Claude mobile app and just talk directly — that works too, especially for quick captures.

The key insight is that your voice is faster than your fingers. You can speak roughly three times faster than you can type. So using voice as your input method and Claude as your structuring engine means you're capturing more information in less time, with better organization on the output.

When to Use This

Brain dumps work best when you have a lot of unstructured thinking that needs to become structured output. Strategy sessions. Post-meeting recaps. Product planning. Brainstorming. Problem diagnosis. Anytime you have a lot of information bouncing around in your head and you need it to be clear and actionable.

They don't work as well for precise, technical tasks where the structure of your input matters. If you're asking Claude to write a specific function or follow a specific format, start organized. But for everything that starts as messy thinking — which is most knowledge work — dump first, structure second.

Try This

Next time you have a messy collection of thoughts — after a meeting, during a brainstorm, while planning your week — just dump everything into Claude without any attempt to organize it. Tell Claude to structure it for you. Compare the result to what you'd have produced if you tried to organize it yourself. I bet Claude's version is cleaner, and I bet you captured more ideas by not trying to filter while you were thinking.

Lesson 14 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 2 — Lesson 15

Pasting Prior Session Summaries as Conversation Starters

The AI Prompting Course

One of the most frustrating things about working with Claude is starting a new conversation and losing all the context from the last one. You spent thirty minutes going deep on a strategy problem, you got to a great place, and then you close the chat. Next day, you open a new conversation and it's like talking to someone with amnesia.

There's a simple technique that fixes this, and it's become one of my most-used habits: ending every important conversation by asking Claude to summarize it, and then pasting that summary at the start of the next one.

The End-of-Session Summary

Before I close out any conversation where real work happened — where decisions were made, where we got to a specific conclusion, where there's a next step — I ask Claude for a summary. The prompt is almost always some version of this:

"Before we wrap up, give me a concise summary of this conversation. Include: what we discussed, what we decided, any key outputs or frameworks we developed, and where we left off. Format it so I can paste it at the start of a future conversation to pick up where we left off."

That last part is crucial — "format it so I can paste it." This tells Claude to write the summary as context-for-Claude, not as meeting notes for a human. The output is optimized for being consumed as input in a future session. It front-loads the most important information and includes enough detail for Claude to reconstruct the working state.

What a Good Session Summary Looks Like

A good summary is usually 150 to 300 words. It includes the problem you were working on, the approach you took, the conclusions you reached, and the specific next steps. It does not include the full back-and-forth dialogue or every option that was considered and rejected. It's the state of the work, not the history of the work.

Here's a real example from a session where I was working through our pricing model:

"Session summary — Pricing Model for Official AI. We explored three pricing approaches: per-seat, usage-based, and hybrid. After analyzing the unit economics, we decided on usage-based pricing with a free tier (50 video generations/month) and two paid tiers ($29/mo for 500 generations, $99/mo for 2,500). Key reasoning: our target users are content creators who have variable output volume, and per-seat doesn't align with their usage patterns. Next steps: Kayan will validate these price points against competitor pricing. The Stripe integration needs to support metered billing. Open question: whether to offer annual discounts at launch or keep it simple with monthly-only to start."

When I paste that at the start of a new conversation, Claude immediately understands the context, the decisions made, and where to pick up. I don't have to re-derive any of it.

The Continuation Prompt

Starting the new conversation is straightforward. I paste the summary and then tell Claude what I want to work on next:

"Here's a summary from our last session: [paste summary]. I want to continue from where we left off. Specifically, I've done the competitor pricing research and found that most tools in our space charge $25-45/mo for similar volume. I think our $29 tier is right but the $99 tier might be too high. Let's revisit the tier structure."

Claude picks up immediately. No re-explaining, no re-establishing context, no starting from scratch. It's like hitting pause and play on a conversation.

Building a Summary Chain

For projects that span weeks or months, I build a chain of summaries. Each session's summary references and builds on the previous one. Over time, I have a running document that captures the evolution of a project through its key decision points.

I keep these in a simple text file or Google Doc — one per project. When I need to bring Claude up to speed on something I haven't worked on in a few weeks, I paste the most recent summary (or the last two or three if the project is complex). It's like giving Claude a compressed briefing that covers weeks of thinking in a few hundred words.

This isn't just useful for AI conversations. I've found these summary chains incredibly valuable for my own reference. They're a decision log — a record of what I decided and why, in chronological order. When I'm second-guessing a past decision, I can go back and read the reasoning.

When to Summarize vs. When to Start Fresh

Not every conversation needs a summary. If I'm asking Claude a quick question or generating something disposable — a one-off email, a quick brainstorm — I don't bother. Summaries are for work sessions where you're building toward something. Strategy, product decisions, multi-step projects, complex analysis. Anything where the thinking compounds over time.

The test is simple: will I need this context again? If yes, summarize. If not, just close the chat.

Try This

At the end of your next substantive conversation with Claude, ask for a session summary formatted for future context. Save it somewhere you'll find it. Then the next time you want to continue that work, paste the summary at the start of a new chat and notice how seamlessly you pick up where you left off. Once you build this habit, you'll never lose a thread of work again.

Lesson 15 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 2 — Lesson 16

When to Start a New Chat vs. Continue — Avoiding Context Decay Mid-Session

The AI Prompting Course

There's a thing that happens in long Claude conversations that nobody tells you about, and it will quietly ruin your outputs if you don't know to watch for it. I call it context decay.

You're twenty or thirty messages into a conversation. You've been going back and forth, refining something, building on ideas. And then you notice the responses start getting... worse. More generic. Claude starts repeating things it already said. It loses track of a constraint you mentioned earlier. It forgets a specific detail that was central to the conversation ten messages ago.

You're not imagining it. This is a real limitation, and understanding it is the difference between productive long sessions and wasted time.

How Context Windows Actually Work

Claude has a context window — a maximum amount of text it can "see" at any given time. Think of it as Claude's working memory. Everything in the current conversation — your messages, Claude's responses, any attached files — takes up space in that window.

When the conversation gets long enough that the total text exceeds the context window, older messages start getting pushed out. Claude can no longer see them. It still knows the general direction of the conversation, but it loses the specific details from early messages. That constraint you carefully established in message three? Gone. That example you gave in message five? Fuzzy at best.

This is context decay, and it's the number one reason long conversations start producing worse outputs.

The Signs to Watch For

Here's how you know context decay is happening:

Claude starts contradicting earlier conclusions. You agreed on approach A, and now it's suggesting approach B as if the earlier discussion never happened.

Claude asks for information you already provided. "Could you tell me more about your target audience?" when you described them in detail twenty minutes ago.

Responses get more generic. The specificity drops. Claude starts giving you the kind of general advice it would give anyone, instead of advice tailored to your situation.

Claude repeats itself. The same points or structures keep appearing in different responses, as if it's lost track of what it already said.

When you see any of these signs, don't keep pushing. The conversation has hit its limit. Continuing will only make it worse.

The Rule I Follow

My rule of thumb: if a conversation is going to go beyond fifteen to twenty substantial back-and-forth exchanges, I plan for a break point. Not every conversation needs to be that long. In fact, most shouldn't be.

Here's my decision framework:

Start a new chat when: you're switching to a different task or topic, even within the same project. When the conversation has accomplished what it set out to do and you're moving to the next thing. When you notice any signs of context decay. When the conversation is getting long and you're about to introduce new, complex context.

Continue the current chat when: you're actively iterating on a specific output — refining an email, debugging code, workshopping copy. When each message builds directly on the last one. When you're in the middle of a multi-step process that requires continuity.

The key insight is that more messages is not always better. A fresh conversation with a good summary pasted in is almost always better than a decayed long conversation. You're trading a few seconds of setup for dramatically better outputs.

The Strategic Break

When I'm in the middle of a complex work session and I feel context decay setting in, I do what I call a strategic break. I ask Claude for a session summary (exactly like in the last lesson), close the conversation, open a new one, paste the summary, and keep going.

This takes about sixty seconds, and it's like hitting a refresh button. Claude goes from foggy and generic back to sharp and specific. The summary carries forward everything important, and the fresh context window means Claude can give full attention to the current problem instead of trying to hold onto a sprawling conversation history.

"Give me a summary of everything we've covered and decided in this conversation. Include any specific outputs, frameworks, or conclusions. Format it for pasting into a new conversation."

Paste that into a new chat, add "continuing from the above summary," and you're back in business.

Topic Separation

The other reason to start new chats: topic separation. Even if you haven't hit context limits, mixing topics in one conversation degrades quality. If you start a conversation about pricing strategy and then pivot to writing social media copy, Claude is trying to hold both contexts simultaneously. Your social media copy will be subtly influenced by the pricing discussion, and not in a good way.

One topic per conversation. One task per conversation. One context per conversation. This is the simplest rule I can give you, and it'll improve your outputs more than any prompting trick.

Try This

Next time you're in a conversation that's been going for a while, pay attention to the quality of responses. The moment you notice any sign of decay — repetition, generic responses, lost details — do a strategic break. Summarize, start fresh, paste, continue. Notice how much sharper the responses become. Once you develop the instinct for when to break, you'll never suffer through a decayed conversation again.

Lesson 16 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 2 — Lesson 17

Teaching Claude Your Communication Register So It Stops Over-Formalizing You

The AI Prompting Course

If there's one complaint I hear more than any other from people using AI to write, it's this: "It doesn't sound like me." And they're right. By default, Claude writes like a well-educated consultant who's been trained to never offend anyone. Formal. Hedged. Full of transitional phrases and qualifiers that no real person uses in actual communication.

I dealt with this for months before I figured out how to fix it. The fix isn't a magic prompt. It's teaching Claude your register — the specific way you actually communicate in real life — and being persistent about it.

What "Register" Means

Your communication register is the combination of formality level, vocabulary, sentence structure, and personality that makes your writing sound like you. It's not just tone. It's the whole package.

My register, for example: I write in short punchy sentences mixed with longer flowing ones. I'm casual. I say "thing" and "stuff" instead of "element" and "component." I start sentences with "And" and "But." I use dashes liberally. I don't hedge — I say what I think directly. I use concrete examples from my own experience instead of abstract concepts.

Your register is different. Maybe you're more formal. Maybe you're funnier. Maybe you use industry jargon. Maybe you write in a way that's warm and encouraging rather than blunt. Whatever it is, Claude needs to learn it.

The Sample-Based Approach

The most effective way I've found to teach Claude your register is to show it examples of your actual writing. Not descriptions of how you write — samples of how you write.

"Here are three emails I've written recently that represent my natural communication style. Study the tone, sentence structure, formality level, and word choice. When you write for me going forward, match this register — not a formal or polished version of it, but this actual register. [paste emails]"

This works dramatically better than describing your style in abstract terms. Saying "I'm casual" means something different to everyone. But showing Claude five of your actual emails gives it a concrete target to match.

I keep three or four writing samples in my context document — a casual Slack message, a client email, a longer strategic write-up. Between those samples, Claude has enough data to calibrate to my voice across different contexts.

The Correction Loop

Even with samples, Claude will drift toward formality. It's baked into how these models work — they're trained on a lot of formal text, and the safety training pushes toward hedging and politeness. You have to actively correct it.

When Claude gives me something that's too formal, I don't just accept it and edit manually. I push back:

"This is too formal. I would never say 'I would like to propose that we consider.' I'd say 'I think we should.' Rewrite it the way I'd actually say it — casual, direct, no corporate language."

And then I get specific about what bothered me. Not just "make it more casual" — that's too vague. Point to the exact phrases that don't sound like you and tell Claude what you'd say instead. This is how it learns your specific register, not just a generic "casual" mode.

The Anti-Patterns to Call Out

There are certain Claude-isms that I've learned to watch for and immediately flag. These are the phrases that scream "AI wrote this":

  • "I'd be happy to help with that" — nobody talks like this in real life
  • "Great question!" — no
  • "Let's dive in" or "Let's unpack this" — overused to the point of meaninglessness
  • "It's important to note that" — filler
  • "In order to" — just say "to"
  • Starting every paragraph with a transitional phrase
  • Excessive hedging: "might," "could potentially," "it's worth considering"

I have a line in my context document that says: "Do not use any of these phrases: [list]. When you're tempted to hedge, just state your opinion directly." This single instruction eliminates probably seventy percent of the over-formalization problem.

Context-Dependent Registers

Here's the thing — you don't have one register. You have several. The way I write to my co-founder is different from how I write to a potential investor, which is different from how I write marketing copy. Claude needs to know which register to use for which context.

In my project instructions, I specify this: "For internal team messages, match my most casual register — short, direct, sometimes blunt. For client communication, match my professional-but-still-human register — clear and warm, but not corporate. For investor communication, match my most polished register — still direct, but with more structured reasoning."

This way, when I ask Claude to draft a Slack message versus an investor update, it knows which version of me to channel. Same person, different contexts, different registers. Claude can handle this nuance if you make it explicit.

The Persistence Factor

Teaching Claude your register isn't a one-time thing. You'll need to correct it repeatedly, especially in the beginning. Don't get frustrated. Each correction makes future outputs better, especially within a project where Claude accumulates understanding of your preferences. After a few weeks of consistent correction, Claude will default much closer to your actual voice.

Try This

Find five pieces of writing you've done recently — emails, messages, documents, anything. Pick ones that represent how you naturally communicate. Paste them into a conversation with Claude and say: "This is how I write. Analyze my communication style in detail — sentence length, formality, word choice, patterns, personality." Then use Claude's analysis as the style section of your context document. Next time you ask Claude to write something, include that style guide and see how much closer the output is to your actual voice.

Lesson 17 of 100

Module 2 — Lesson 18

Explicit vs. Implicit Memory: Telling Claude What to Remember vs. Letting It Infer

The AI Prompting Course

There are two ways Claude builds an understanding of who you are and what you need. One is reliable. One is unpredictable. The people who get consistently great outputs understand the difference and lean heavily on the reliable one.

Explicit memory is when you directly tell Claude something about yourself, your preferences, or your situation. "My company is called Mudita Studios." "I prefer short paragraphs." "Our tech stack is Next.js and Supabase." You stated it, Claude knows it. Clean and clear.

Implicit memory is when Claude infers something based on patterns in your conversations. You keep asking for casual rewrites, so Claude infers you prefer casual tone. You mention your co-founder Ben several times, so Claude infers Ben is important to your work. You always reject bullet points, so Claude eventually stops using them.

Here's the thing — implicit memory is convenient, but it's also where most context problems come from.

Why Implicit Memory Fails

Claude's inferences are educated guesses. Sometimes they're right. Sometimes they're close-but-not-quite. And sometimes they're flat-out wrong in ways you don't notice until the outputs start going sideways.

Let me give you a real example. I was working on a marketing strategy for one of our products, and I mentioned that our target audience was "creators." Over several conversations, Claude built up an implicit understanding that by "creators" I meant YouTube creators — people making long-form video content. But what I actually meant was social media content creators, a much broader group that includes people on TikTok, Instagram, and Twitter who might never make a YouTube video.

I didn't catch this for a while. The marketing suggestions I was getting were subtly wrong — too focused on YouTube-specific strategies, too heavy on long-form content tactics. It wasn't terrible output, so I didn't immediately spot the problem. But it was misaligned because Claude's inference was too narrow.

This kind of thing happens constantly. Claude infers your industry based on the topics you discuss, but gets the specific segment wrong. It infers your communication preference from a few samples, but over-indexes on one atypical example. It infers project priorities based on what you talk about most, not what's actually most important.

The Case for Being Explicit

The fix is simple: tell Claude things directly instead of hoping it figures them out. Every important piece of context should be stated, not inferred. This is especially true for:

Definitions and terminology. If you use a word in a specific way — "creators" meaning social media creators, "pipeline" meaning your sales process, "the platform" meaning your specific product — define it explicitly. Don't assume Claude will interpret your jargon the way you intend.

Preferences that matter. If you care about output format, tone, length, or structure, state it. "I want responses under 300 words" is explicit. Hoping Claude notices you always ask for shorter versions and eventually starts writing shorter — that's implicit, and it's unreliable.

Relationships and roles. "Ben is my product engineer, he handles the frontend. Bree manages operations. Cyrus is my business partner." State these. Don't let Claude piece together your org chart from scattered mentions across conversations.

Current state and priorities. "We just launched our MVP last week. Our top priority right now is user onboarding. Revenue target for Q2 is $50K MRR." Explicit context produces exponentially better strategic advice than context Claude has to guess at.

When Implicit Memory Is Fine

I'm not saying implicit memory is useless. For low-stakes preferences — the fact that you tend to prefer numbered lists over bullet points, or that you like examples from the tech industry — it's fine to let Claude pick up on patterns organically. These are nice-to-have calibrations, not critical context.

Implicit memory also works well for stylistic preferences that are hard to articulate. Your sentence rhythm, your sense of humor, the specific way you structure arguments — these are things Claude can pick up from exposure that would be very difficult to describe in explicit instructions.

The rule I follow: if getting it wrong would meaningfully impact the output, make it explicit. If getting it wrong would be a minor annoyance, implicit is fine.

The Hybrid Approach

In practice, the best setup is a combination. Your context document and project instructions handle the explicit layer — all the things Claude absolutely needs to know. These are stated clearly, reviewed regularly, and updated when things change.

The implicit layer builds on top of that. As Claude observes your patterns and preferences over time, it fills in the gaps. But the explicit foundation ensures that even when the implicit layer gets something wrong, the most important context is still accurate.

Think of it like managing a team member. You give them an explicit brief with the key requirements. And then over time, they learn your preferences through observation. But if they only had observation and no brief, they'd make a lot more mistakes early on.

The Audit Habit

Every few weeks, I do a quick audit. I look at Claude's stored memories and ask myself: what here is something I explicitly told it versus something it inferred? For the inferred items, is the inference correct? If not, I either correct it or delete it and replace it with an explicit statement.

This takes five minutes. It prevents the gradual drift that happens when implicit memory goes unchecked — where Claude's model of you slowly diverges from reality, one small inference at a time.

Try This

Make a list of the five most important things Claude needs to know about your work. Your company, your role, your current project, your audience, your preferences. Now check: are these things explicitly stated in your context document or project instructions? Or are you relying on Claude to have inferred them? If any of the five are implicit, make them explicit today. State them directly. Watch how the specificity of your outputs improves.

Lesson 18 of 100

Module 2 — Lesson 19

Building an "AI Brain" for Recurring Operations (Team Structure, Preferences, Relationships)

The AI Prompting Course

Once you start using Claude seriously for business operations, you'll hit a point where the one-off context approach isn't enough. You're not just asking occasional questions anymore — you're running a significant portion of your daily work through AI. And at that scale, you need something more systematic.

I call it building your AI brain. It's a structured collection of documents that gives Claude a deep, comprehensive understanding of your operation — not just who you are, but how your entire business works.

Beyond the Context Document

The personal context document we talked about in Lesson 11 is your starting point. But when you're using Claude to help manage a team, communicate with clients, make strategic decisions, and handle day-to-day operations, you need more depth.

Here's what my AI brain looks like. It's a set of documents, each covering a different domain of my business. They live in a folder, and relevant ones get pinned to different Claude projects depending on the context.

Team Document. This is a profile of every person I work with regularly. Not just names and titles — their communication preferences, their strengths, their working style, what they care about, how to approach them. For Ben, I have: "Product engineer, handles frontend and most technical implementation. UCLA student. Very technical, prefers detailed specs over vague direction. Communicates primarily via Slack. Processes things by building — give him context and let him prototype rather than asking him to write a plan."

Why does Claude need to know this? Because when I ask it to help me draft a message to Ben, or create a task breakdown for him, or think about how to delegate a project, Claude can tailor its suggestions to how Ben actually works. Generic management advice is useless. Advice calibrated to the specific person is gold.

Client Relationship Map. For each active client: who they are, what we do for them, the key contacts and their personalities, the history of the relationship, any sensitivities or preferences. When I need to write a client email or prepare for a call, Claude already knows the relationship dynamics.

Decision Log. A running list of key decisions we've made and why. "Decided to use usage-based pricing because our target users have variable output volume" or "Chose Next.js over Remix because Ben has more experience with it and speed of development matters more than framework ideology right now." This prevents Claude from suggesting things we've already considered and rejected, and helps it understand the reasoning behind our current approach.

Preferences and Standards. How we handle common recurring tasks. Our email response time expectations. Our meeting structure. Our naming conventions. Our quality bar for different types of deliverables. The stuff that would normally live in someone's head after working at a company for six months.

How This Changes Daily Operations

Let me show you what I mean with a specific example. Last week I needed to delegate a complex feature to Ben. Without the AI brain, I'd ask Claude something like "help me write a task brief for a developer." Generic prompt, generic output.

With the AI brain loaded, I said:

"I need to hand off the video stitching feature to Ben. Here's what I'm thinking at a high level — we need to take individually generated 2-3 second clips and combine them into a seamless final video using FFmpeg. Write a task brief that works for how Ben operates."

Because Claude knows Ben prefers detailed specs, builds by prototyping, and communicates via Slack, the brief it generated was specific, technical, formatted for Slack, and structured as an implementation spec rather than a high-level overview. It even anticipated the questions Ben would likely ask based on his profile.

That's the difference the AI brain makes. Claude stops being a generic assistant and starts operating like someone who actually knows your team and your business.

Building It Incrementally

You don't need to build this all at once. Start with the team document — even if your team is just you and one other person. Add the client relationship map when you notice you're re-explaining client context in every conversation. Add the decision log when you find Claude suggesting things you've already ruled out.

Each document should be living — updated whenever something meaningful changes. A new team member joins? Add their profile. A client relationship shifts? Update the map. A major decision gets made? Log it.

The investment is maybe thirty minutes upfront for each document and five minutes every week or two to keep them current. The return is that every interaction with Claude is calibrated to your actual reality instead of generic defaults.

The Compound Effect

What makes the AI brain so powerful is the compound effect. Each document makes every other document more useful. The team profiles make the delegation advice better. The decision log makes the strategic recommendations better. The client map makes the communication drafts better. Together, they create a version of Claude that genuinely understands your operation.

Six months from now, your AI brain will be the most valuable asset in your workflow. Not because of any single document, but because the accumulated context makes Claude exponentially more useful over time.

Try This

Start with the team document. Write a one-paragraph profile for every person you work with regularly — three to five people is enough to start. Include their role, their communication style, their working preferences, and one thing that makes working with them unique. Pin it to your main Claude project. Then the next time you need to draft a message to any of them, notice how much more tailored and useful Claude's output is.

Lesson 19 of 100

Module 2 — Lesson 20

Cross-Tool Memory: Combining Gmail + Calendar + Drive for a Unified Operating Brain

The AI Prompting Course

Everything we've covered in this module so far has been about giving Claude context within Claude itself — context documents, projects, summaries, memory management. But the real unlock comes when you connect Claude to the tools where your actual work lives. Your email. Your calendar. Your documents. Your project management system.

This is where AI stops being a tool you use and starts becoming an operating system for how you work.

The Problem with Isolated Context

Right now, your information is scattered. The email thread about a client project is in Gmail. The meeting where you discussed the timeline is on your calendar. The project brief is in Google Docs. The task list is in your project management tool. Your notes from the last call are in a random Notes app on your phone.

When you ask Claude for help with that project, you have to manually gather context from all these places. Open the email, copy the relevant parts. Find the meeting notes, paste those in. Pull up the doc, reference the key sections. It works, but it's slow, and you inevitably miss important context because you forgot which thread had that one key decision.

Cross-tool memory solves this. When Claude can access your Gmail, your Calendar, and your Drive directly, it can pull context from across your entire workflow automatically.

What This Looks Like in Practice

Let me walk you through a real scenario. I have a call with a potential client tomorrow. In the past, my prep process would be: search Gmail for our previous correspondence, check my calendar for when we last met and what the notes said, look up their company, review any proposals or documents I've sent them, and then try to synthesize all of that into a mental model of where the relationship stands.

Now it looks like this:

"I have a call with Sarah Chen from Meridian Labs tomorrow at 2pm. Pull up our recent email threads, check our last meeting, and find any docs I've shared with them. Give me a prep brief: where the relationship stands, what we last discussed, any open items, and what I should focus on in tomorrow's call."

Claude searches my Gmail for threads with Sarah, finds the calendar event with any attached notes, locates the proposal I sent them in Drive, and synthesizes all of that into a concise prep brief. Two minutes. Better prep than I'd do manually in twenty.

Setting Up the Connections

The specific setup depends on what tools you're using. Claude can connect to Google Workspace through integrations — Gmail, Calendar, Drive. The key is giving it access to these tools through official MCP (Model Context Protocol) connections or similar integrations.

Here's my practical advice: start with the three tools that contain the most context about your work. For most people, that's email, calendar, and documents. Get those connected first. You can add project management, Slack, and other tools later.

The setup takes a bit of time upfront — connecting accounts, granting permissions, testing that things work. But it's a one-time investment that fundamentally changes how you interact with AI.

The Daily Operating Rhythm

Once you have cross-tool memory set up, it changes your daily rhythm. Here's what mine looks like:

Morning briefing. I ask Claude to pull my calendar for the day, check for any urgent emails that came in overnight, and flag anything that needs my attention before my first meeting. This takes sixty seconds and replaces ten minutes of manually checking email and calendar.

Meeting prep. Before any external call, I ask Claude to compile a relationship brief using email history, past meetings, and shared documents. I walk into every call fully briefed without spending time on manual research.

Follow-up drafting. After calls, I do my brain dump (Lesson 14), and Claude drafts follow-up emails that reference specific things from our email history and previous meetings. The emails feel personal and informed because they are — they're grounded in the actual relationship history.

End-of-day wrap. Claude reviews what I accomplished against what was on my calendar, flags anything I said I'd do but haven't done, and helps me set priorities for tomorrow.

This isn't hypothetical. This is how I actually operate. And it only works because Claude has access to the full picture — not just what I remember to paste into a conversation, but the actual data from my actual tools.

Privacy and Judgment Calls

A word on privacy, because it matters. When you give Claude access to your email and calendar, you're trusting it with sensitive information. Be thoughtful about this. Understand what data is being accessed and how it's stored. Use official integrations rather than janky workarounds. And be aware of what conversations involve confidential information that maybe shouldn't be flowing through any AI system.

I'm comfortable with the tradeoff because the productivity gains are enormous and I trust the platforms I'm using. But you should make that decision for yourself based on your own situation and the sensitivity of your data.

The Unified Brain Effect

Here's the thing — the value of each connected tool is greater than the sum of its parts. Email alone is useful. Calendar alone is useful. But email plus calendar means Claude can connect "Sarah emailed about rescheduling" with "Sarah's meeting is on Thursday" and proactively suggest an updated agenda. Drive plus email means Claude can reference the exact proposal you sent when drafting a follow-up. The connections between tools create context that no single tool could provide.

This is what I mean by a unified operating brain. It's not just Claude knowing about your business in the abstract. It's Claude having access to the living, breathing reality of your work — the conversations, the meetings, the documents, the decisions — all in one place, all connected.

Try This

Pick one integration to set up this week. If you're on Google Workspace, connect Gmail first — it's usually the richest source of relationship and project context. Once it's connected, try the meeting prep workflow: before your next external call, ask Claude to search your email history with that person and give you a relationship brief. Compare the quality of that brief to what you'd normally prepare on your own. That difference is the beginning of what cross-tool memory makes possible.
