Module 3 — Lesson 21

Voice Memo to Polished Professional Message: The Fastest Daily Workflow

The AI Prompting Course

I send probably thirty messages a day that need to sound professional. Emails to partners, Slack messages to contractors, follow-ups with potential clients. And I'd say at least half of them start the same way — me talking into my phone while walking somewhere, rambling through what I actually want to say.

Here's the thing. When you talk out loud, you say everything that matters. You just say it in the wrong order, with filler words, with half-finished thoughts and tangents. But the substance is there. The intent is there. The real you is there, in a way that it often isn't when you sit down and try to "write professionally."

So I started doing something that changed my daily workflow completely. I take that voice memo transcription — the raw, messy, unfiltered version — and I hand it to Claude with one simple instruction.

The Workflow

Step one: I record a voice memo on my phone. I just talk through what I want to say. No editing, no thinking about structure. I literally say it the way I'd say it to a friend sitting across from me. "Hey so I need to tell Mark that the timeline shifted because the dev team found some issues with the API integration, and we're probably looking at two extra weeks but it's actually a good thing because it means we can ship the dashboard update at the same time, and I don't want him to freak out about the delay."

Step two: I get the transcription. Most phones do this automatically now. Apple's transcription, Google's, whatever — it doesn't need to be perfect.

Step three: I paste it into Claude with a prompt like this:

"Here's a voice memo transcription of what I want to say to Mark, who's a client. Clean this up into a professional but warm email. Keep my actual points and tone — don't make it corporate. Two to three short paragraphs max."

And then I paste the raw transcription.

What comes back is almost always something I can send within thirty seconds. Maybe I tweak a word or two. But the core message, the tone, the important details — they're all there because they came from me actually talking through the situation.

Why This Beats Writing From Scratch

I see this all the time — people sit down to write an important email and freeze. They overthink the opening line. They worry about tone. They write three drafts and delete all of them. Twenty minutes gone for a four-sentence email.

The voice memo approach bypasses all of that. When you talk, you don't overthink. You don't get writer's block talking to a friend. You just say what you mean. The AI's job is just to clean up the packaging — not to generate the substance.

This is a crucial distinction. You're not asking Claude to write a message for you. You're asking it to translate your actual thoughts from spoken form to written form. The difference in output quality is massive because the input already contains everything that matters — your real reasoning, your real tone, your actual relationship context.

Tuning the Output

The key variable is how much you want Claude to change. I use different instructions depending on the situation:

For casual Slack messages:

"Clean this up but keep it casual. This is going in Slack, not an email. Short and direct."

For emails to someone important:

"Make this professional but still warm. I have a good relationship with this person — don't make it stiff. Paragraph form, no bullet points."

For something sensitive:

"This is a tricky situation. I need to deliver some bad news (timeline delay) but frame it constructively. Clean up my rambling but make sure the positive spin I mentioned comes through clearly."

The more you tell Claude about the context — who the person is, what channel it's going in, what the relationship is like — the better the translation works.

The Speed Factor

Let me put some real numbers on this. Before I started doing this, writing a thoughtful email to a client took me ten to fifteen minutes. I'd draft, re-read, edit, second-guess the tone, edit again. Now the whole process takes under two minutes. Voice memo: thirty seconds. Paste and prompt: twenty seconds. Review and send: thirty seconds.

Over the course of a day, that's easily an hour saved. Over a week, it's a full working afternoon. And the outputs are actually better because they sound like me — not like someone trying to sound professional.

I run Mudita Studios this way now. Most of my client communication starts as a voice memo. The messages are more natural, more specific, and more human than anything I'd write from scratch while staring at a blank text box.

Try This Today

Next time you need to send a professional message — any message — try the voice memo route first. Just talk through what you want to say. Don't worry about how it sounds. Get the transcription, paste it into Claude, and tell it the context: who you're writing to, what channel, what tone. See how close the output is to something you'd actually send.

I think you'll be surprised. Not by how smart the AI is, but by how much better your own thinking is when you let yourself just talk instead of trying to write.

Lesson 21 of 100

Module 3 — Lesson 22

The Split-Option Technique: One Brief, Three Strategic Message Variants

The AI Prompting Course

There are certain messages where the stakes are high enough that you don't just want one draft. You want options. Different angles, different tones, different strategies for the same situation. And you want to pick the one that feels right instead of trying to engineer the perfect version on the first try.

I started doing this when I was writing outreach emails for Official AI. We were reaching out to potential design partners — people we'd never met — and the difference between a good cold email and a great one was literally the difference between getting a response and getting ignored. I needed to explore different approaches fast.

So I developed what I call the split-option technique. One brief, three variants. It's become one of my most-used workflows.

How It Works

Instead of asking Claude to write one message, you give it a thorough brief and then ask for three distinct versions that each take a different strategic approach.

Here's an actual prompt structure I use:

"I need to write an email to [person, context, relationship]. The situation is [what's happening, what I need from them, any relevant background]. Here are the key points I want to hit: [list them]. Give me three versions:

Version A: Direct and concise — get to the point fast, minimal preamble
Version B: Relationship-first — lead with something personal or warm before getting to the ask
Version C: Value-first — lead with what's in it for them before making the request

Keep all three in my natural voice — casual, not corporate. Each should be under 150 words."

What comes back are three genuinely different approaches to the same message. Not three rewordings of the same thing — three different strategies.

Why Three Is the Magic Number

Two options feels like a binary choice. Four or five gets overwhelming. Three is the sweet spot. You can read all three in under a minute, and almost always one of them immediately feels right. Sometimes I'll grab the opening from Version B and the closing from Version A. Sometimes Version C nails it and I just send it.

The point is that you're not trying to imagine the best approach in your head before writing. You're seeing concrete examples of different approaches and picking the one that matches the vibe you want. It's so much faster than iterating on a single draft.

Real Example

Last month I needed to follow up with a contractor who'd missed a deadline. Not a huge deal, but I needed the work done and I didn't want to be passive-aggressive about it. Here's roughly what I sent Claude:

"I need to message a freelance developer who was supposed to deliver a feature by Friday. It's now Tuesday and I haven't heard from him. He's generally reliable — this is the first time this has happened. I don't want to burn the relationship but I need the work. We communicate on Slack. Give me three versions: one that's super direct, one that gives him an easy out to explain, and one that reframes the deadline around an upcoming milestone to create urgency without blame."

The three versions were completely different in tone and approach. I ended up going with a hybrid — the easy-out framing from Version B with the milestone urgency from Version C. Sent it in under a minute. Got a reply within the hour with an apology and a revised timeline.

When to Use This

I don't do this for every message. For routine stuff, one-and-done is fine. But I pull out the split-option technique whenever:

  • The relationship matters and I'm not sure about the right tone
  • There's something at stake — a deal, a partnership, a sensitive situation
  • I'm second-guessing myself about how to approach it
  • I need to say something uncomfortable and want to find the right angle

It's also incredible for sales emails, investor outreach, and any situation where you're writing to someone who gets a lot of messages and you need yours to stand out.

The Key Detail People Miss

The brief matters more than anything. If you just say "write three versions of an email," you'll get three versions that are basically the same thing with slightly different word choices. The variants need to be strategically different, and that means you need to define what makes each one distinct.

Don't just say "three tones." Say what each tone is and why someone might choose it. "Direct and concise" is a strategy. "Warm and personal" is a different strategy. "Urgency-driven" is another. Name the strategies, and the variants will actually be useful.

Try This

Think of a message you've been putting off writing. Something where you're not quite sure of the right approach. Write a brief that covers the situation, the key points, and the relationship. Then ask for three variants, each with a named strategy. Read all three. I bet you'll know which one to send within thirty seconds — and it'll be better than anything you would've agonized over for twenty minutes.

Lesson 22 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 3 — Lesson 23

Drafting in Someone Else's Voice While Preserving Your Own Register Underneath

The AI Prompting Course

This one comes up more than you'd think. You need to write something that sounds like it's coming from someone else — your CEO, your co-founder, a client — but you're the one drafting it. And the tricky part isn't mimicking their voice. It's making sure the message still carries the intent and strategic thinking that you're bringing to the table.

I do this constantly. When Ben and I are working on Official AI, sometimes I'm drafting messages that'll go out under his name, or vice versa. I've ghostwritten investor updates, partner emails, even internal Slack messages for other people. The secret is that you're not trying to impersonate someone. You're trying to write in their register while thinking with your brain.

The Difference Between Voice and Register

Let me explain what I mean by register, because it matters. Voice is the deep stuff — someone's personality, their worldview, the way they think. Register is the surface layer — how formal they are, the words they tend to use, their sentence length, whether they use humor or stay serious.

When you're ghostwriting for someone, you mostly need to match their register. You don't need to think like them. You need to sound like them while delivering the message you've strategically designed.

Here's how I prompt this:

"I'm drafting an email that will be sent from my co-founder Ben. Here's his communication style: he's direct, uses shorter sentences than I do, tends to lead with data or results before getting into the narrative, rarely uses exclamation points, and signs off with just his first name. He's technical but explains things simply.

The message needs to cover [situation and key points]. I want to make sure it [strategic objective]. Draft it in his voice."

Giving Claude Samples

The single most effective thing you can do is give Claude actual examples of how the person writes. Copy-paste two or three emails or messages they've sent previously and say:

"Here are three emails Ben has written recently. Match his communication style — sentence length, level of formality, how he structures his points, his typical greeting and sign-off. Don't copy his exact phrases, but match the feel."

This works remarkably well. Claude picks up on patterns you might not even consciously notice — the way someone always acknowledges a problem before proposing a solution, or how they tend to end emails with a question, or their habit of using em dashes instead of commas.

The Strategic Layer

Here's where it gets interesting. The reason you're drafting the message — not the person whose name it's going under — is because you have a strategic perspective on what it should accomplish. Maybe you're thinking about how this email fits into a larger negotiation. Maybe you know something about the recipient that the sender hasn't considered.

Don't lose that. Your prompt should include both the voice instructions and the strategic intent:

"Draft this in Ben's voice [style details], but make sure it accomplishes the following: opens the door for a pricing conversation without committing to a number, positions our timeline delay as intentional quality focus, and ends with a soft close that moves toward a call next week."

You're the strategist. Claude is the translator. The person whose name is on the email is the character. All three roles are distinct, and keeping them distinct is what makes the output work.

Common Mistakes

Mistake one: over-describing their voice. If you write a paragraph about someone's communication style, Claude tends to overcorrect and produce something that feels like a parody. Keep the style notes to three or four concrete observations, or just give samples and let Claude figure it out.

Mistake two: forgetting your own strategic objectives. You get so focused on matching the voice that the message doesn't actually accomplish what you need it to. Always include what the message should do, not just what it should sound like.

Mistake three: not running it by the person. This is a practical note, not a prompting note. Even when the draft is great, let the person whose name is on it review and tweak. People can always tell when something is almost-but-not-quite their voice. A quick review turns a 90% draft into a 100% one.

Where This Becomes a Superpower

Once you get good at this, you can draft for multiple people quickly. I've drafted three different emails in one sitting — one from Ben's perspective, one from mine, one from our advisor — all related to the same partnership opportunity, each hitting a different angle. That's a coordinated outreach strategy that would've taken hours if each person wrote their own, done in twenty minutes.

If you're a founder, a chief of staff, an executive assistant, or anyone who drafts communications on behalf of others, this technique alone will transform your workflow. Give Claude the voice samples, give it the strategy, and let it build the bridge between the two.

Lesson 23 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 3 — Lesson 24

Cleaning Up Stream-of-Consciousness Into Boardroom-Ready Language

The AI Prompting Course

Some of my best thinking happens when I'm not trying to think clearly. I'll be in the shower, or on a walk, or just typing fast in a notes app, and a whole argument will pour out — messy, non-linear, full of "wait actually" and "oh and another thing." It's not polished. But the ideas are usually sharper than anything I'd produce sitting down to Write A Document.

The problem is that nobody else can read it. A stream-of-consciousness brain dump is fantastic input for me. It's terrible output for anyone else. Definitely not something I'm putting in front of a board, a client, or an investor.

This is one of the highest-value things AI does for me. It takes my messy thinking and restructures it without losing the ideas.

The Prompt Pattern

Here's a version of what I do regularly. I'll have a giant block of notes — sometimes voice transcription, sometimes just fast typing — and I'll drop it into Claude with something like:

"Here's a brain dump of my thoughts on [topic]. It's messy and non-linear. I need you to extract the key arguments, organize them into a logical structure, and rewrite it as a clear, concise briefing document. Preserve my actual positions and reasoning — don't soften anything or add caveats I didn't include. Professional tone but not stiff. This is going to [audience]."

The key phrase in there is "preserve my actual positions and reasoning." Because here's what happens if you don't say that: Claude will clean things up so much that it sands off all the edges. Your strong opinion becomes a "consideration." Your definitive stance becomes "one perspective among many." Your urgency becomes a polite suggestion.

You have to tell it to keep the teeth in your writing.

The Before and After

Let me show you what I mean. Here's something close to a real brain dump I had about a product decision:

"ok so the dashboard thing — I think we're overcomplicating this. everyone keeps adding features and nobody is asking whether users actually want any of it. I looked at the analytics and like 80% of users use three features. three. everything else is noise. we should strip it down. I know the design team spent weeks on the expanded view but honestly it's not what people need right now. we need to ship the simple version, get it in front of people, and then decide what to add based on what they actually ask for. not what we think they want. we keep building for hypothetical users instead of real ones."

That's raw. It's also clear thinking buried under messy delivery. Here's what Claude turns it into when I tell it to make it boardroom-ready:

"Based on our usage analytics, approximately 80% of users regularly engage with just three core features. The remaining feature set sees minimal adoption. My recommendation is to ship a simplified dashboard focused on those three high-usage features, then iterate based on actual user requests rather than internal assumptions. This means pausing the expanded view that's currently in design. I recognize the team has invested significant time there, but the data suggests we're building for hypothetical use cases rather than demonstrated need."

Same argument. Same positions. Same conclusion. But structured, clear, and ready for a meeting.

Controlling the Level of Polish

Not everything needs to be boardroom-level. Sometimes I just need to clean up my notes enough to share with my co-founder. Sometimes I need something that could go in a board deck. The polish level should match the audience:

For internal team sharing:

"Clean this up enough that someone else can follow my reasoning. Keep it casual — this is for my team, not an external audience."

For client presentations:

"Restructure this into a clear recommendation document. Professional but not bureaucratic. This is going to a client who values directness."

For board or investor communications:

"Turn this into a concise executive summary. Data-forward, clear conclusions, no hedging. Under 300 words."

The Structure Problem

The biggest thing Claude does when cleaning up stream-of-consciousness is find the structure you didn't know was there. When I brain dump, my thoughts come out in the order I think them — which is almost never the order other people need to hear them. I might start with a conclusion, jump back to supporting evidence, mention a tangent, return to the main point, and end with context that should've been at the beginning.

Claude reorganizes this automatically. Context first, then the core argument, then supporting evidence, then the recommendation. It finds the structure because it can see all the pieces at once in a way that's hard to do when you're mid-ramble.

If you want to control the structure, specify it:

"Organize this as: the problem, what the data shows, my recommendation, and what I need from the team."

Try This

Open your notes app right now. Find the messiest, most stream-of-consciousness note you have — something with real ideas buried in chaotic delivery. Paste it into Claude and tell it who the audience is and what level of polish you need. Compare the input to the output. The ideas are the same. The delivery is transformed. That gap between your raw thinking and a polished document? AI closes it in seconds. Stop letting good ideas die in messy notes.

Module 3 — Lesson 25

Writing Sensitive Internal Communications With the Right Framing

The AI Prompting Course

There are emails you write where getting the tone wrong by five percent can create a problem that takes weeks to fix. Layoff announcements. Policy changes. Telling a team their project got cut. Addressing a conflict. Pushing back on a decision from leadership. These are the communications that keep people up at night, not because they don't know what to say, but because they're terrified of how it'll land.

I've been on both sides of this. I've received internal messages that made me furious — not because of the news, but because of how it was framed. And I've sent messages that I thought were fine but landed completely wrong because I didn't think carefully enough about the reader's emotional state.

AI is incredibly useful here. Not to write the message for you — this is exactly the kind of thing where you need to own every word — but to help you see the message from the reader's perspective before you send it.

The Approach

When I'm writing something sensitive, I don't start by asking Claude to draft it. I start by thinking through what I want to say, writing a rough version myself, and then using Claude as a framing advisor. Here's the prompt pattern:

"I need to send this message to my team. Here's the situation: [explain the context, what happened, and what the decision is]. Here's my draft: [paste your draft]. Read this from the perspective of someone on the receiving end who might feel [anxious / frustrated / blindsided / undervalued]. Tell me: where might this land wrong? What's missing? Is there anything that could be misread? Then give me a revised version that addresses those concerns while keeping my core message and not sugar-coating the situation."

The critical phrase is "not sugar-coating." Because the instinct with sensitive communications — especially when AI is helping — is to soften everything into meaninglessness. People can smell that. When a serious situation gets communicated in vague, overly positive language, it doesn't reassure people. It insults them. They know something's wrong, and now they also know you're not being straight with them.

The Framing Framework

Through a lot of trial and error, I've landed on a framework for sensitive internal messages that I always include in my prompt. I tell Claude the message needs to hit four things:

Acknowledge the reality. Don't pretend this isn't a big deal if it is. If you're cutting a project, say "I know this is frustrating" — not "we're excited about this new direction."

Explain the reasoning. People can handle bad news. What they can't handle is bad news with no explanation. Even if the reasoning is "we ran out of money," that's better than nothing.

Be specific about what changes and what doesn't. Ambiguity in sensitive communications creates anxiety spirals. If you're restructuring the team, say exactly who's affected and who isn't. If you're changing a policy, say exactly what's different.

Give people a clear next step. "If you have questions, here's what to do." "We'll discuss this in our all-hands on Thursday." Something that prevents the message from just landing in someone's inbox like a grenade with no follow-up.

A Real-ish Example

I once had to tell a freelance team that we were cutting their hours significantly because a client reduced their budget. My first draft was something like: "Hey team, wanted to let you know we'll be scaling back hours starting next month due to some budget adjustments on the client side. Let me know if you have questions."

I ran it through Claude with the framing prompt. Claude flagged several things: "scaling back hours" was vague and would create immediate anxiety about job loss. "Budget adjustments" sounded like I was hiding something. And "let me know if you have questions" put the burden on them to chase information.

The revised version was direct about the numbers (hours going from 30/week to 15/week), explained why (client's Series A didn't close, they needed to cut spend), clarified what it meant for each person (nobody's getting let go, but weekly commitments are halving for at least two months), and ended with a specific next step (one-on-ones scheduled for the following day to discuss individual situations).

Same news. Completely different impact.

Using Claude as a Pre-Flight Check

Even when I don't need help with the writing itself, I use Claude as a sensitivity reader. I'll paste a finished draft and ask:

"Read this internal message and flag anything that could be misinterpreted, land poorly, or create unnecessary anxiety. Be specific about which phrases are problematic and why."

This is like having a thoughtful colleague read your email before you send it — except you can do it at midnight when no one's around and the message needs to go out first thing in the morning.

What You Should Never Outsource

I want to be clear about something. AI can help you frame sensitive communications. It can help you find blind spots and soften sharp edges. But the decision about what to say, and the accountability for saying it, has to stay with you. Don't use AI to distance yourself from hard messages. Don't send something that reads like it was written by a committee when it should read like it came from a person who cares.

The best sensitive communications feel like they were written by a human being who wrestled with the situation and chose their words carefully. Use Claude to help you do that wrestling faster. Don't use it to avoid the wrestling altogether.

Lesson 25 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 3 — Lesson 26

The "Write It in Chat, No Formatting" Override for Fast Prose

The AI Prompting Course

If there's one thing that drives me insane about AI-generated writing, it's the default formatting. You ask for a paragraph and you get headers, bullet points, bold text, numbered lists — like the AI is terrified of just writing a normal sentence without some kind of visual scaffolding around it.

I get why it happens. AI models are trained on a ton of web content, documentation, and structured text. So their default instinct is to organize everything into formatted chunks. For technical docs or how-to guides, that's fine. For an email, a message, a quick write-up, or basically any normal human communication, it's awful.

I see this all the time. Someone asks Claude for help with an email and gets back something with a subject line in bold, bullet-pointed key messages, and a formatted signature block. They wanted an email. They got a template.

The Override

There's a simple instruction that fixes this instantly. I use some variation of it almost every time I want natural prose:

"Write this as plain text — no headers, no bullets, no bold, no formatting. Just write it like you're typing in a chat window. Flowing paragraphs, natural sentences."

That's it. Those two sentences completely change the output. Instead of a structured document, you get something that reads like a person actually wrote it. Which is what you wanted.

Let me show you what I mean with a concrete example. Say I ask Claude to help me write a project update for a client. Without the override, I might get something like:

Project Update — Week of March 10

Completed:
- Dashboard redesign (Phase 1)
- API integration testing

In Progress:
- User authentication flow
- Performance optimization

Blockers:
- Waiting on client feedback for design approval

That's fine for a project management tool. It's terrible for an email to a client you have a real relationship with. With the no-formatting override, here's what you get instead:

Quick update on where things stand. We wrapped up the first phase of the dashboard redesign and finished testing the API integration — both are looking solid. Right now we're working through the authentication flow and doing some performance tuning. The one thing we're waiting on is your feedback on the latest design round — once we have that, we can move into the final build phase. Happy to jump on a call if it's easier to walk through the designs together.

Same information. But one reads like a human being and the other reads like a Jira ticket.

When to Use This

I use the no-formatting override for:

  • Any email or message that goes to a real person
  • Long-form writing — blog posts, newsletters, anything narrative
  • Social media captions and posts
  • Personal notes and messages
  • Any situation where I'll paste the output directly into a text field that doesn't render markdown

That last one is important. A lot of people paste Claude's output into Slack, an email client, or a Google Doc, and all the markdown formatting shows up as literal asterisks and hash marks. Looks terrible. The no-formatting override prevents this entirely.

Variations of the Override

Depending on what I'm writing, I'll tweak the instruction:

For conversational writing:

"Write this like a text message to a friend. No formatting at all. Short sentences, casual tone."

For professional but natural writing:

"Write this as flowing prose. Paragraph breaks are fine, but no headers, no bullets, no bold text. It should read like a well-written email, not a document."

For long-form content:

"Write this as continuous prose. You can use paragraph breaks for readability but no structural formatting — no headers, no lists, no bold. Think essay style, not blog post style."

Why This Matters More Than You Think

Here's the thing. Formatting is a signal. When someone reads a message full of bullet points and headers, their brain categorizes it as "produced content" — a report, a document, something generated. When they read flowing prose, their brain categorizes it as "someone talking to me." The formatting alone affects whether your message feels personal or institutional.

If you're using AI to help with communications and you want those communications to feel human, turning off the default formatting is one of the simplest, highest-impact things you can do. It takes five extra words in your prompt and it transforms the output from "clearly AI-generated" to "someone sat down and wrote this."

Make It a Habit

For the next week, add some version of "no formatting, just natural prose" to every prompt where you're asking for written communication. Watch how different the outputs feel. Once you see the difference, you'll never go back to letting AI default to its bullet-point-and-headers instinct.

Lesson 26 of 100

Module 3 — Lesson 27

Making AI-Assisted Writing Sound Human — Removing the Tells

The AI Prompting Course

You know that feeling when you're reading something and you just know it was written by AI? Before you even think about why, something in your brain flags it. The rhythm is too even. The vocabulary is slightly off. Every paragraph has the same structure. It's like hearing a cover band play a song technically perfectly but with zero soul.

I've spent a lot of time studying what exactly creates that "AI smell" in writing. Not because I want to fool anyone into thinking AI didn't help me — I'm open about using it. But because I want the output to actually sound like me, and the default AI voice sounds like nobody.

Here are the specific tells, and how to eliminate each one.

Tell #1: The Adverb Problem

AI loves adverbs. "Effectively communicate." "Significantly improve." "Seamlessly integrate." "Truly understand." Real people use adverbs sometimes. AI uses them constantly. They're filler. They make every sentence sound like a press release.

The fix is simple. Add this to your prompt:

"Avoid adverbs. Use strong verbs instead of weak verb + adverb combinations. 'Sprinted' not 'ran quickly.' 'Demolished' not 'effectively destroyed.'"

Tell #2: The Thesaurus Voice

AI reaches for fancy words when simple ones work better. "Leverage" instead of "use." "Facilitate" instead of "help." "Utilize" instead of... just "use" again. It's like it's trying to hit a word count on a college essay.

I see this all the time and it's probably the biggest single tell. Nobody says "I want to leverage our synergies" in real life. They say "I think we should work together on this." The fix:

"Use simple, direct language. If there's a common word and a fancy word for the same thing, use the common one. Write at an 8th grade reading level — not because the reader isn't smart, but because clear writing uses plain words."

Tell #3: The Identical Paragraph Shape

Look at AI-generated long-form content and you'll notice every paragraph is roughly the same length. Four to five sentences, each with similar structure. Claim, elaboration, example, transition. Claim, elaboration, example, transition. Over and over.

Real writing has rhythm. Short paragraphs for emphasis. Longer ones when you're building an argument. One-sentence paragraphs when you want something to land hard.

Like this.

Tell Claude to vary paragraph length. Tell it that some paragraphs should be one sentence and others should be six. Tell it to mix short punchy sentences with longer flowing ones. This alone makes an enormous difference.

Tell #4: The Absence of Imperfection

Real writing has personality, and personality shows up in the small imperfections. Starting a sentence with "And." Using a fragment. Saying "look" or "honestly" or "okay so." AI defaults to grammatically perfect prose that follows every rule — which, ironically, makes it feel less human.

I handle this by giving Claude explicit permission to break rules:

"Write the way a real person talks. You can start sentences with 'And' or 'But.' You can use fragments for emphasis. You can be slightly informal. The goal is to sound like a specific human being, not like a textbook."

Tell #5: The Hedge Parade

"It's important to note that..." "It's worth mentioning that..." "One might consider..." "It could be argued that..." These hedging phrases are AI's security blanket. It uses them to avoid stating anything directly, and they pile up fast.

Real people have opinions. They state them. When I write, I say "this doesn't work" — not "it could be argued that this approach may not be optimal in certain contexts." The fix:

"State things directly. No hedging, no qualifying phrases, no 'it's important to note.' If you have a point, make it. Don't wrap it in three layers of caveats."

Tell #6: The Emotional Summary

AI loves to end sections with a sweeping emotional statement that summarizes what it just said. "At the end of the day, what matters most is..." "Ultimately, the real takeaway here is..." "In a world where..." These closings are the writing equivalent of a laugh track — telling the reader how to feel instead of just letting the content land.

Tell Claude to cut the summary statements. End on a specific point, not a platitude.

The Nuclear Option

When I really want something to sound human, I use this prompt as a baseline:

"Write this like a real person — not like AI. That means: vary your sentence and paragraph length. Use simple words. State things directly without hedging. Skip the adverbs. No sweeping summary statements. Start some sentences with 'And' or 'But.' Include the small imperfections that make writing feel alive. If it reads like it could have been generated, rewrite it until it doesn't."

It's a lot of instruction, but it works. The output reads like a person sat down and wrote it, which is the entire point.

The Ongoing Practice

Start noticing the tells in everything you read. Once you see them, you can't unsee them — and you'll get faster at prompting them away. The goal isn't to hide that you use AI. The goal is to make sure that what comes out the other side actually sounds like you.

Lesson 27 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 3 — Lesson 28

How to Give Claude a Rough Draft Reply and Have It Synthesize a Clean Outbound

The AI Prompting Course

This is maybe my single most-used workflow, and I've never seen anyone else talk about it. Someone sends me an email or a message with three questions, two requests, and a bunch of context. I know what I want to say back, but organizing a clean reply that addresses everything without being a wall of text? That takes time. Time I don't have.

So here's what I do. I read the incoming message, and I type out my response as fast as possible — no structure, no polish, just my actual answers and thoughts in whatever order they come out. Then I give Claude both the incoming message and my rough response, and ask it to synthesize a clean outbound.

The Exact Workflow

Step one: I copy the email or message I need to reply to.

Step two: I write my raw reply. This is messy. It looks something like: "yes on the timeline, tell them 2 weeks not 3. the budget thing — we can do 15k but not 12, that's below our floor. and about the design review, I think we should push that to after the beta launch so we're not context-switching. oh and he asked about the contract — tell him we'll send a revised SOW by friday."

Step three: I paste both into Claude with this prompt:

"Here's an email I received [paste incoming message]. Here are my rough notes on what I want to say back [paste rough reply]. Synthesize this into a clean, professional reply that addresses each of their points in a logical order. Keep my decisions and tone — just organize and polish it. Match the formality level of the incoming email."

Why This Works So Well

The magic is in that last instruction: "match the formality level of the incoming email." If someone wrote you a casual three-line message, your reply shouldn't be a five-paragraph formal letter. If someone sent you a detailed professional email, your reply should match that register. Claude picks up on this instantly when it has both messages to reference.

But the real reason this works is that it preserves your decision-making while outsourcing the organization. You've already done the hard work — you decided yes on the timeline, no on the budget below 15k, to push the design review, and to commit to a Friday SOW delivery. Those are real decisions. Claude isn't making them. It's just packaging them into a reply that flows well and doesn't miss anything.

The "Did I Miss Anything" Check

One of the most useful additions to this workflow is asking Claude to flag anything from the incoming message that you didn't address in your rough reply. I add this to the prompt:

"Also, check if there's anything in their email that I didn't address in my notes. If so, flag it so I can decide what to say."

This has saved me multiple times. Someone asks four things, I respond to three without realizing I missed one, and Claude catches it before I send a reply that leaves a question hanging. It's like having a proofreader who checks for completeness, not just grammar.

Handling Complex Thread Replies

This gets even more powerful with long email threads. Sometimes I'm replying to a message that references three previous emails, a shared document, and a meeting that happened last week. My rough reply might address things out of order or reference points from different parts of the thread.

For these situations, I expand the prompt:

"Here's the email thread [paste the relevant parts]. Here's the latest message I need to reply to [paste it]. Here are my rough thoughts on the reply [paste rough notes]. Synthesize a clean reply that flows naturally. Group related points together. If I'm referencing something from earlier in the thread, make sure the connection is clear to the reader."

The output is a reply that reads like I carefully read the entire thread and crafted a thoughtful, organized response. Which I did — I just didn't spend twenty minutes arranging it into paragraphs.

The Speed Difference

Without this workflow, a complex email reply takes me fifteen to twenty minutes. I read the incoming message, think about what I want to say, draft a reply, re-read the incoming message to make sure I hit everything, reorganize my reply, read it again for tone, and send.

With this workflow, I read the incoming message, brain-dump my responses in about sixty seconds, paste both into Claude, review the output for thirty seconds, and send. Three minutes. Maybe four if it's complicated.

And honestly? The Claude-organized version is usually better than what I'd have written on my own. Not because the ideas are different — they're my ideas — but because Claude is better at organizing a multi-point reply into a logical sequence than I am when I'm doing it from memory at 11pm.

Try This Now

Go to your inbox. Find the reply you've been putting off — the one with multiple questions or a complicated thread. Don't try to write a clean reply. Just dump your answers into a notes app, as messy as you want. Then give Claude both the incoming message and your brain dump. Watch how fast a polished reply comes together. This is how I handle email now. Once you try it, you won't go back to the old way.

Lesson 28 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 3 — Lesson 29

Offer Letters, Contractor Agreements, and Legal-Adjacent Docs Without Lawyers

The AI Prompting Course

I need to say something upfront: I'm not a lawyer and this lesson isn't legal advice. What I'm going to share is how I use AI to draft legal-adjacent documents — the kind of stuff that small companies and startups deal with constantly where hiring a lawyer for every single document is neither practical nor affordable.

When I was building out the team at Mudita Studios, I needed offer letters, contractor agreements, NDAs, and project scope documents. At the beginning, I tried to do what everyone tells you to do: hire a lawyer for everything. But here's the reality — when you're a small company, paying $300-500 for a lawyer to draft a basic contractor agreement for a freelance designer you're hiring for a two-week project doesn't make sense. Especially when you need the agreement today, not next Wednesday.

So I started using Claude to draft these documents, and then learned how to do it in a way that's actually useful and reasonably safe.

The Ground Rules

Before I show you the workflow, here are the rules I follow, and you should too:

Rule one: Get one real legal template reviewed by an actual lawyer for each document type you use regularly. Contractor agreement, offer letter, NDA, SOW — get one solid version of each. This is a one-time cost. Then use those as the foundation for AI-assisted drafting going forward.

Rule two: Use AI to customize templates, not to invent legal language from scratch. There's a big difference between "adapt this reviewed contractor agreement for a new freelancer with these specific terms" and "write me a contract." One is customization. The other is risky.

Rule three: For anything involving significant money, equity, or liability, get a lawyer. Full stop. AI is for the routine stuff — the $3,000 freelance gig, the standard NDA, the basic offer letter. Not for your Series A docs or a partnership agreement that could make or break your company.

The Workflow

Once I have a lawyer-reviewed template for a document type, here's how I use Claude to customize it for each new situation:

"Here's our standard contractor agreement template [paste it]. I need to customize this for the following situation: we're hiring a freelance frontend developer for 6 weeks at $85/hour, estimated 20 hours per week. The project is rebuilding our customer dashboard. We need them to start April 1. All work product should be owned by us. They'll have access to our codebase and design files so the confidentiality section applies. Payment is net-15 after invoice. Customize the template with these specifics, keeping all the standard legal language intact."

The key instruction is "keeping all the standard legal language intact." You don't want Claude rewriting the indemnification clause or changing the governing law section. You want it to fill in the blanks — names, dates, rates, project scope, specific deliverables — while leaving the lawyered language alone.

Offer Letters

Offer letters are probably the easiest document to do this with because they're less legally complex than contracts. They're essentially "here's the job, here are the terms, are you in?" I keep a template and then customize with:

"Customize this offer letter template for a senior product designer. Full-time, $130K base salary, standard benefits package, start date May 1, reporting to me. They'll be employee number 8. We're a small startup so keep the tone warm and excited — not corporate. Include the equity section with [X shares, 4-year vest, 1-year cliff]. Make it feel like a real welcome, not a form letter."

That last part matters. The best offer letters don't just list terms — they make someone excited to join. AI is great at adjusting tone to make a document feel personal while keeping all the necessary legal elements in place.

SOWs and Project Scopes

Statements of work are where this workflow really shines. Every client project needs an SOW, and they're incredibly tedious to write from scratch. They're also where most disputes originate — because the scope wasn't clear enough.

My approach:

"Here's our SOW template. Here are my notes from the client kickoff call [paste notes or transcript]. Draft an SOW that covers: project scope, specific deliverables with acceptance criteria, timeline with milestones, what's explicitly out of scope, payment schedule tied to milestones, and revision limits. Be extremely specific about what's included and what isn't — the goal is that six months from now, if there's a disagreement, this document should make it clear."

That last sentence is the most important instruction. The whole point of an SOW is to prevent future disagreements by being specific now. I tell Claude to optimize for clarity at the boundary — the places where scope could be ambiguous.

The Red Line Technique

When someone sends me a contract or agreement, I use Claude as a first-pass reviewer before I read it myself:

"Read this contract and flag anything that's unusual, one-sided, or that I should pay special attention to. Highlight any clauses that give the other party significantly more power or protection than they give me. Summarize the key terms and any potential issues in plain English."

This doesn't replace a lawyer reviewing important contracts. But it gives me a head start so I'm not reading a 15-page agreement blind. I go in already knowing which sections need my attention.

The Bottom Line

AI doesn't replace lawyers. But it replaces the 80% of legal document work that is filling in templates, customizing standard language, and turning your project details into properly structured agreements. For a small company that needs these documents regularly, this workflow saves thousands of dollars a year and hours of time per document. Just follow the ground rules: start with reviewed templates, use AI for customization not creation, and bring in a real lawyer when the stakes are high.

Module 3 — Lesson 30

Personal Notes That Don't Sound Generated

The AI Prompting Course

There's a weird irony in using AI for personal writing. The whole point of a personal note is that it's personal — it came from you, it reflects your actual feelings, it carries the weight of a real human sitting down and choosing words. And the second it sounds generated, all of that evaporates. It goes from thoughtful to lazy. From meaningful to insulting.

I've thought about this a lot because I use AI to help with personal writing all the time. Thank-you notes, congratulations messages, sympathy cards, birthday messages for people I actually care about. Not because I don't have real feelings — but because I'm not always good at expressing them on the spot, and sometimes having a starting point makes it easier to say what I actually mean.

The trick is a complete inversion of how most people use AI for writing.

The Wrong Way

Here's what most people do:

"Write a thank you note to my mentor."

And they get back something like: "Dear [Name], I wanted to take a moment to express my sincere gratitude for your invaluable guidance and mentorship. Your wisdom and support have been instrumental in my growth, both personally and professionally. I am truly grateful for the time and energy you have invested in my development."

Nobody talks like this. Nobody writes like this. If you sent this to someone who actually knows you, they'd know instantly that you didn't write it. It's the written equivalent of a store-bought card with "thinking of you" printed inside. Technically a nice gesture. Emotionally empty.

The Right Way

The approach that actually works is to give Claude the specific, personal details and then ask it to keep things understated. The more specific your input, the more personal the output. The less you tell it, the more generic it gets.

"I want to write a thank-you note to David, who's been my mentor for about three years. He helped me through a really rough patch last fall when I was questioning whether to keep building my company or go get a real job. He didn't give me a pep talk — he asked me hard questions and made me think through it myself, which is why I respect him. He also introduced me to two people who became key partners. I'm not a sappy person and neither is he. Keep it short, sincere, and a little understated. He'd be uncomfortable with anything too emotional."

Now Claude has real material to work with. Not "write a thank you" but "here's the specific relationship, here's what he actually did, and here's the emotional register we both operate in." The output sounds like a person because it's built from personal specifics, not generic sentiment.

The Specificity Rule

Here's a rule I follow: a personal note should contain at least one detail that only you would know. Something specific to your relationship with that person, a shared experience, an inside reference. If the note could be sent by anyone to anyone, it's not personal — it's just polite.

When I prompt Claude for personal writing, I always include those specific details in the brief:

"Mention the time he told me to stop reading business books and just go talk to ten customers. That advice changed everything and he'll know exactly what I'm referring to."

One specific memory does more than ten sentences of generic gratitude. It proves you were paying attention. It proves this message is for this person. That's what makes it personal.

Tone Calibration

Different relationships need different levels of warmth. I'm not going to write the same way to my college roommate as I do to a professional contact who helped me out. The tone instructions matter enormously for personal notes:

For someone close:

"Write this like I'm texting someone I've known for years. Casual, warm, maybe a little funny. They'd be weirded out by anything formal."

For a professional mentor:

"Sincere but not gushing. He's a no-nonsense person. A short, genuine message means more to him than a long effusive one."

For someone going through a hard time:

"Don't try to be uplifting or silver-lining this. Just acknowledge that this is hard and that I'm here. Simple. No platitudes."

That last one is critical. The worst AI writing happens with sympathy messages because the model defaults to optimism and encouragement. Sometimes people don't need to hear "things will get better." Sometimes they need to hear "this sucks and I'm sorry." Tell Claude which one you mean.

The Editing Approach

For my most important personal notes, I actually don't ask Claude to write the whole thing. I write it myself first — even if it's awkward or clunky — and then ask Claude to clean it up while preserving every specific detail and keeping my voice:

"Here's a note I wrote to a friend. It's a little rough but the feelings are real. Clean it up — fix any awkward phrasing, tighten it up — but don't change what I'm saying or how I'm saying it. If anything sounds generated after your edit, rewrite that part to sound more like a real person."

This gives you the best of both worlds. The substance is entirely yours. The delivery gets a gentle polish. And because you wrote the first draft, the specific details and emotional nuances are already baked in.

The Test That Always Works

Before you send any personal note that AI helped with, read it out loud. If any sentence makes you cringe, or sounds like something you'd never actually say, rewrite that sentence yourself. The AI got you 90% of the way there. The last 10% — the part that makes it truly yours — that comes from you. And that's fine. That's exactly how this tool is supposed to work. You're not outsourcing your feelings. You're getting help with the words.

Module 4 — Lesson 31

The One-Word Redirect: How to Course-Correct Claude Without Re-Explaining Everything

The AI Prompting Course

I was writing ad copy for a new campaign last week. I'd spent a good five minutes getting Claude dialed in — gave it the product context, the audience, the tone, the emotional angle, all of it. The first output was close but a little too formal. And my instinct used to be to retype the whole prompt with adjustments baked in.

Now I just type one word: "Warmer."

And the output shifts. Same structure, same information, same everything — just warmer. Exactly what I needed. Took two seconds.

I see this all the time with people who are getting comfortable with AI. They've learned the lesson about providing context. They give these great, detailed prompts. But then when the output isn't quite right, they go back to square one and rewrite the entire thing. It's like calling a meeting to tell someone to change the font size.

Why Short Redirects Work

Here's the thing most people don't fully internalize: Claude remembers the entire conversation. Every bit of context you gave it three messages ago? Still there. The brand voice you described? Still loaded. The audience profile? Still in play.

When you send a follow-up message, you're not starting from scratch. You're continuing a conversation. And just like in a real conversation, you don't need to repeat your entire briefing to ask for a small adjustment.

If you told a copywriter everything about the campaign and they handed you a draft that was too long, you wouldn't re-explain the entire brief. You'd say "Shorter." They'd know exactly what you mean because they have the full context already.

Claude works the same way. The context is already loaded. Your redirect just needs to point in the right direction.

The One-Word Toolkit

I have a handful of single-word or short-phrase redirects that I use constantly. Let me show you what I mean with real examples from how I actually work:

"Shorter." — When the output is good but too long. This is probably my most-used redirect. Claude tends to be thorough, which is usually great, but sometimes I need something punchy.

"Bolder." — When the copy is playing it safe. I use this a lot with ad headlines. The first version is usually correct but boring. "Bolder" pushes it toward something with more edge.

"Simpler." — When it's overcomplicating something. Especially useful for technical explanations or anything customer-facing.

"Again." — When I want a completely different take on the same brief. Not a revision — a fresh swing.

"More specific." — When the output is too generic or hand-wavy. This one is gold for frameworks and strategy docs.

"Less corporate." — When it sounds like a press release instead of a person. This comes up a lot.

When to Use a Phrase Instead

Sometimes one word isn't enough, but you still don't need to rewrite the whole prompt. A single sentence does the job.

"Same thing but written for someone who's never heard of us."
"Keep the structure but make every sentence half as long."
"This is good but it reads like a blog post — make it read like a text to a friend."

Notice what these all have in common. They reference the existing output ("same thing," "keep the structure," "this is good but"). They give one clear direction. And they don't re-explain any of the original context.

That's the pattern. Acknowledge what's there, point to what needs to change. That's it.

The Mistake People Make

The biggest trap I see is people re-prompting from scratch because they're afraid Claude will "forget" something. It won't. Not within the same conversation, at least. If your conversation is getting really long — like 30+ back-and-forth messages — context can start to fade a bit. We'll cover that in the next lesson.

But for normal conversations? The context is solid. Trust it. A one-word redirect is not lazy prompting. It's efficient prompting. You already did the hard work of setting things up. Now you're just steering.

The other mistake is stacking too many redirects at once. If you say "Shorter, warmer, more specific, and add a call to action" — that's four different changes and you're more likely to lose something in the process. Better to do them one at a time. "Shorter" first. Read the result. Then "Now add a clearer CTA at the end." Incremental adjustments beat shotgun edits every time.

A Real Example From This Week

I was working on a landing page headline for Official AI. My original prompt had the full context — what the product does, who it's for, the positioning we're going for, competing products, all of it. Claude gave me a headline that was accurate but flat.

Here's the conversation that followed. The whole thing took about ninety seconds:

"Punchier."

Better, but a bit aggressive.

"Pull back ten percent."

Close. But the second half of the headline felt weak.

"Stronger second half."

That was the one. Four total messages. Never re-explained the brief once. The final headline was something I never would've written from scratch, and it came from a conversation that was mostly one to three words at a time.

Try This

Next time you're working with Claude and the output is close but not right, resist the urge to start over. Instead, identify the one thing that's off and say just that. One word. One phrase. See what happens.

You'll be surprised how well it works. And you'll wonder why you ever rewrote entire prompts to change something small.

Module 4 — Lesson 32

Catching and Correcting Context Drift in Long Threads

The AI Prompting Course

Last month I was deep in a Claude thread — probably forty messages in — working through a positioning strategy for a client project at Mudita Studios. The early outputs had been sharp. Specific. Tight. But around message thirty, something started to feel off. The recommendations got more generic. The tone flattened out. It was still technically good work, but it had lost the edge that made the first outputs useful.

That's context drift. And if you use AI for anything longer than a quick one-off question, you're going to run into it.

What Context Drift Actually Is

Here's the thing. AI models have a context window — a limit on how much text they can "see" at once. Think of it like a sliding window over the conversation. When the conversation is short, everything fits. But as it gets longer, the earliest messages start getting pushed out of the window, or at least get less attention.

The result? Claude starts to lose the nuance you established early on. The specific constraints you set in message three get fuzzy by message thirty-five. The brand voice you carefully described? Slowly reverts to default. The competitive landscape you laid out? Gets replaced by generic market assumptions.

It's not that Claude is being careless. It's a structural limitation. And the good news is, once you know how to spot it, it's easy to fix.

How to Spot It

There are three telltale signs that context drift is happening:

The output gets more generic. This is the big one. If early outputs were referencing specific details — your company name, specific competitors, exact metrics — and later outputs start using vague language like "your industry" or "relevant stakeholders," drift is happening.

The tone shifts. You spent time establishing a voice and now the outputs sound like a different writer. More formal, more cautious, more... default. That's a sign the voice instructions have faded.

It starts contradicting earlier decisions. You agreed on a positioning angle in message ten, and in message forty Claude suggests something that conflicts with it. Not because it changed its mind — because it can't quite see message ten anymore.

The Quick Fix: The Context Refresh

When I notice drift happening, I do what I call a context refresh. It's dead simple. I just re-state the key constraints in a single message.

"Quick context refresh before we continue: We're building this positioning for Official AI, targeting solo founders and small teams. The tone should be direct and conversational — no corporate speak. We decided earlier that our primary angle is speed-to-value, not feature comparison. Our main competitors are [X] and [Y]. Keep going with the messaging framework."

That's it. One paragraph. Takes thirty seconds to write. And it snaps the conversation right back into focus. You're essentially re-loading the important variables without starting a new conversation.

The Preventive Approach

Better than fixing drift is preventing it. Here's what I do when I know a conversation is going to be long:

I front-load the critical context. The most important constraints — audience, tone, key decisions — go in the very first message. Not buried in message five or eight. First message.

I periodically re-anchor. Every ten to fifteen messages, I'll drop a quick one-liner that reinforces the key constraints. Something like "Keeping our audience of solo founders in mind..." before my next request. It's a tiny nudge that keeps things on track.

I use a running brief. For really big projects — like when Ben and I were mapping out the Official AI architecture — I keep a text file with the core context. When drift starts, I paste it in. No rewriting from memory, no forgetting things. Just copy, paste, back on track.

When to Start a New Thread

Sometimes the right move isn't to fix the drift — it's to start fresh. I have a general rule: if I've been in the same thread for more than fifty or sixty messages, or if I'm shifting to a fundamentally different task, I start a new conversation.

When I do, I don't just jump in cold. I write a proper brief that includes the key decisions and outputs from the previous thread. Something like:

"Starting a new thread. Here's where we landed: [summary of key decisions]. The positioning angle is [X]. The messaging framework we built is [paste it]. Now I need to take this and build out the landing page copy."

This gives you a clean context window with all the important stuff right at the top — exactly where Claude can see it most clearly.

The Real Lesson

Context drift isn't a bug you need to work around. It's just the nature of having a conversation with a tool that has a finite memory. The same way you'd recap key points in a long meeting with a colleague, you recap them with Claude.

The people who get consistently great outputs over long projects aren't the ones with better prompts. They're the ones who manage context intentionally. They know when to refresh, when to re-anchor, and when to start clean.

Try This

Next time you're in a long thread and something feels off — the output is getting blander or less specific — try dropping a context refresh. Restate the three or four most important things Claude needs to remember, right in the conversation. See how fast the quality snaps back.

Once you feel that snap, you'll never let a conversation drift again.

Lesson 32 of 100

Module 4 — Lesson 33

Generating 40 Variants, Ranking by CTR, Picking 3: The Funnel Approach

The AI Prompting Course

Most people use AI to generate one thing at a time. One headline. One email subject line. One ad hook. They prompt, they get a result, they either use it or throw it away. And that's fine for some tasks.

But for anything that matters — anything where performance is measurable — that approach leaves massive value on the table. Let me show you what I mean.

The Volume-First Mindset

When I'm writing ad hooks for a Meta campaign at Mudita Studios, I don't ask Claude for one hook. I ask for forty.

Not because I need forty hooks. I need three. Maybe five. But I need forty to find the three that are actually going to work. This is a concept that every great creative director understands: the best ideas come from having a lot of ideas to choose from. You can't edit your way to brilliance from a single mediocre starting point. You need volume first, then you filter.

Here's the thing — generating forty variants with AI takes roughly the same amount of time as generating four. The marginal cost of volume is basically zero. So why would you limit yourself?

How I Actually Do It

Let me walk you through the exact process I use. This is real, not theoretical — I ran this workflow last week for a campaign.

Step one: Set up the context. One detailed prompt with everything Claude needs — product, audience, emotional angle, constraints, tone.

"I need ad hooks for [product]. The audience is [specific audience]. The tone should be [tone]. The hook needs to stop the scroll — first line of a Facebook ad, max 15 words. The emotional angle is [angle]. Give me 40 variations. Go wide — I want safe options, bold options, question-based hooks, statement hooks, story hooks, controversy hooks. Don't self-filter. I'd rather cut bad ones than miss good ones."

That last part is important. "Don't self-filter." Claude's instinct is to give you polished, safe outputs. When you tell it to go wide and not self-censor, you get a much more interesting range.

Step two: Get the raw list. Claude gives you forty hooks. Some will be great. Some will be mediocre. A few will be terrible. That's exactly what you want.

Step three: First cut. I scan the list and immediately cut anything that's generic, confusing, or doesn't land. This takes about sixty seconds. I'm usually down to fifteen to twenty.

Step four: Rank by predicted performance. This is where it gets interesting. I take my shortlist and ask Claude to evaluate them.

"Here are 18 hooks I shortlisted. Rank them by predicted click-through rate for our target audience of [audience]. Consider scroll-stopping power, curiosity gap, emotional resonance, and clarity. Give me your top 5 with a one-sentence explanation of why each one would perform."

Now Claude is switching from creative mode to analytical mode. It's applying a different kind of reasoning — pattern matching against what tends to perform in paid social, weighing the elements that drive clicks. The ranking won't be perfect, but it's a strong signal.

Step five: Pick your winners. I take Claude's top five, overlay my own judgment — I know my audience better than any AI does — and pick the three I'm going to test. Sometimes I agree with Claude's ranking. Sometimes I swap one out for a hook I liked that Claude ranked lower. The point is I'm choosing from strength, not from a single option.

Why This Beats the One-at-a-Time Approach

When you generate one headline at a time, you're in edit mode. You're trying to polish a single thing until it's good enough. That's a local optimization — you're making the best version of one idea.

When you generate forty and filter, you're in selection mode. You're choosing the best idea from a wide field. That's a global optimization. And global beats local almost every time.

I've seen this play out in actual ad performance. The hooks I find through this process consistently outperform the ones I would've landed on through iterative editing of a single concept. Not always. But often enough that it's now my default workflow for anything performance-driven.

Where Else This Works

This isn't just for ad hooks. I use this same funnel approach for:

  • Email subject lines. Generate 30, rank by predicted open rate, test the top 3.
  • Product names. Generate 50 options across different naming styles, filter to 10, evaluate against criteria like memorability, domain availability, and trademark risk.
  • Positioning statements. Generate 20 angles, rank by differentiation and clarity, pick the top 3 to test with real users.
  • Call-to-action copy. Generate 25 CTA variations, rank by urgency and specificity, A/B test the top 2.

Any time the task is creative and the output is measurable, the funnel approach wins.

Try This

Next time you need a headline, a subject line, or any piece of copy where performance matters — don't ask for one. Ask for forty. Tell Claude to go wide and not self-filter. Then use Claude to help you rank them. You'll end up with better options in less time, and you'll never go back to the one-at-a-time approach.

Lesson 33 of 100

Module 4 — Lesson 34

The "Too Many Changes" Correction — Preserving Original Intent Across Edits

The AI Prompting Course

This one's personal because it used to drive me insane. I'd give Claude a piece of copy and ask it to make one small change. Maybe tighten the opening paragraph. And it would tighten the opening paragraph — great — but it would also rewrite the middle section, swap out a metaphor I liked, change the closing line, and rearrange two sentences that were already perfect.

I'd look at the output and think, "I asked you to change one thing. Why did you change twelve things?"

If you've worked with AI for more than a week, you've felt this. It's one of the most common frustrations. And it has a specific cause and a specific fix.

Why Claude Over-Edits

When you ask Claude to revise something, it's not just making the change you asked for. It's regenerating the entire piece of text, and in that process, it's making micro-optimizations everywhere. From Claude's perspective, it's trying to make the whole thing better. Every word gets reconsidered.

The problem is, some of those words were already exactly right. That turn of phrase you spent twenty minutes landing on? Claude doesn't know it was hard-won. It just sees text that could be slightly different and changes it.

This is the core tension: Claude's goal is to produce the best possible output. Your goal is to preserve what's already working while fixing what isn't. Those are different objectives, and if you don't make yours explicit, Claude will default to its own.

The Fix: Be Surgically Specific

The solution is to tell Claude exactly what to change and explicitly tell it to leave everything else alone. Let me show you what I mean.

Instead of this:

"Make the opening paragraph more compelling."

Try this:

"Rewrite ONLY the opening paragraph to be more compelling. Keep every other sentence in the document exactly as-is. Do not change any wording, phrasing, or structure outside of the first paragraph."

I know it feels redundant. "Only" should be enough, right? It's not. Claude needs the reinforcement. Saying "only change X" AND "don't change anything else" is the belt-and-suspenders approach, and it works way better than either one alone.

The Anchoring Technique

For bigger documents where I'm asking for targeted edits, I use what I call anchoring. I literally point to the specific section by quoting it.

"In the paragraph that starts with 'The real challenge for most founders...' — rewrite that paragraph to be more direct. Shorter sentences. Cut the first two sentences entirely and start with the third one. Everything else in the document stays exactly as written."

By quoting the text, I'm leaving zero ambiguity about what should change. Claude can see exactly which paragraph I mean, exactly what I want done to it, and exactly what should stay the same.

This is way more effective than saying "fix the third paragraph." Which third paragraph? Does the intro count? Does a one-sentence transition count as a paragraph? Remove the ambiguity and Claude will respect the boundaries.

The Version Control Trick

Here's something I started doing that saves me a ton of frustration. When I have a document I'm iterating on and I'm worried about losing good parts, I explicitly version it.

"Here's the current version of the landing page copy. I'm labeling this V3. I want you to create V4 with the following change and ONLY the following change: [specific edit]. If I diff V3 and V4, the only differences should be in the section I specified."

The phrase "if I diff V3 and V4" works surprisingly well. It frames the task in terms Claude understands — the output should be a minimal diff, not a rewrite. It's a constraint that maps directly to the behavior you want.

When the Whole Thing Needs Work

Sometimes the problem isn't that Claude changed too many things — it's that you actually do need a bigger revision but you're afraid of losing the parts that work.

In that case, I take a different approach. I tell Claude what to protect.

"I need you to rework this email pretty substantially — the structure isn't flowing right and the middle section drags. But there are specific things I want to keep exactly as they are: the opening line ('We've been thinking about this wrong'), the bullet points in section two, and the closing paragraph. Rework everything else but those elements are locked."

By telling Claude which pieces are "locked," I'm giving it freedom to improve the rest while protecting the things I've already decided are right. It's like telling a contractor "tear out the kitchen but don't touch the living room."

The Deeper Principle

This lesson is really about something bigger than document editing. It's about maintaining control over the creative direction. AI is a powerful collaborator, but if you don't steer it, it will make choices for you. Some of those choices will be good. Some will undo work you've already done.

The people who use AI best aren't the ones who let it run free. They're the ones who give it clear boundaries. "Change this. Protect that. Don't touch the rest." That's not limiting the AI — it's directing it. And directed AI produces way better results than AI that's guessing at what you want.

Try This

Take something you've already written — an email, a doc, anything. Ask Claude to make one specific change. But when you do, use the full constraint: tell it exactly what to change, quote the specific section, and explicitly say everything else stays the same. Compare this to what happens when you just ask for the change without those guardrails. The difference is night and day.

Lesson 34 of 100
