Module 4 — Lesson 35

Asking Claude to Critique Its Own Output Before You Do

The AI Prompting Course

One of the biggest upgrades to my workflow happened when I stopped being the only quality filter. Not because I'm bad at spotting issues — but because I realized I could make Claude do the first pass for me.

Here's the setup. Claude gives you a draft. You read it. You spot the problems. You tell Claude to fix them. Claude fixes them. You read it again. Maybe you spot more. Another round of fixes.

That works. But it's slow. And it puts all the critical thinking burden on you.

What if, before you even read the output, Claude already told you what was wrong with it?

The Self-Critique Prompt

After Claude gives me any substantial output — a strategy doc, a piece of copy, a framework — I'll often follow up with something like this:

"Before I review this, I want you to critique your own output. What are the three weakest parts? Where did you make assumptions that might be wrong? What would a skeptic push back on? Be genuinely critical — I need honest feedback, not a polite summary."

And here's what's wild: Claude is usually really good at this. It'll point to the exact sections that were bothering me before I even articulated what was off. It'll flag assumptions I hadn't noticed. It'll identify structural weaknesses that I would've eventually caught but might have missed on a quick read.

It's like having a built-in editor who reads every draft before you do.

Why This Works So Well

There's something interesting happening here from a cognitive perspective. When Claude generates text, it's optimizing for completion — producing the most likely, most coherent continuation of the conversation. It's in "creation mode."

When you ask it to critique, you're switching it to "evaluation mode." Different objective, different behavior. In evaluation mode, Claude applies more rigorous reasoning. It looks for gaps, inconsistencies, weak arguments. It's essentially running a second pass with a different lens.

The output from that second pass is often genuinely insightful. Not always — sometimes it'll flag things that aren't actually problems, or miss the real issue. But as a first filter? It's incredibly useful.

The Three-Layer Critique

I've found the critique is most useful when I give it specific lenses to evaluate through. Here's the structure I use most often:

"Critique this output on three levels: (1) Logic — are there any gaps in reasoning or unsupported claims? (2) Audience — would this actually resonate with our target audience, or does it miss what they care about? (3) Specificity — where is this too vague or generic, and what specific details would make it stronger?"

By giving Claude specific evaluation criteria, you get a more structured and useful critique. Without criteria, it tends to default to surface-level feedback like "the tone could be more engaging" — which isn't very actionable. With criteria, it goes deeper.

Critique Then Fix — The Two-Step

The real power move is making the critique and the revision a two-step process. Don't just ask Claude to critique. Ask it to critique, then fix the issues it found.

"Critique your output — what are the weakest parts? Then give me a revised version that addresses every issue you identified."

This consistently produces better second drafts than just asking for a revision without the critique step. It's the difference between "make this better" (vague) and "identify the specific problems, then solve them" (structured). The critique creates a roadmap for the revision.

I used this last week on a pitch deck script. The first draft was solid but something felt off. Instead of trying to pinpoint it myself, I asked Claude to critique it. It identified that the "problem" slide was too abstract — it described the problem in industry terms instead of in the customer's language. Dead on. Then the revised version grounded everything in a specific customer scenario. Huge improvement, and I barely had to think about it.

When to Use This (And When Not To)

I use self-critique for anything substantial — strategy docs, long-form copy, frameworks, presentations. Anything where quality matters and there's enough complexity for things to go wrong.

I don't use it for quick tasks. If I'm asking Claude to write a three-sentence email, I don't need it to critique its own output. That's overkill. The critique step adds time and tokens, so use it where it actually adds value.

I also don't use it as a substitute for my own judgment. Claude's self-critique is a first filter, not the final word. Sometimes it'll flag something as a weakness that's actually a deliberate choice. Sometimes it'll miss the real problem entirely. Your taste and judgment are still the most important quality control. This just makes the process faster.

Try This

Next time Claude gives you a draft of something important, don't start editing immediately. Ask it to critique its own work first. Use specific criteria — logic, audience fit, specificity, whatever matters for that particular output. Read the critique. See if it matches what you were already thinking. Then ask for a revised version that addresses the issues. You'll be surprised how much better the second draft is when Claude has already identified what's wrong.

Lesson 35 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 4 — Lesson 36

Running the Same Prompt Through Multiple Framings and Comparing

The AI Prompting Course

I learned this one by accident. I was writing a product description for a feature we were shipping at Official AI, and I wasn't happy with the output. So I tried a different angle — same feature, same audience, but framed as a problem-solution instead of a feature-benefit. The result was dramatically better. Not incrementally better. A completely different level of quality.

That made me realize something. The framing of your prompt — the lens you ask Claude to look through — often matters more than the specific wording. And most people only ever try one framing.

What I Mean by "Framing"

A framing is the angle or perspective you use to approach a task. Same information, different lens. Let me give you a concrete example.

Say I need to write a landing page section explaining why someone should switch from their current tool to ours. I could frame that prompt in at least five different ways:

  • Problem-solution: "Start with the pain of using the current tool, then present our product as the solution."
  • Before-after: "Describe the user's life before and after switching."
  • Comparison: "Do a direct, honest comparison of the old approach vs. the new approach."
  • Story: "Tell the story of a specific person who made the switch and what changed."
  • Contrarian: "Challenge the assumption that the current approach is working at all."

Each of these will produce fundamentally different output. Same product, same audience, same facts — wildly different copy. And you don't know which one will resonate most until you see them side by side.

The Multi-Framing Workflow

Here's how I actually run this. I start with one solid context block — the product info, audience, constraints, tone. Then I run it through three to five different framings in sequence.

"Using all the context above, write this section as a problem-solution narrative."

Then:

"Now write the same section, same information, but frame it as a before-and-after transformation."

Then:

"One more version — this time frame it as a direct challenge to the status quo. Be provocative."

Now I have three completely different takes. I can read them side by side, see which angle hits hardest, and often the final version is a hybrid — the structure of one with the emotional punch of another.

Why This Beats Iterating on a Single Version

When you iterate on one version — "make this punchier," "add more urgency," "shorten it" — you're polishing. You're making incremental improvements to a single approach. And sometimes that's all you need.

But polishing can only take you so far. If the underlying angle isn't right, no amount of word-smithing will fix it. An average headline with the right angle will outperform a brilliant headline with the wrong angle every single time.

Multi-framing gives you diversity of approach, not just diversity of wording. It expands the solution space. It's the difference between testing different shades of blue vs. testing blue, red, and green. Sometimes the answer isn't a better blue — it's that you should've been using green all along.

The Comparison Step

Once I have multiple versions, I sometimes ask Claude to compare them for me:

"You just wrote three versions of this section: problem-solution, before-after, and contrarian. Compare them. Which one is most likely to convert a skeptical reader? Which one is most emotionally compelling? Which one is clearest? Give me your honest ranking and tell me why."

Claude's comparison won't always match my instincts, but it surfaces considerations I might not have thought about. And sometimes it makes a case for a version I would've dismissed that changes my mind.

Where This Matters Most

I use multi-framing anytime the task is creative and the "right" approach isn't obvious. Ad copy, landing pages, positioning statements, pitch narratives, sales emails. Basically, anything where the angle you choose is a creative decision, not a given.

I don't use it for technical writing, data analysis, or tasks where there's clearly one right approach. If I'm asking Claude to summarize a report, I don't need five framings of the same summary. But if I'm asking it to make that summary compelling for a specific audience? Then framing matters.

The key insight is this: most creative tasks have multiple valid approaches. If you only explore one, you're hoping you picked the right one. If you explore several, you're choosing from options. Choosing beats hoping.

Try This

Pick a piece of copy or content you need to create. Before you iterate on the first version Claude gives you, ask for two more versions with completely different framings. Read all three back to back. Notice how different the same information can feel when you change the angle. Then pick the one that hits hardest — or combine elements from multiple versions into something better than any single one.

Lesson 36 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 4 — Lesson 37

The A/B Testing Mindset: Provocative vs. Aspirational, Short vs. Long

The AI Prompting Course

I used to have strong opinions about what kind of copy works best. Punchy and provocative. Short sentences. Challenge the reader. That was my default, and for a while, it worked.

Then I started actually testing. Running different styles head-to-head on real audiences with real data. And you know what I found? My instincts were right about half the time. Which means they were wrong about half the time. That's humbling. And that's exactly why an A/B testing mindset — applied to how you prompt AI — is one of the most valuable skills you can develop.

What A/B Testing Means for Prompting

In paid advertising, A/B testing is straightforward. You run two versions of something, split the audience, and see which one performs better. You let data decide, not opinions.

You can apply the exact same thinking to how you generate content with AI. Instead of deciding in advance that your ad should be provocative, generate both a provocative version and an aspirational version. Instead of assuming short copy will outperform, generate a short version and a long version. Then look at them with clear eyes — or better yet, test them for real.

Claude makes this almost free. Generating a second or third variation takes seconds. There's no reason to commit to one approach when you can easily see multiple options side by side.

The Core Dimensions to Test

Through running ads for clients and for our own projects, I've identified the creative dimensions that make the biggest difference. These are the axes where variation actually matters:

Provocative vs. Aspirational. Provocative copy challenges the reader, creates tension, calls out a problem. "You're wasting 3 hours a day on this and you don't even know it." Aspirational copy paints a picture of the future, creates desire. "Imagine closing your laptop at 3pm knowing everything's handled." Both work. Which one works better depends entirely on your audience and where they are in the funnel.

Short vs. Long. There's a persistent myth that short copy always wins. It doesn't. For cold audiences, longer copy that builds context and trust often outperforms. For warm audiences who already know you, short and direct tends to win. The point is, you don't know until you test.

Emotional vs. Logical. Some audiences respond to stories and feelings. Others respond to data and proof. "I went from burned out to booking 5 new clients a month" vs. "Users report a 340% increase in qualified leads within 60 days." Same product, totally different approach.

Direct vs. Indirect. Direct says what the product is and what it does. "A CRM that updates itself." Indirect tells a story or asks a question that leads the reader to the product. "What if your CRM already knew what happened in the meeting?" Sometimes the indirect path is more compelling because it creates curiosity.

How I Generate A/B Pairs

I have a simple workflow for this. After setting up the context, I'll ask for deliberate contrasts:

"Give me two versions of this ad. Version A should be provocative — challenge an assumption, create tension, make the reader slightly uncomfortable. Version B should be aspirational — paint a picture of the outcome, make the reader want what we're offering. Same product, same audience, completely different emotional approach."

Then I'll follow up:

"Now give me two more versions. Version C should be short — three sentences max, punchy, no wasted words. Version D should be longer — tell a mini-story, build context, take the reader on a journey. Aim for 150 words on D."

Now I have four distinct versions across two dimensions. I can mix and match — maybe the provocative angle works better in short form, and the aspirational angle works better as a longer narrative. I wouldn't know that if I only generated one version.

The Testing Part

If you're running paid ads, you can test these for real. Put them in an A/B test on Meta or Google and let the data decide. This is the gold standard.

If you're not running ads — say you're writing a cold email or a landing page — you can still apply the mindset. Show both versions to two or three people you trust and ask which one they'd click on. Or send different versions to different segments of your list. Or just use your judgment, informed by having seen the actual options side by side instead of imagining them in the abstract.

The key principle is this: don't let your assumptions narrow the creative space before you've seen the options. Generate the variations first. Then decide.

What I've Learned From Real Tests

A few things I've learned from running actual A/B tests on AI-generated copy that might surprise you:

Aspirational copy tends to win for premium products. Provocative copy tends to win for problem-solving products. But not always.

Long copy beats short copy more often than you'd think, especially on Facebook. People will read if the hook is good enough.

Question-based hooks outperform statement hooks in most of my tests. "What would you do with 10 extra hours a week?" beats "Save 10 hours a week with X." The question creates engagement. The statement feels like an ad.

But these are my data points from my audiences. Yours will be different. That's the whole point of testing.

Try This

Next time you're creating any piece of copy — an ad, an email, a headline — don't just generate one version and refine it. Ask Claude for deliberate A/B pairs along one of the dimensions I mentioned. Provocative vs. aspirational. Short vs. long. Emotional vs. logical. Read them side by side. Notice which one you're drawn to and ask yourself honestly: is that the one that would actually perform best, or is it just the one that matches your taste? There's often a gap between the two.

Lesson 37 of 100

Module 4 — Lesson 38

Stress-Testing a Framework Against Edge Cases Before Shipping It

The AI Prompting Course

A few months ago, I built a pricing framework for a client project. It was clean. It was logical. It had a nice three-tier structure that made sense in a slide deck. I was proud of it.

Then a customer asked about annual billing with a mid-year upgrade and the whole framework fell apart. The tiers didn't account for prorated upgrades. The enterprise tier had a feature that conflicted with the mid-tier SLA. An edge case that took about four seconds to imagine blew up something I'd spent hours building.

That's when I started using Claude to stress-test everything before it ships. And I wish I'd learned this years ago.

What Stress-Testing Means

Stress-testing is simple: you take something you've built — a framework, a process, a strategy, a set of rules — and you deliberately try to break it. You throw unusual scenarios at it and see if it holds up.

Most people skip this step because it feels negative. You just built something and now you're trying to destroy it? But that's exactly the point. Better to find the holes before your customers do. Before your boss does. Before reality does.

And Claude is the perfect stress-testing partner because it can generate edge cases faster than you can think of them. It doesn't have the same blind spots you do. It hasn't spent hours building the thing, so it doesn't have the emotional attachment that makes you unconsciously avoid poking at weak spots.

How I Run a Stress Test

The process is straightforward. First, I present the framework or system to Claude. Then I ask it to break it.

"Here's a pricing framework I built for [product]. Three tiers, structured like this: [details]. I need you to stress-test this. Give me 10 realistic scenarios where this framework breaks down, creates confusion, or produces an outcome that would frustrate a customer. Think about edge cases — upgrades, downgrades, team size changes, annual vs. monthly billing, refund situations, feature overlap between tiers. Be adversarial. Your job is to find the holes."

That last instruction — "be adversarial" — is important. Without it, Claude tends to be polite. It'll give you gentle observations instead of pointing at the structural cracks. You want it playing the role of the person who's trying to break your system, not the person who's trying to be supportive about it.

What I Stress-Test

I use this for way more than pricing. Here's a partial list of things I've stress-tested with Claude in the past few months:

  • Onboarding flows. "Here's our onboarding sequence. Give me 10 user scenarios where someone would get stuck, confused, or drop off."
  • Sales scripts. "Here's our sales call framework. Give me the 10 hardest objections a prospect could raise and tell me where our script doesn't address them."
  • Content calendars. "Here's our publishing schedule. What gaps exist? What topics are we over-indexing on? Where would audience fatigue set in?"
  • Refund policies. "Here's our refund policy. Give me 10 customer situations where this policy would either be ambiguous, unfair, or create a support nightmare."
  • Partnership agreements. "Here's the outline of a partnership deal. What are the 10 most likely ways this could go sideways?"

Basically, anything with rules, conditions, or if-then logic is a candidate for stress-testing. And the more important it is, the more you should stress-test it.

The Fix-and-Retest Loop

Finding the holes is only half the value. The other half is fixing them and testing again. Here's the workflow:

Step one: Present the framework.

Step two: Ask Claude to break it with edge cases.

Step three: Review the edge cases. Some will be legitimate problems. Some will be unlikely scenarios you can accept. Sort them.

Step four: Fix the legitimate problems and present the revised framework.

Step five: Ask Claude to break it again with new edge cases, given the changes.

Usually two rounds is enough. By the end of the second round, the framework is significantly more robust than what you started with. The holes that would've been embarrassing in a client meeting or a live product? Already patched.

The Mindset Shift

Here's the thing most people get wrong about stress-testing. They see it as criticism. It feels bad to have something you built picked apart.

Flip that. Stress-testing isn't criticism — it's quality assurance. Every edge case Claude finds is a problem you don't have to deal with later. Every hole it identifies is a customer complaint that won't happen, a support ticket that won't get filed, a meeting that won't go sideways.

I've gotten to the point where I don't trust anything I haven't stress-tested. Not because I don't trust my own work. Because I know that everything has edge cases, and the difference between amateur work and professional work is whether you found them before or after shipping.

Try This

Take something you've built recently — a process, a framework, a set of guidelines, a plan. Paste it into Claude and ask it to generate ten realistic edge cases where the thing breaks down. Be specific about what kind of stress you want applied. Then fix the real problems and test again. By the second round, you'll have something meaningfully stronger than what you started with. And you'll start to see stress-testing not as an extra step but as a non-negotiable one.

Lesson 38 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 4 — Lesson 39

How to Give Feedback That Tightens Claude's Output Without Starting Over

The AI Prompting Course

There's a skill that separates people who are pretty good at using AI from people who are really good at it. And it's not prompting. It's feedback. Specifically, the ability to look at an output that's seventy percent right and give feedback that gets it to ninety-five percent without blowing up the seventy percent that was already working.

I see this all the time. Someone gets a solid first draft from Claude. It's got the right structure, the right information, mostly the right tone. But something's off. Maybe it's too long. Maybe the opening is weak. Maybe it's using jargon the audience wouldn't know. And instead of giving precise feedback, they either start over with a new prompt or give vague direction like "make it better."

Both of those waste the good work that's already there. Let me show you how to give feedback that builds on what's working.

The Anatomy of Good Feedback

Good feedback to Claude has three components. What's working. What's not working. What to do about it.

Most people skip the first one and go straight to what's wrong. But telling Claude what's working is just as important as telling it what isn't. It anchors the revision. It says "keep this" before you say "change that."

Here's what this looks like in practice:

"The structure of this is solid — I like that you led with the problem and built to the solution. Keep that flow. But the tone is too formal for our audience. We're writing for indie founders who talk casually. Rewrite with the same structure but make it sound like a smart friend explaining something over coffee. Also, the third paragraph repeats a point from the first paragraph — cut the redundancy."

Look at what's in that feedback. Positive anchor ("structure is solid, keep that flow"). Specific problem ("tone is too formal"). Context for the fix ("indie founders who talk casually"). A concrete metaphor for the target tone ("smart friend over coffee"). And a specific structural note ("third paragraph repeats, cut it").

That's actionable feedback. Claude knows exactly what to preserve and exactly what to change.

Common Feedback Mistakes

Let me walk through the feedback patterns that don't work and explain why.

"Make it better." Better how? This tells Claude nothing. It'll make random changes, some of which might improve things and some of which might make them worse. You haven't given it a direction. This is the equivalent of telling a chef "make it taste better" without saying whether you want more salt, more spice, or less sweetness.

"I don't like it." Okay, but what specifically? Is it the tone, the structure, the content, the length? Claude can't read your mind. When you don't like something, take ten seconds to figure out why, then tell it the why.

"Try again." This is a coinflip. Sometimes you'll get a better result because Claude will take a different approach. But you have no control over what changes. Maybe the second version is worse in different ways. "Try again" is not feedback — it's gambling.

Too many notes at once. If you give Claude eight different pieces of feedback in one message, the revision will address most of them but probably drop one or two. Better to group feedback into two or three key points. If you have more, do two rounds of revision.

The Ratio Technique

Here's a technique I use when the output is close but I can't quite articulate what's wrong. I use ratios.

"This is about 80% there. The part that's working is the argument structure and the specific examples. The 20% that's off is the energy — it reads a bit flat. I want it to feel more urgent, like there's something at stake. Amp up the tension without changing the substance."

The ratio gives Claude a sense of scale. "80% there" communicates that this isn't a teardown — it's a polish. And by identifying what's in the 80% and what's in the 20%, you draw a clear line between what to keep and what to fix.

I've found this works especially well when the issue is tonal or emotional rather than structural. Tone is hard to describe precisely, but saying "the energy is flat" or "it needs more tension" gives Claude something to work with.

Layered Revision

For important pieces — anything going to a client, going on a landing page, going into a pitch deck — I do layered revision. Multiple passes, each focused on one thing.

First pass: structure and logic. Is the argument sound? Is the flow right? Fix that first.

Second pass: tone and voice. Does it sound right? Is it hitting the emotional register I want?

Third pass: precision. Every word earning its place? Anything I can cut? Any vague phrases I can make specific?

Each pass gets its own message to Claude with focused feedback. This is way more effective than trying to fix everything at once. By the third pass, the output is tight.

The Feedback Loop Mindset

Here's what I want you to take away from this lesson. Working with AI isn't a one-shot process. It's a conversation. And the quality of that conversation — specifically, the quality of your feedback — determines the quality of the final output.

Great feedback is specific, constructive, and clear about what to preserve. It doesn't ask Claude to read your mind. It doesn't start from scratch when the foundation is solid. It builds iteratively, one focused revision at a time.

Once you get good at this, you'll find that you can take a mediocre first draft and turn it into something excellent in two or three exchanges. Not by writing a better initial prompt — by giving better feedback on what comes back.

Try This

Next time Claude gives you an output that's close but not quite right, write your feedback using the three-part structure: what's working, what's not, and what to do about it. Be specific. Use the ratio technique if the issue is tonal. Do one focused revision at a time instead of dumping every note in a single message. Notice how much more control you have over the final result when your feedback is precise.

Lesson 39 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 4 — Lesson 40

When to Push Harder vs. When the Output Is Good Enough

The AI Prompting Course

I need to be honest about something. I have a problem with over-iterating. There have been times when I've spent thirty minutes going back and forth with Claude on a piece of copy that was perfectly good after the second draft. Tweaking a word here. Adjusting a phrase there. Chasing some imaginary perfect version that doesn't exist.

And that's a real trap. Because the same skills that make you good at iterating — attention to detail, high standards, knowing how to give good feedback — can also turn you into someone who spends an hour perfecting an email that nobody will read twice.

This lesson is about knowing when to stop. Which, honestly, might be the most practical lesson in this entire module.

The Diminishing Returns Curve

Here's something I've noticed after a year of using AI intensively. The quality improvement from iteration follows a predictable curve:

First draft to second draft: Huge improvement. This is where the biggest gains happen. The second draft usually fixes the major issues — wrong tone, missing information, structural problems. Going from draft one to draft two is almost always worth it.

Second draft to third draft: Solid improvement. You're refining. Tightening language, cutting fluff, strengthening specific sections. This is still clearly valuable for important work.

Third draft to fourth draft: Marginal improvement. You're polishing edges. The changes are real but small. Worth it for high-stakes deliverables — a pitch deck, a major campaign, a sales page. Not worth it for internal docs or routine communications.

Fourth draft and beyond: You're in diminishing returns territory. The improvements are so small that you're basically trading time for perfectionism. And sometimes you actually make it worse — over-polished copy can lose its energy. I've seen it happen. Draft three was alive and punchy, and by draft six it was technically perfect but lifeless.

The "Stakes Test"

The simplest framework I use for deciding when to push harder is what I call the stakes test. I ask myself one question: what happens if this output is 85% instead of 95%?

If the answer is "nothing meaningful" — it's a Slack message, an internal update, a social media post that'll be buried in a day — then 85% is good enough. Ship it. Move on. Your time is better spent on the next thing.

If the answer is "something real" — it's a client deliverable, a landing page that thousands of people will see, a pitch to an investor, the copy for a high-spend ad campaign — then push to 95%. Do the extra rounds. Sweat the details. The stakes justify the time.

Most things in business are in the first category. We just convince ourselves they're in the second category because perfectionism feels productive. It's not. It's a time trap dressed up as high standards.

Signs You Should Push Harder

There are specific signals that tell me an output needs more work:

You can feel something is off but can't name it. That vague unease usually means there's a real problem lurking. When I feel this, I ask Claude to critique its own output (lesson 35) — the critique often surfaces what my gut was sensing.

The output is structurally sound but emotionally flat. This is common. The logic is right, the information is right, but it doesn't move you. That's a sign to push on tone and energy. These are usually quick fixes — one more round with focused feedback.

You'd be embarrassed if the wrong person saw it. The "would I show this to the CEO" test. Or the client. Or the audience. If there's a cringe factor, it's not done yet.

The core argument is weak. If the foundation isn't solid — if the logic doesn't hold up, if the positioning isn't right, if the strategy has gaps — polishing the surface won't help. You need to push harder on the substance, not the style.

Signs You Should Stop

And here are the signals that tell me it's time to ship:

You're changing things back. Classic sign. You changed a phrase in round three, then changed it back in round four. If you're going in circles, the output was done two rounds ago.

The changes are about preference, not quality. "Should I say 'fast' or 'quick'?" If the choices are lateral — neither is better, just different — stop. You're past the point of meaningful improvement.

You've been on this for more than fifteen minutes and the last two rounds didn't change much. Set a time limit for yourself. When I catch myself spending more than fifteen minutes iterating on something that isn't truly high-stakes, I force myself to ship it.

You can articulate exactly why it's good but have a vague feeling it could be "better." "Better" is infinite. At some point, good enough is good enough. If you can explain why the output works — it hits the right audience, makes the right argument, in the right tone — then it works. Ship it.

The Speed Principle

Here's something I've learned running Mudita Studios. Speed of output is itself a form of quality. A good email sent today is worth more than a perfect email sent tomorrow. A solid ad campaign launched this week beats a flawless one launched next month.

AI makes us faster. But it can also make us slower if we use all that speed to iterate endlessly instead of to ship more. The real productivity gain isn't "I can make one perfect thing in the time it used to take to make one good thing." It's "I can make five good things in the time it used to take to make one."

Volume and speed win in most business contexts. Not sloppy speed. Not careless volume. But the discipline to know when something is ready and to move on.

The Real Lesson

The entire point of this iteration module — lessons 31 through 40 — has been about getting better outputs through refinement. And that's genuinely important. But refinement has to serve a purpose. If you're refining to make something better for a real audience in a real context, great. If you're refining because stopping feels uncomfortable, that's a different thing entirely.

The best AI users I know iterate fast and ship fast. They're decisive. They know the difference between "this needs work" and "this is done and I'm just fiddling." They use all the techniques we've covered in this module — redirects, self-critique, multi-framing, precise feedback — but they use them efficiently. Two or three rounds, not ten.

Learn to iterate well. But also learn to stop. That combination is what makes you dangerous.

Try This

Next time you're iterating on something with Claude, set a rule: three rounds max. After three rounds of revision, ship it. Notice what happens. You'll probably find that the output after three rounds is better than you expected. And you'll definitely find that you have more time for everything else. The constraint forces you to give better feedback earlier — which, ironically, makes the iteration process more effective, not less.

Lesson 40 of 100

Module 5 — Lesson 41

Using Claude to Find the Research Backing for Something You Already Intuitively Know

The AI Prompting Course

You know that feeling when you've been running a business long enough that you just know something is true, but you couldn't cite a study if someone asked? That's where this technique comes in. And honestly, it's one of the most underrated ways to use AI for research.

Here's the thing. When I was building out the positioning for Official AI, I had a gut sense that most small business owners don't actually want more features in their software. They want fewer decisions. I'd seen it in every client conversation at Mudita Studios. But "I just feel like that's true" doesn't fly in a pitch deck or a blog post.

The Intuition-First Research Prompt

Most people start research from scratch. They ask Claude something broad like "what do small businesses want from software?" and get back a wall of generic text. The trick is to start with what you already believe and ask Claude to find the evidence.

I have a working hypothesis based on years of direct experience: small business owners experiencing software fatigue prioritize simplicity and fewer decisions over additional features. Can you find the empirical research, surveys, and published data that either supports or contradicts this? I want specific studies, sample sizes where available, and the actual findings — not summaries of conventional wisdom.

See the difference? You're not asking Claude to think for you. You're asking it to be your research assistant for a thesis you already hold. This is a fundamentally different mode of operation, and it produces dramatically better output.

Why This Works So Well

When you give Claude a specific hypothesis, three things happen. First, it has a concrete direction to search through its training data, which means less generic filler. Second, you get results organized around proving or disproving something specific, which is actually useful. Third, you often discover that your intuition maps to well-documented phenomena with names you didn't know — like "decision fatigue" or "the paradox of choice."

When I ran that prompt, Claude came back with Sheena Iyengar's choice overload research, Hick's Law from cognitive psychology, and specific SaaS churn studies showing that feature bloat is the number two reason small businesses cancel software subscriptions. My gut feeling suddenly had an academic backbone.

The Follow-Up That Makes It Rigorous

Don't stop at the first pass. The gold is in the follow-up.

Now play devil's advocate. What research exists that contradicts this hypothesis? Are there studies showing that small businesses actually do want more features, or contexts where complexity is preferred? I want to steelman the opposing view.

This is critical. If you only ask for supporting evidence, you're doing confirmation bias with extra steps. The devil's advocate prompt forces Claude to surface the nuance — and there's always nuance. In my case, it surfaced research showing that businesses in growth phases actually do seek more capabilities, but they want them surfaced progressively rather than all at once. That's a more sophisticated version of my original thesis, and it only emerged because I asked for the counter-argument.

Turning It Into Something Usable

Once you have both sides, you can ask Claude to synthesize:

Given the supporting and contradicting evidence you've found, write a nuanced two-paragraph summary of what the research actually says about small business software preferences. Write it in a tone I could use in an investor memo — authoritative but not academic.

Now you've gone from "I feel like this is true" to a research-backed position with citations in about ten minutes. That's the power of starting with your intuition instead of starting from zero.

Try This

Think of one thing you believe about your industry, your customers, or your market that you've never bothered to look up. Something you'd bet money on but couldn't prove in a meeting. Run it through this three-step process: hypothesis prompt, devil's advocate, synthesis. You'll either confirm what you knew with receipts, or you'll discover your intuition was slightly off in a way that makes your thinking sharper. Either way, you win.

Lesson 41 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 5 — Lesson 42

Competitive Moat Analysis in One Session

The AI Prompting Course

I see this all the time: founders who can tell you everything about their product but go blank when you ask "why can't someone else just copy this in six months?" That's a moat question, and most people either hand-wave through it or avoid it entirely. Claude can help you answer it honestly in a single session — if you prompt it correctly.

When Ben and I were working through the positioning for Official AI, we needed to articulate why our approach was defensible. Not just "we're better" but structurally, architecturally, why this thing compounds over time. I didn't want a cheerleading session. I wanted a real analysis.

Setting Up the Competitive Landscape

The first prompt needs to establish the playing field. Don't just name competitors — describe the entire competitive landscape including indirect substitutes.

I'm building [describe your product in one sentence]. My direct competitors are [list 3-5]. But people also solve this problem by [describe workarounds and substitutes — spreadsheets, agencies, doing nothing, etc.]. Analyze the competitive moat potential across these seven dimensions: network effects, switching costs, data advantages, brand/trust, economies of scale, regulatory barriers, and technical complexity. For each dimension, rate my current moat as nonexistent, emerging, or established, and explain why.

The key here is including the substitutes. Most competitive analyses only look at direct competitors. But the spreadsheet your customer is currently using? That's your real competitor. The agency they're paying $5K/month to? That's your real competitor. Claude can only analyze what you give it, so give it the full picture.

The Honest Assessment Push

Here's where most people get lazy. Claude will sometimes give you generous ratings because you framed the question around your product. Push back hard.

Be more critical. For any dimension you rated as "emerging," explain specifically what would need to be true for it to be "established," and estimate how long that would take. For any dimension rated "nonexistent," tell me whether it's worth pursuing or if I should accept it's not part of my moat and focus elsewhere.

This follow-up is where the real insight lives. When I ran this for Official AI, Claude correctly identified that our data advantage was "emerging" and told us it would take approximately 18-24 months of active usage data to become a meaningful moat. That's not what I wanted to hear, but it was exactly what I needed to hear for planning purposes.

The "What Would Kill This" Test

The best moat analysis includes a kill scenario. This is uncomfortable, but it's the most valuable part.

Now imagine you're a well-funded competitor who just raised $10M and your sole mission is to destroy my competitive position. What would you do? What sequence of moves would be most threatening? Where am I most vulnerable in the next 12 months?

When Claude puts on the adversary hat, it often surfaces risks you've been subconsciously ignoring. For us, it identified that a larger platform could bundle our core functionality as a free add-on, which is exactly the kind of threat that doesn't show up in a feature comparison but could be existential.

Building the Moat Roadmap

The final step turns analysis into action:

Based on this analysis, give me a prioritized 12-month moat-building roadmap. What are the three highest-leverage things I can do in the next quarter to strengthen my most promising moat dimensions? Be specific about actions, not strategies.

You'll walk out of this single session with a competitive moat assessment that would cost you $15-25K from a strategy consultant. It won't be as nuanced as months of deep market research, but for most early-stage decisions, it's more than enough to act on.

Try This

Run this full sequence for your business or project. But here's the key — do it before you need it. Don't wait until an investor asks about your moat or a competitor launches a threatening feature. Do it now, while you have the mental space to actually think about the answers and build your strategy around them.

Lesson 42 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 5 — Lesson 43

Investment Scoring with Composite Rubrics: Building Your Own Evaluation Framework

The AI Prompting Course

One of the biggest problems I see with decision-making is that people evaluate options by vibes. They look at an opportunity, get a gut feeling, and go with it. Sometimes that works. But when you're comparing five potential investments, three partnership opportunities, or eight possible features to build next, vibes don't scale. You need a rubric. And Claude is shockingly good at helping you build one from scratch.

Why Composite Scoring Beats Pros-and-Cons Lists

A pros-and-cons list treats every factor equally. "Great team" sits next to "office is far away" as if those carry the same weight. A composite rubric lets you assign weights to what actually matters, score each option against those weighted criteria, and get a number you can compare across options. It doesn't replace judgment — it structures judgment so you can see where your thinking is solid and where it's fuzzy.

Let me show you what I mean. When we were evaluating which market segment to target first with Official AI, I had opinions but I didn't have a framework. So I built one with Claude.

The Framework-Building Prompt

I need to evaluate and rank [X options] for [specific decision]. Help me build a weighted scoring rubric. First, suggest 8-10 evaluation criteria that would be relevant for this type of decision. For each criterion, suggest a weight from 1-5 based on how important it typically is. Then define what a score of 1, 3, and 5 would look like for each criterion so I have clear anchors. Format this as a table I can actually use.

What Claude gives you back is a structured rubric with clear definitions. But here's where it gets good — you're not supposed to use it as-is. You're supposed to argue with it.

The Calibration Conversation

After Claude generates the initial rubric, push back on the weights and criteria:

I'd argue that [criterion X] should be weighted higher because [reason from your experience]. And I think you're missing a criterion around [thing you care about]. Also, your definition of a "5" for market size feels too generous — in my space, a realistic "5" would look more like [your definition]. Revise the rubric with these adjustments.

This back-and-forth is where the rubric becomes genuinely yours. Claude provides the structure. You provide the domain knowledge and the judgment calls about what matters. By the end of two or three rounds of calibration, you've got a scoring tool that reflects how you actually think about decisions — just made explicit and repeatable.

Scoring the Options

Now you use it. Give Claude the details on each option and ask it to score:

Using the rubric we built, score each of these three options. For each criterion, give the score AND a one-sentence justification for why you assigned that score. Then calculate the weighted total. At the end, note any criteria where the scores are particularly close and might be worth deeper investigation.

The one-sentence justification is the secret weapon. It forces Claude to show its work, which means you can immediately spot where you disagree and adjust. Maybe Claude scored Option B a 4 on "team capability" and you know for a fact it should be a 2. You change it, the total shifts, and now you're seeing the decision through a clearer lens.

The Sensitivity Check

One more step that most people skip:

Run a sensitivity analysis. If I change the weight of [your top criterion] from 5 to 3, does the ranking change? What about if I bump [your second criterion] to 5? I want to know how robust this ranking is and which weight changes would flip the top recommendation.

This tells you whether your decision is robust or fragile. If the top option wins no matter how you adjust the weights, you can move forward with confidence. If a small weight change flips the ranking, you know exactly where to focus your deeper research.

Try This

Pick a real decision you're facing — hiring between candidates, choosing a vendor, selecting a feature to build, deciding between markets. Build the rubric with Claude. Score your options. Run the sensitivity analysis. The whole process takes about 20 minutes, and you'll make a better decision than you would have by staring at a whiteboard for two hours.

Module 5 — Lesson 44

Turning a Market Scan into a Prioritized Channel Strategy

The AI Prompting Course

Market research is only useful if it leads to action. I've seen founders do extensive competitive analysis, customer research, and trend mapping — then have no idea what to actually do on Monday morning. The gap between "I understand my market" and "I know which channels to invest in" is where most research goes to die. Let me show you how to bridge that gap in a single Claude session.

Starting With What You Actually Know

The mistake people make is asking Claude to tell them which marketing channels to use from zero context. That's like asking a doctor for a prescription without describing your symptoms. Instead, dump everything you know first.

Here's what I know about my market. My target customer is [detailed description — not demographics, but their situation and problem]. My competitors are primarily acquiring customers through [list what you've observed]. My budget is [realistic number] per month for growth. My team is [size and capabilities]. My product's strongest selling point is [one sentence]. Given all of this, what does the research and conventional wisdom say about which acquisition channels typically work best for this type of product, customer, and budget level?

Notice I'm asking Claude to match channels to my specific constraints, not just list every channel that exists. A solo founder with $2K/month has a completely different optimal channel mix than a funded startup with $50K/month and a marketing hire. If you don't provide your constraints, Claude will give you advice for a generic company that doesn't exist.

The Prioritization Framework

Once Claude gives you the initial channel list, force it to prioritize ruthlessly:

Now rank these channels using an ICE framework — Impact (how many qualified customers could this realistically reach), Confidence (how certain are we this will work based on comparable companies), and Ease (how quickly can I test this given my current resources). Score each 1-10 on all three dimensions, multiply for a composite score, and rank them. I want your honest assessment, not an optimistic one.

I ran something like this when figuring out the go-to-market for Mudita Studios' service offerings. Claude ranked cold outbound email as my highest-ICE channel, which surprised me because I'd been spending most of my energy on content marketing. The confidence score was high because agency services in our price range have well-documented outbound playbooks. The ease score was high because I could test it in a week with zero budget. Content marketing scored well on impact but low on ease — it would take months to see results.

Building the 90-Day Test Plan

Rankings alone aren't a strategy. You need a test plan.

For the top 3 channels, build me a 90-day testing plan. For each channel, specify: the minimum viable test (smallest thing I can do to validate this channel works), the key metric I should track, what "success" looks like at day 30 so I know whether to keep going or kill it, and the estimated time commitment per week. Be realistic about timelines — don't assume everything works on day one.

This is where Claude really earns its keep. It'll break down something like "LinkedIn organic" into specific actions: post frequency, content types to test, engagement benchmarks by follower count, and what response rates to expect in the first month. You get a playbook, not a suggestion.

The Kill Criteria Matter Most

Here's the thing most channel strategies miss: knowing when to quit. Without clear kill criteria, founders pour months into channels that aren't working because they keep thinking "maybe next month."

For each channel, define explicit kill criteria. What specific numbers, after what specific time period, would tell me this channel is not going to work for my business? I want concrete thresholds, not "if it doesn't feel right."

Now you have a complete system: ranked channels, test plans, success metrics, and kill criteria. You've gone from "I should do some marketing" to a structured experiment with clear decision points. All from one Claude session built on top of your market knowledge.

Try This

Take whatever marketing channels you're currently using and run them through this sequence. You might discover you're investing heavily in your third-best channel while ignoring your first-best one. Or you might validate that you're already focused correctly — but now you'll have the kill criteria to know when to pivot if things change.

Module 5 — Lesson 45

Using Web Search + Claude to Synthesize Conflicting Information Sources

The AI Prompting Course

The internet is full of confident, contradictory information. One article says email marketing has the highest ROI. Another says it's dead. One report says the AI market is $200 billion. Another says $50 billion. This isn't a bug — it's the nature of complex topics. Different methodologies, different definitions, different incentives. The question isn't "which source is right?" The question is "what's actually going on across all of these sources?"

Claude is exceptionally good at synthesis — taking multiple conflicting inputs and finding the signal. But you have to set it up correctly.

The Source-Gathering Phase

If you have access to web search through Claude, start by collecting the conflicting data points. But don't just ask for "research on X." Ask for the disagreement.

Search for recent data and analysis on [topic]. I'm specifically looking for sources that disagree with each other — different market size estimates, conflicting trend analyses, or contradictory recommendations. I want to understand the range of opinions and data points, not just the consensus view. Find at least 4-5 sources with meaningfully different conclusions.

This prompt is doing something subtle but important. It's telling Claude you want the messy reality, not a clean narrative. Most research prompts implicitly ask for agreement — "tell me about X" biases toward the dominant view. Asking for disagreement surfaces the full landscape.

The Decomposition Step

Once you have conflicting sources, don't ask Claude to tell you which one is right. Ask it to explain why they disagree.

These sources disagree significantly. Break down the likely reasons for the disagreement. Consider: different definitions of key terms, different time periods being measured, different methodologies, different sample populations, potential conflicts of interest or incentive structures of the publishers, and different levels of rigor. For each source, note what its specific methodology or perspective seems to be.

Let me show you what I mean with a real example. When I was researching the AI agent market for Official AI's positioning, I found market size estimates ranging from $5 billion to $65 billion. That's not a small discrepancy. When I asked Claude to decompose the disagreement, it identified that the lower estimates were counting only standalone AI agent platforms, while the higher estimates included AI agent functionality embedded in existing software suites. Same term, completely different definitions. Once you see that, the "contradiction" dissolves — they're measuring different things.

The Synthesis Prompt

Now you're ready for the real work:

Given the sources and the reasons they disagree, synthesize what we can actually say with confidence about [topic]. Structure it as: (1) What all sources agree on, regardless of methodology. (2) What the disagreement is actually about once you control for definitional and methodological differences. (3) What remains genuinely uncertain and why. (4) What practical conclusion I should draw given my specific context of [describe your situation].

That four-part structure is the key to the entire technique. Part one gives you solid ground to stand on. Part two resolves false contradictions. Part three tells you where the real uncertainty lives. Part four makes it actionable for you specifically.

Why This Matters More Than Most People Realize

The ability to synthesize conflicting information is arguably the highest-value research skill you can develop with AI. Anyone can find data. Anyone can find an article that supports their view. The person who can look at five conflicting sources and extract what's actually true — that person makes better decisions than everyone else in the room.

Most business decisions are made on incomplete, contradictory information. The question isn't whether you have perfect data. The question is whether you're being rigorous about what you know, what you don't know, and what you're assuming.

Try This

Pick a topic where you've been relying on a single source or a single narrative. Maybe it's a market trend, a best practice, or a competitive claim. Run the full sequence: find the disagreement, decompose why sources conflict, and synthesize what you can actually say with confidence. You'll almost certainly discover that reality is more nuanced than the single-source story you've been carrying around.

Module 5 — Lesson 46

Building a Six-Dimension Framework from Scratch and Stress-Testing It in One Session

The AI Prompting Course

Frameworks are how smart people compress complexity into something they can reason about. But most frameworks people use are borrowed — Porter's Five Forces, SWOT analysis, the Business Model Canvas. These are fine. They're also generic. The most powerful frameworks are the ones you build yourself for your specific problem, and Claude can help you build and pressure-test one in a single session.

When You Need a Custom Framework

I reached for this approach when we were evaluating potential partnership structures for Official AI. No existing framework quite fit our situation — we needed to assess partnerships across dimensions that were unique to our business model, our stage, and our strategic priorities. A generic partnership evaluation template would have missed half of what mattered.

Here's the thing about frameworks: their value isn't in being comprehensive. It's in forcing you to decide what the relevant dimensions are. That act of selection is itself an analytical exercise.

The Framework Generation Prompt

I need to build a custom evaluation framework for [specific problem or decision type]. The existing frameworks I've seen don't capture [what's missing or unique about your situation]. Help me identify 6 dimensions that would be most useful for evaluating [what you're evaluating]. For each dimension, I need: a clear name, a one-sentence definition of what it measures, why it matters for my specific context, and a 1-5 scale with concrete anchor descriptions at 1, 3, and 5. Don't give me generic business dimensions — these should be specific to the problem of [your problem].

The instruction "don't give me generic business dimensions" is doing heavy lifting. Without it, Claude defaults to things like "market opportunity" and "competitive advantage." With it, you get dimensions tailored to your actual decision. When I ran this for partnership evaluation, I got dimensions like "integration burden" (how much engineering time does this partnership require from us) and "signal value" (does this partnership make us look more credible to our target market). Those are dimensions that matter for an early-stage startup. They wouldn't appear in any textbook framework.

The Stress Test

Building the framework is only half the work. Now you break it.

Stress-test this framework. Try to identify: (1) Two dimensions that might be measuring the same underlying thing and could be collapsed into one. (2) An important consideration I'm missing entirely — a seventh dimension I should add. (3) A scenario where this framework would give a misleading result — where something scores well on all six dimensions but would actually be a bad decision. (4) Whether the 1-5 anchors are calibrated so that real options would actually spread across the scale, or if everything would cluster around 3-4.

This stress-test prompt is gold. I've never run it without discovering at least one meaningful flaw in my initial framework. Usually it's the clustering problem — your anchors are defined in a way that makes almost everything a 3 or 4, which means the framework can't differentiate between options. Claude catches this and helps you recalibrate the anchors so you get real spread.

The Real-World Validation

The final step is running a known case through your framework to see if it produces sensible results:

Let me validate this framework against a known outcome. Here's a decision I made in the past: [describe a past decision and how it turned out]. Score that option using our framework. Does the score match the actual outcome? If it would have scored high but actually turned out badly, we need to figure out what the framework is missing. If it would have scored low but actually worked great, same thing — something's miscalibrated.

Back-testing against a known outcome is how you build confidence that your framework actually works before you use it for a live decision. If it would have predicted your past correctly, you can trust it more for the future.

Try This

Think of a recurring decision type in your work — evaluating hires, assessing projects, choosing clients, picking features. Build a custom six-dimension framework with Claude, stress-test it, and validate it against a past decision. Then save it. You now have a reusable decision tool that gets better every time you use it and refine the calibration.

Module 5 — Lesson 47

The "What Does the Empirical Data Actually Say" Push — Getting Past the Framing

The AI Prompting Course

There's a type of answer Claude gives that sounds authoritative but is actually just well-written framing. It'll say things like "research shows that diverse teams outperform homogeneous ones" or "studies indicate that morning routines boost productivity." These statements aren't wrong, exactly. But they're hiding a lot of complexity behind confident language. And if you're making real decisions based on these claims, you need to get past the framing to the actual data.

The Problem With Narrative-First Research

I see this all the time when people use AI for research. They ask a question, get a clean narrative, and treat it as fact. But narratives are constructed. Every clean story about what "the research says" is a simplification of messy, contradictory, context-dependent findings. The skill is knowing when to accept the narrative and when to push through it.

Here's when you should push: any time you're making a significant decision based on what "the evidence shows." Any time a claim feels too clean, too universal, or too convenient for the argument being made. Any time you're about to repeat something to a client, investor, or team member as if it's settled science.

The Empirical Data Push Prompt

You said [claim Claude made]. I want to get past the narrative framing and understand the actual empirical data. Specifically: What are the most-cited studies behind this claim? What were their sample sizes and methodologies? What were the effect sizes — not just "significant" but how large was the effect? What are the most common critiques of these studies? Has this finding replicated consistently, or are there notable failed replications? What conditions or contexts does the finding apply to, and where does it break down?

This prompt forces Claude out of storytelling mode and into data mode. The difference in output is dramatic. Instead of "studies show X," you get "a 2018 meta-analysis of 43 studies with a combined N of 12,000 found an effect size of d=0.3, which is considered small to moderate, and the finding was strongest in [specific context] but did not hold in [different context]."

A Real Example

When I was putting together research for a client presentation at Mudita Studios, Claude initially told me that "companies with strong brand consistency see 23% more revenue growth." Sounds great. Sounds citable. But I pushed it.

Turns out, that 23% figure came from a single Lucidpress survey — not a peer-reviewed study. The survey was self-reported by marketing professionals (who have incentive to inflate the value of brand consistency). The sample was primarily large enterprises, not the SMBs my client served. And the definition of "brand consistency" in the survey was so broad that it basically meant "companies that have their act together generally do better."

That's a massive difference between the initial claim and the reality. And I only found it because I pushed past the framing.

The Follow-Up That Gets You to Ground Truth

Given those limitations, what can we actually say with confidence? What's the most honest, defensible version of this claim that I could make in a professional context without overstating what the evidence shows?

This question is incredibly valuable. It asks Claude to do something most people never do with research: find the defensible version. Not the strongest claim you could make. Not the weakest. The one you could stand behind if someone who actually read the studies challenged you on it.

When to Use This and When Not To

You don't need to do this for every claim. If you're writing a blog post and Claude says "email marketing typically has strong ROI," that's fine to use without interrogation. But if you're about to tell an investor that "the market is shifting toward X" or tell a client that "this strategy increases conversion by Y%," you should push. The stakes determine the rigor.

Try This

Go back to the last research you did with Claude. Find one specific claim that you accepted at face value and ran the empirical data push on it. See what comes back. If the claim holds up, great — now you have a stronger, more defensible version of it. If it falls apart, you just avoided building a strategy on a shaky foundation.

Module 5 — Lesson 48

How to Ask Claude to Identify the Single Common Thread Across 25 Sources

The AI Prompting Course

There's a specific research situation I find myself in constantly: I've read a bunch of stuff — articles, interviews, case studies, reports — and I can feel a pattern emerging, but I can't quite articulate it. It's like having a word on the tip of your tongue, except it's an insight on the tip of your brain. Claude is exceptional at finding that thread, but only if you give it the right kind of input.

Why Summarization Isn't the Same as Synthesis

Most people ask Claude to "summarize these sources." Summarization compresses each source individually. Synthesis finds what connects them. These are fundamentally different cognitive operations, and you need the second one.

I hit this when I was doing customer research for Official AI. I'd talked to about 25 people, read through their feedback, and had pages of notes. I could tell they were all circling the same frustration, but each person described it differently. One talked about "tool fatigue." Another said "I spend more time managing software than doing my actual work." Another complained about "context switching between twelve tabs." Same underlying problem, different language.

The Pattern-Extraction Prompt

I'm going to give you information from [number] different sources. I want you to find the single deepest common thread — not a list of themes, not a summary of each source, but the ONE underlying pattern or insight that connects the majority of them. Think of it like this: if all of these sources are different expressions of the same underlying reality, what is that reality? Here are the sources: [paste your notes, quotes, summaries, or key points from each source]

The critical instruction here is "not a list of themes." Left to its own devices, Claude will give you five themes. That's useful sometimes, but it's not what we're after. We want the single thread. Forcing Claude to commit to one answer makes it do deeper analysis. It can't hide behind comprehensiveness.

Layering In the "So What"

Once Claude identifies the thread, push for implications:

Good. Now tell me: if this is the underlying pattern, what does it predict about [your specific context]? What decisions should it inform? And is there a name for this pattern in academic research, behavioral psychology, or business literature that I should know about?

That last question — "is there a name for this" — is secretly powerful. When I ran my customer research through this process, Claude identified the underlying thread as "tool overhead exceeding tool value" and connected it to a concept called "productivity tax" from organizational psychology. Having a name for the pattern transformed how I talked about our product. Instead of listing features, I could say "we eliminate the productivity tax" and people immediately got it.

Handling Large Source Sets

If you have more material than fits in a single prompt, batch it:

I'm going to share sources in three batches. After each batch, give me your preliminary read on the common thread. After the final batch, give me your refined answer incorporating all sources. Feel free to change your initial read as new information comes in — I want your best final answer, not consistency with your first guess.

That last instruction — "not consistency with your first guess" — matters because Claude, like humans, has an anchoring bias. Its first impression of the pattern will color how it interprets later sources. Explicitly telling it to revise prevents it from forcing later data into an early framework that might not be right.

When One Thread Isn't Enough

Sometimes there genuinely isn't one thread. There are two or three. If Claude pushes back and says the sources don't converge on a single point, ask this:

If you had to identify no more than three distinct threads, what would they be? And for each thread, which specific sources cluster around it? I want to see the groupings.

This gives you a map of your sources organized by underlying pattern, which is often more useful than a single thread anyway. You can see which insight has the most evidence behind it and which is a minority pattern that might be worth investigating separately.

Try This

Gather the last 10 pieces of customer feedback, user research notes, or market observations you've collected. Paste them into Claude with the pattern-extraction prompt. See what thread it finds. I'd bet money it surfaces something you've been feeling but haven't been able to name — and naming it will change how you talk about your work.

Module 5 — Lesson 49

Using Claude to Draft a Competitive Teardown Without Being Unfair

The AI Prompting Course

Competitive teardowns are one of the most requested and most poorly executed types of analysis. Most of them fall into one of two traps: either they're so generous to competitors that they're useless ("everyone's great in their own way!"), or they're so unfair that they destroy your credibility with anyone who actually knows the space. The goal is a teardown that's honest, specific, and makes you look sharp — not petty.

Why Fairness Is a Strategic Advantage

Here's the thing that most people miss about competitive analysis: your credibility is your most valuable asset. If a potential customer reads your teardown and thinks "this seems biased," you've lost more than you've gained. But if they read it and think "wow, they really understand the landscape, including where their competitors are strong," you've just established yourself as the most trustworthy voice in the room.

I learned this the hard way at Mudita Studios. Early on, I wrote a competitive positioning doc that was basically a hit piece on our competitors. A prospect who was evaluating us alongside one of those competitors saw it and said "this just seems like you're trashing the other guys — I don't trust the comparison." That was a $40K lesson in fairness.

The Balanced Teardown Prompt

I need a competitive teardown comparing my product [describe yours] with [competitor name and description]. Structure it this way: For each company, identify their genuine strengths, their genuine weaknesses, and who they're best suited for. Be fair — acknowledge where they beat us. But also be specific about where our approach is genuinely different or better, and explain the structural reason why, not just "we're better." The reader should finish this feeling like they got an honest, insider-level view of the market, not a sales pitch.

The instruction "explain the structural reason why" is key. Saying "our product is faster" is a claim. Saying "our product is faster because we process data on-device rather than making round trips to a cloud server, which eliminates network latency" is an explanation. Explanations are persuasive. Claims are just noise.

The Generosity Trick

This is the counterintuitive move that makes teardowns powerful: be more generous to your competitors than they'd be to you.

For the competitor's strengths section, steelman their position. What's the strongest case a smart person could make for choosing them over us? Don't straw-man it. Then, after stating that case fairly, explain the specific situation or priority where our approach would be the better choice despite those strengths.

When you steelman the competitor's case, two things happen. First, you earn the reader's trust because they can see you're being honest. Second, your counter-argument hits harder because it's responding to the strongest version of the competitor's pitch, not a weak caricature of it.

Handling the "They're Copying Us" Impulse

Every founder thinks competitors are copying them. Sometimes they are. But framing it that way in a competitive teardown makes you look insecure. Instead:

Where our features overlap with [competitor], explain the different philosophical approaches behind similar-looking functionality. Same feature can exist for different reasons with different design priorities — surface that distinction without making it sound like we're claiming they copied us.

This produces analysis like "Both platforms offer automated scheduling, but [competitor] optimizes for volume (scheduling as many posts as possible with minimal input) while we optimize for timing precision (scheduling fewer posts at moments of peak audience engagement)." That's a meaningful, fair distinction that helps the reader understand the real difference.

The Final Check

Before you use any competitive teardown, run this final filter:

Read this teardown from the perspective of: (1) a customer of [competitor] who loves their product, (2) someone from [competitor]'s team, and (3) a neutral industry analyst. Would any of them consider any claim here unfair, misleading, or easily disprovable? Flag any statements that wouldn't survive scrutiny.

This perspective-check catches the subtle unfairnesses that sneak in — the cherry-picked comparisons, the slightly misleading framings, the claims that are technically true but contextually deceptive. Anything that gets flagged either gets revised or removed. What survives is a teardown that's genuinely bulletproof.

Try This

Pick your most direct competitor. Run the full sequence: balanced teardown, steelman their strengths, surface the philosophical differences, and do the three-perspective check. You'll end up with a competitive analysis you can confidently share with prospects, investors, or your team — because it's the kind of analysis that makes you look like you actually understand the market, not like you're afraid of the competition.
