Module 5 — Lesson 50

Research-to-Deck Pipeline: Turning Findings into Structured Slide Content

The AI Prompting Course

You've done the research. You've got pages of notes, data points, frameworks, and insights. And now you need to turn it into a deck — 15 slides that tell a clear story to an investor, a client, or your team. This is where most people lose hours. They stare at a blank slide, try to figure out which finding goes where, and end up with either a wall-of-text deck or a pretty-but-empty one. Claude can compress this entire process into a structured pipeline.

Why Research Doesn't Automatically Become a Deck

The gap between research and a presentation is a narrative gap. Research is organized by topic. Decks are organized by story. Your research might have sections on market size, competitive landscape, customer pain points, and technology trends. But your deck needs to flow: here's the problem, here's why it's getting worse, here's our unique insight, here's the solution, here's the evidence it works, here's the ask. Same information, completely different structure.

I build decks constantly — for pitching Official AI, for Mudita Studios client proposals, for strategic presentations. Every time, the hardest part isn't the research. It's the translation from "everything I know" to "the 15 things you need to see in this order."

Step 1: The Story Arc Extraction

Here are my research findings on [topic]: [paste your research, notes, data points, frameworks — everything]. I need to turn this into a [number]-slide deck for [specific audience: investors, clients, board, team]. Before we write any slides, identify the narrative arc. What's the story these findings tell? Give me: the opening hook (why should the audience care), the tension (what problem or opportunity is at stake), the insight (what we know that changes everything), the resolution (what we're proposing), and the proof (what evidence supports the resolution). Just the arc, not the slides yet.

This step is crucial and most people skip it. They jump straight to "write me slide content" and end up with a deck that feels like a report chopped into rectangles. The story arc is what makes a deck persuasive instead of merely informative.

Step 2: The Slide Outline

Good. Now map that arc onto a [number]-slide structure. For each slide, give me: the slide title (short, specific, makes a claim — not just a topic label), the single point this slide makes, which specific research findings or data points support this slide, and whether this slide needs a chart, a comparison table, a single stat, a quote, or just text. Don't write the full content yet — just the structure.

The instruction about titles is important. "Market Overview" is a topic label. "The SMB software market is consolidating around three platforms" is a claim. Claims-as-titles is a presentation technique from management consulting that makes every slide instantly readable. Even if someone just flips through your title slides, they should understand your argument.

Step 3: The Content Pass

Now write the content for each slide. Keep text minimal — no more than 30 words per slide for the main body (bullet points or short sentences). Any supporting data should be formatted as a specific number, not a paragraph. For chart or table slides, describe the visual content that should appear. Write speaker notes separately for each slide — 2-3 sentences that I'd actually say out loud while showing this slide.

The 30-word constraint is deliberate. Most decks have too much text. If you can't make the point in 30 words, you either don't understand the point well enough or you're trying to make two points on one slide. Either way, the constraint forces clarity.

Step 4: The Stress Test

Final step before you build the slides:

Review this deck structure from the audience's perspective. Where would a skeptic push back? Which slide's argument is weakest? Is there a logical gap between any two consecutive slides where the audience might get lost? Are there any findings from my original research that are important but didn't make it into the deck — and should they? Give me specific revision suggestions.

This catches the biggest presentation killer: logical gaps between slides. You know your research intimately, so your brain fills in connections that the audience won't have. Claude catches the places where slide 7 assumes knowledge that wasn't established until slide 10, or where the transition from "market problem" to "our solution" skips the "why existing solutions fail" step.

Try This

Take any research you've done recently — even the output from earlier lessons in this module — and run it through this four-step pipeline. Story arc, slide outline, content pass, stress test. You'll have a deck structure in under 30 minutes that would normally take you an afternoon. And it'll be a better deck, because the narrative was designed before the content was written, not reverse-engineered after.

Module 6 — Lesson 51

Designing Multi-Step Workflows With Claude Before Writing a Single Line of Code

The AI Prompting Course

Before I write a single line of code for anything at Mudita Studios, I design the entire workflow in a conversation with Claude. The whole thing. Inputs, outputs, decision points, error handling, edge cases. All of it hashed out in plain English before anyone touches a codebase.

I see this all the time with founders and operators -- they jump straight into building. They open up n8n or start writing a script, get halfway through, realize the logic doesn't work, tear it down, and start over. They do this three or four times before they land on something functional. And then they wonder why the project took two weeks instead of two days.

The problem isn't the building. The problem is they never designed the system first.

Why Claude Is Better Than a Whiteboard

I used to design workflows on whiteboards and in Notion docs. It worked okay. But there's something Claude can do that a whiteboard can't -- it can stress-test your logic in real time. You describe a workflow, and Claude can immediately say "What happens when the input is empty?" or "This step assumes the API always returns a 200 -- what's your fallback?"

That back-and-forth is worth more than any diagram. You're not just mapping the happy path. You're pressure-testing every branch before you've committed to anything.

Here's the thing. When I was building the lead qualification pipeline for Official AI, I didn't start with code. I started with this prompt:

"I need to design a multi-step workflow for qualifying inbound leads. Here's the current process: leads come in through a Typeform, we manually review them in a spreadsheet, then someone sends a personalized email. I want to automate this. Walk me through the full workflow step by step -- every input, every decision point, every output. Ask me questions about anything that's unclear before you start designing."

Claude came back with twelve clarifying questions. Things like: What fields does the Typeform collect? What criteria make a lead "qualified"? What's the SLA on response time? Do different lead tiers get different email templates?

I answered all twelve. And then Claude mapped out a seven-step workflow that was better than anything I would have designed on my own, because it caught edge cases I hadn't thought about.

The Framework I Use Every Time

When I'm designing a workflow with Claude, I always structure the conversation in three phases.

Phase 1: Describe the current state. Tell Claude exactly how things work right now. The messy version. The manual steps, the bottlenecks, the parts that break. Don't clean it up. The messier and more honest your description, the better the design will be.

Phase 2: Define the desired state. What should this look like when it's working? What's the trigger? What's the end result? How fast should it happen? Who's involved at each step? What tools are you already using?

Phase 3: Let Claude design the bridge. This is where the magic happens. Claude takes your current state and desired state and maps out every step in between. But the key is to tell it to ask questions first. Don't let it just start designing -- make it interrogate your assumptions.

"Before you design anything, ask me every question you need answered to build this properly. I'd rather answer twenty questions now than rebuild this system three times."

That one line has saved me dozens of hours.

What This Looks Like in Practice

Let me give you a concrete example. Last month I needed a content repurposing workflow. Take a long-form blog post and turn it into a LinkedIn post, three tweets, an email snippet, and a short-form video script. I described the process to Claude and asked it to design the workflow.

What Claude came back with wasn't just "Step 1: Summarize the post." It was a detailed pipeline:

  • Step 1: Extract the core argument and three supporting points from the blog post
  • Step 2: Identify the most emotionally resonant quote or insight
  • Step 3: Generate the LinkedIn post using the core argument, optimized for the hook-story-insight format
  • Step 4: Generate three tweets -- one provocative take, one tactical tip, one that links back to the full post
  • Step 5: Write the email snippet as a teaser with a single CTA
  • Step 6: Write the video script as a 60-second monologue using the emotional hook from Step 2
  • Step 7: Quality check -- does each piece stand alone without needing the original post?

Each step had defined inputs and outputs. Each one was specific enough to implement. And the whole thing took fifteen minutes of conversation, not two days of trial and error.

Try This

Think about a process in your business or work that involves more than three manual steps. Something you do regularly. Something that feels like it should be automated but you haven't gotten around to it.

Open Claude and describe it. All of it -- the messy, manual, current-state version. Then tell Claude: "Design a workflow that automates this. Before you start, ask me every question you need answered."

Answer the questions. Let it design. Then look at what it built. I guarantee there will be at least two steps or edge cases you hadn't thought of. That's the whole point. You're using Claude as a systems designer before you ever touch the implementation.

Module 6 — Lesson 52

Prompt Chaining: Breaking Complex Tasks Into Sequential Claude Calls

The AI Prompting Course

One of the biggest mistakes I made early on was trying to get Claude to do everything in a single prompt. I'd write these massive, 500-word prompts asking it to research a topic, analyze the findings, draft a strategy document, and format it for presentation -- all at once. And the output would be... fine. Not great. Kind of shallow across the board. Like asking one person to cook, serve, and clean up a dinner party simultaneously.

Then I learned about chaining. And it changed how I use AI for basically everything complex.

What Prompt Chaining Actually Is

Prompt chaining is simple in concept: instead of asking Claude to do one big complex thing, you break it into a sequence of smaller, focused tasks. The output of Step 1 becomes the input for Step 2. The output of Step 2 feeds Step 3. And so on.

Each individual prompt is tight. Focused. Clear. And because each step is doing one thing well instead of five things poorly, the quality at every stage is dramatically higher.

Here's the thing -- this isn't just a prompting technique. It's how every good system works. Assembly lines. Software pipelines. Cooking a meal. You don't do everything at once. You do things in order, and each step builds on the last.

A Real Example From My Business

When we were doing competitive analysis for Official AI, I needed to understand our market positioning relative to about fifteen competitors. Here's how I chained it.

Prompt 1 -- Research:

"Here are 15 companies in the AI agent space: [list]. For each one, give me a two-sentence summary of what they do, who they target, and their primary differentiator. Just the facts, no analysis yet."

Prompt 2 -- Categorize:

"Here's the competitive landscape data: [paste output from Prompt 1]. Group these companies into 3-4 categories based on their approach and target market. Name each category and explain why they belong together."

Prompt 3 -- Analyze gaps:

"Here's our competitive landscape grouped into categories: [paste output from Prompt 2]. Our company, Official AI, does [description]. Where are the gaps in this market that nobody is filling? What positioning opportunities exist?"

Prompt 4 -- Strategy:

"Based on this gap analysis: [paste output from Prompt 3], draft a one-page positioning strategy for Official AI. Include our target customer, primary differentiator, and three key messages we should lead with."

Four prompts. Each one clean and focused. The final output was a positioning document that would've taken a consultant a week to produce. And it was actually good because each step had the full attention of the model.

Why Single Prompts Fail on Complex Tasks

When you cram everything into one prompt, you're asking Claude to hold too many objectives in its head at once. It's like asking someone to simultaneously translate a document, fact-check it, and rewrite it in a different tone. They can do each of those things well. They can't do all three at the same time without something suffering.

Claude has the same constraint. Not because it's limited in some technical way, but because when you give it five objectives, it has to balance them against each other. It'll sacrifice depth for breadth. It'll give you a little of everything instead of a lot of anything.

Chaining removes that tradeoff. Each step has one job. Do it well. Move on.

The Three Chain Patterns I Use Most

Linear chain: A leads to B leads to C. This is the most common. Research, then analyze, then draft. Each output feeds the next input directly.

Fan-out chain: One input produces multiple parallel outputs. I use this when I need the same source material turned into different formats. One blog post goes into Claude and comes out as a LinkedIn post, three tweets, and an email -- each in its own focused prompt.

Refinement chain: The same task gets repeated with increasing specificity. Draft 1 is rough. Prompt 2 critiques Draft 1. Prompt 3 rewrites based on the critique. This is how I get writing quality that actually matches my voice -- not on the first pass, but by the third.

The Key Principle

Here's what I want you to remember: if your prompt includes the words "and then" more than once, you should probably be chaining.

"Research competitors and then analyze the gaps and then write a strategy and then format it as a deck." That's four prompts pretending to be one. Break them apart. Let each one breathe. Your outputs will be night-and-day better.

Try This

Take a complex task you've been doing in a single prompt. Something where the output has been okay but not great. Break it into three to five sequential steps. Run each one separately, passing the output forward.

Compare the final result to what you were getting before. The difference will be obvious. And once you see it, you'll never go back to the monolithic-prompt approach for anything complex.

Lesson 52 of 100

Module 6 — Lesson 53

Building Automation Stacks With Claude as the Architect (n8n, Clay, Apify)

The AI Prompting Course

I'm going to be honest about something. I don't think of myself as a technical person. I can't sit down and write a Python script from scratch without help. But I've built automation stacks that run core parts of my business -- lead enrichment, content distribution, competitor monitoring -- and they work. They've been running for months. The secret is that Claude designed every single one of them.

Here's what I mean. Tools like n8n, Clay, and Apify are incredibly powerful. But they have learning curves. Each one has its own logic, its own interface, its own way of thinking about data flows. If you try to learn all three from scratch and then figure out how to connect them, you're looking at weeks of work.

Or you can describe what you want to Claude and let it architect the whole thing for you.

How I Use Claude to Design Automation Stacks

The approach is always the same. I describe the business problem, not the technical solution. I don't say "build me an n8n workflow." I say "here's what I need to happen, and here are the tools I have access to. Design the system."

Let me show you what I mean. When I needed a lead enrichment pipeline, I prompted Claude like this:

"I need to build an automated pipeline that does the following: When a new lead fills out our Typeform, I want to automatically enrich their data -- find their LinkedIn, company info, company size, recent funding. Then score the lead based on criteria I'll define. Then route qualified leads to one Slack channel and unqualified leads to another. I have access to n8n for workflow automation, Clay for enrichment, and my data lives in Airtable. Design the full stack. Tell me exactly which tools handle which steps, what the data flow looks like, and what I need to configure in each tool."

Claude came back with a complete architecture. n8n as the orchestrator, triggered by a Typeform webhook. Clay for the enrichment step, using their LinkedIn and company data enrichment nodes. A scoring function built right into n8n using an IF node. Slack notifications at the end. It even told me which specific Clay enrichment templates to use and how to structure the Airtable base to store the results.

The Stack Design Prompt

I've refined this into a repeatable prompt pattern that works for basically any automation stack:

"I need to automate [business process]. Here's the current manual process: [describe step by step]. Here are the tools I have available: [list tools and what they do]. Here are my constraints: [budget, technical skill level, existing infrastructure]. Design the full automation stack. For each step, tell me: which tool handles it, what the input is, what the output is, and how it connects to the next step. Flag any steps that require custom code vs. no-code configuration."

That last line is important. I always ask Claude to flag what's no-code versus what requires custom code. Because I want to know upfront where I'm going to need help versus where I can configure things myself in a tool's UI.

Real Stack Architectures Claude Has Designed for Me

Competitor monitoring stack: Apify scrapes competitor websites and social profiles on a weekly schedule. Data flows into n8n, which runs it through Claude's API for analysis. Summary gets posted to a dedicated Slack channel every Monday morning. Total setup time: about three hours.

Content distribution stack: When I publish a blog post (detected via RSS in n8n), the content gets sent to Claude's API to generate platform-specific versions. Those get queued in a Buffer-like scheduling system. LinkedIn post goes out same day, Twitter thread goes out next day, email newsletter version goes into our email tool as a draft.

Client onboarding stack: New client signs a contract in PandaDoc. Webhook triggers n8n. Claude generates a personalized onboarding email based on the client's industry and package. Slack notification goes to the account manager. Airtable record gets created with timeline milestones auto-populated.

None of these required me to be a developer. All of them required me to describe the problem clearly to Claude and then follow its architectural guidance.

The Part Nobody Talks About

Here's something I learned the hard way: the first architecture Claude gives you is a starting point, not a final answer. You need to push back. Ask it about failure modes. Ask what happens when the API is down, when the data is malformed, when the enrichment returns empty results.

"This architecture looks good. Now stress-test it. What are the five most likely failure points, and how should I handle each one? Add error handling to the design."

That follow-up prompt is what separates a prototype from a production system. Claude will add retry logic suggestions, fallback paths, error notification steps, and data validation checks that you wouldn't have thought of on your own.

Try This

Pick one manual process in your work that involves data moving between at least two tools. Describe the full process to Claude -- every step, every tool, every input and output. Then ask it to design the automation stack. Don't worry about whether you know how to implement it yet. Just get the architecture. That's step one. The implementation becomes dramatically easier when you have a clear blueprint.

Module 6 — Lesson 54

Turning a Vague Idea Into a PRD in One Conversation

The AI Prompting Course

I've started and scrapped more projects than I can count because I jumped into building before I actually knew what I was building. I'd have a vague idea -- "we should build a dashboard for tracking client health" -- and I'd immediately start talking to a developer about it. Three weeks later we'd have something that technically worked but didn't actually solve the problem. Because I never defined the problem clearly in the first place.

Now I don't start any project without a PRD. A Product Requirements Document. And I can go from "vague idea floating around in my head" to a complete, structured PRD in a single conversation with Claude. Usually takes about twenty minutes.

Why PRDs Matter Even If You're Not a PM

A PRD isn't some corporate artifact that only product managers at big companies need. It's a thinking tool. It forces you to answer the questions you've been avoiding: Who exactly is this for? What specific problem does it solve? How will we know if it's working? What are we NOT building?

Those questions are hard. Which is exactly why most people skip them. They'd rather just start building and figure it out as they go. Sometimes that works. Most of the time it doesn't.

Here's the thing -- Claude is really good at asking those hard questions. Better than most people, honestly. Because it doesn't have the same blind spots you do. It doesn't share your assumptions. It just sees the gaps in your thinking and asks about them.

The Conversation Pattern

Here's exactly how I turn a vague idea into a PRD. I open a new conversation and start with this:

"I have a product idea that's still pretty rough. I want to turn it into a proper PRD by the end of this conversation. Here's what I'm thinking: [describe the idea in whatever messy, incomplete way it exists in your head]. Before you write anything, interview me. Ask me the questions a great product manager would ask to fully scope this out. Go one question at a time so I can think through each answer."

The "one question at a time" part is crucial. If you let Claude ask ten questions at once, you'll give shallow answers to all of them. One at a time forces you to actually think.

When I was scoping out a new feature for Official AI -- a way for users to create custom AI agents from templates -- the conversation went like this. Claude asked me about the target user. Then about the specific pain point. Then about existing solutions they might be using. Then about the user's technical skill level. Then about what success looks like. Then about scope constraints.

Each answer I gave led to a more specific follow-up question. It was like having a really sharp product thinking partner who never gets tired and never lets you hand-wave past the hard parts.

From Interview to Document

After about fifteen minutes of Q&A, I hit Claude with the second prompt:

"Based on everything we just discussed, write a complete PRD. Include: Problem statement, target user persona, user stories (as a [user], I want to [action], so that [outcome]), success metrics, scope (what's in v1, what's explicitly out), technical considerations, and open questions we still need to resolve. Format it so I can paste it directly into a Notion doc."

What comes back is usually 80-90% ready to share with a team. Not because Claude is magic, but because the interview process front-loaded all the thinking. The document is just organizing thoughts that already exist in the conversation.

The Parts That Surprise People

Three things consistently surprise people when they try this.

First, the "what's NOT in scope" section. Claude is great at explicitly stating what you're not building. This is one of the hardest things for founders to do -- we want to build everything. Having Claude explicitly list what's out of scope for v1 creates a forcing function for focus.

Second, the open questions list. Claude will identify things you discussed that still don't have clear answers. "We haven't decided how to handle the case where a user wants to edit a template they've already deployed." Those open questions are gold. They're the landmines you'd have stepped on during development.

Third, the user stories are actually specific. Not the generic "As a user, I want to log in so that I can access my account" stuff. When you've done the interview properly, Claude generates user stories like "As a non-technical marketer, I want to select from pre-built agent templates so that I can deploy an AI assistant without writing any configuration."

The Refinement Step

After I get the first draft of the PRD, I always do one more pass:

"Read this PRD as if you're a skeptical engineer who has to build it. What's unclear? What's missing? What would you push back on? Give me a list of the weaknesses."

This role-shift prompt is powerful. Claude will find ambiguities, undefined terms, conflicting requirements, and missing edge cases. Fix those, and your PRD is ready to ship.

Try This

Think about an idea you've been kicking around. Something you've been meaning to build or propose but haven't gotten around to formalizing. Open Claude and start with the interview prompt above. Let it ask you questions one at a time. Answer honestly, including when you don't know. Then ask for the PRD. You'll have a more complete, more thoughtful product document in twenty minutes than most teams produce in a week of meetings.

Module 6 — Lesson 55

Using Claude to Design Hiring Systems, Sourcing Pipelines, and Evaluation Rubrics

The AI Prompting Course

Hiring is the thing that can make or break a small company, and it's also the thing most founders are terrible at systematizing. I know because I was one of them. For the longest time, my "hiring process" was: post on LinkedIn, read through resumes when I had time, do a vibe-check call, and make a gut decision. Sometimes that worked. A lot of the time it didn't.

The problem wasn't that I'm bad at evaluating people. The problem was that I had no system. No consistent criteria. No structured pipeline. Every hire was a one-off, and that meant every hire was inconsistent.

Claude fixed that for me. Not by doing the hiring -- but by helping me design the system.

Designing the Sourcing Pipeline

When I needed to hire a content strategist for Mudita Studios, I started by asking Claude to design the sourcing strategy:

"I need to hire a content strategist for a small creative studio. Budget is $70-90K. They'll be creating content strategies for our clients, which are mostly early-stage B2B SaaS companies. I want to find candidates who have actual experience in this space, not just marketing generalists. Design a sourcing pipeline -- where should I look, what should the outreach say, and how do I filter for the right people before I ever get on a call?"

Claude broke it down into specific sourcing channels ranked by likelihood of finding the right person. LinkedIn search with specific boolean strings I could copy and paste. Three niche Slack communities where content strategists hang out. A list of Twitter/X accounts to check whose followers might include the right candidates. And a cold outreach template that was casual, specific, and didn't sound like every other recruiter message in someone's inbox.

But the real value was the pre-screening filter. Claude designed a short application with four questions that would immediately separate experienced B2B content strategists from generalists. Questions like "Describe a content strategy you built for a B2B SaaS company with less than $5M ARR. What was the core insight that drove the strategy?" You can't fake that answer.

Building an Evaluation Rubric

This is where most people stop. They source candidates, review resumes, and then wing it in the interview. I used to do this too. Now I have Claude design a scoring rubric before I talk to anyone.

"Based on the role we just defined, create an evaluation rubric I can use during interviews. I want 5-6 criteria, each scored 1-5 with clear descriptions of what a 1, 3, and 5 look like. The criteria should cover both hard skills and soft skills relevant to this specific role. Also give me 2-3 interview questions for each criterion that will actually reveal whether someone meets it."

What Claude produced was a spreadsheet-ready rubric with criteria like "B2B Content Strategy Experience" (where a 5 means "Has independently built and executed content strategies for 3+ B2B SaaS companies, can speak to specific results") and "Client Communication" (where a 5 means "Proactively manages client expectations, translates strategy into language non-marketers understand").

Each criterion had specific interview questions. Not generic stuff like "Tell me about a time you showed leadership." Specific, role-relevant questions that force real answers.

The Full System Design

Here's what the complete hiring system looked like when Claude was done designing it:

  • Stage 1 -- Sourcing: Three channels, custom outreach per channel, with a target of 30 initial candidates
  • Stage 2 -- Application filter: Four screening questions, auto-reject criteria for anyone who can't answer specifically
  • Stage 3 -- Portfolio review: Rubric for evaluating work samples with specific criteria Claude defined
  • Stage 4 -- First interview: 45-minute structured interview using the rubric questions, scored in real-time
  • Stage 5 -- Paid trial project: A small, paid assignment designed to simulate actual work they'd do in the role
  • Stage 6 -- Final interview: Culture fit and logistics, using a separate set of questions Claude helped me define

The paid trial project was Claude's idea, and it was brilliant. Instead of trying to evaluate someone's skills in a 45-minute conversation, you give them a real task and see how they actually work. Claude even designed the trial brief -- a realistic scenario that would take about 4-5 hours and would reveal exactly the skills I cared about.

Why This Beats Gut Feel

I'm not saying gut feel doesn't matter. It does. But gut feel plus a structured system is dramatically more reliable than gut feel alone. The rubric gives you a common language. When you're comparing two candidates, you're not just saying "I liked this one more." You're saying "Candidate A scored 4 on strategic thinking and 3 on client communication. Candidate B scored 3 on strategic thinking and 5 on client communication." That's a real conversation.

Try This

Even if you're not hiring right now, try this exercise: pick any role you might hire for in the next year. Describe it to Claude and ask it to design the full hiring system -- sourcing strategy, screening filter, evaluation rubric, interview questions, and a trial project. You'll end up with a complete hiring playbook you can deploy whenever you need it. And the next time you're scrambling to fill a role, you won't be starting from zero.

Module 6 — Lesson 56

SOPs From Scratch: How to Extract and Document Any Process Using Claude

The AI Prompting Course

I have a confession. For the first year of running Mudita Studios, our standard operating procedures lived entirely in my head. Every process, every workflow, every "here's how we do things" -- all of it was just stuff I knew and would explain to people when they needed it. Which meant I was the bottleneck for literally everything.

I see this all the time with founders and team leads. They know how things should work, but they've never written it down. And the reason they haven't written it down is because documenting processes is tedious, time-consuming, and boring. Nobody starts a company because they love writing SOPs.

Here's the thing -- Claude turns a two-hour documentation task into a fifteen-minute conversation. And the SOPs it helps you create are actually better than what most people write on their own, because it asks the questions you'd skip.

The Extraction Method

The hardest part of writing an SOP isn't the writing. It's the extraction. Getting the process out of your head and into a structured format. Most people try to do this by sitting down and typing it out from memory. That doesn't work well because you skip steps you do unconsciously, you forget edge cases, and you write at a level of detail that's obvious to you but not to someone learning the process.

Instead, I let Claude interview me. Same pattern as the PRD lesson, but applied to process documentation:

"I need to document our process for [specific process]. I'm going to describe it to you in rough, conversational terms. Your job is to listen, ask clarifying questions, and then turn it into a clean, step-by-step SOP that someone new could follow without any prior context. Start by asking me to walk through the process from beginning to end."

Then I just talk through it. Stream of consciousness. "Okay so when a new client signs, first I check the contract to see what package they bought. Then I create their project in ClickUp. Well, actually first I create the Slack channel. No wait -- first I add them to our billing system. Then the Slack channel. Then ClickUp..."

See how messy that is? That's real. That's how processes actually live in people's heads. And Claude is great at taking that mess and turning it into structure.

The Questions Claude Asks

After my initial brain dump, Claude asks follow-ups like:

  • "You mentioned creating a ClickUp project -- what template do you use? What fields do you fill in?"
  • "When you said 'check the contract for the package' -- what are the different packages and does the process change depending on which one?"
  • "Who else is involved? At what point does someone other than you touch this process?"
  • "How long does this process typically take end to end?"
  • "What are the most common things that go wrong, and how do you handle them?"

That last question is gold. Error handling is the thing people always leave out of SOPs. "What do you do when the client's email bounces?" "What if ClickUp is down?" "What if the contract is missing the start date?" Claude surfaces all of these.

The Output Format

I always ask for SOPs in a specific format:

"Write this SOP with the following structure: Purpose (one sentence on why this process exists), Trigger (what initiates this process), Prerequisites (what needs to be true before you start), Steps (numbered, with sub-steps where needed), Decision Points (any if/then logic), Common Issues (what goes wrong and how to fix it), and Owner (who's responsible for each section). Write it at a level where someone in their first week could follow it without asking me any questions."

That "first week" framing is everything. It forces Claude to be explicit about things that seem obvious. Don't write "update the CRM." Write "Open HubSpot, navigate to the Deals pipeline, find the deal by searching the company name, change the status from 'Closed Won' to 'Onboarding', and add a note with the kickoff date."

The Bulk SOP Sprint

Once I figured out this method, I did something I call an SOP Sprint. I set aside one afternoon and documented twelve core processes in about three hours. Client onboarding. Invoice processing. Content publishing. Social media workflow. Sales follow-up. QA review. All of them.

Each one was a fifteen-minute conversation with Claude. I'd describe the process, answer the questions, and get a polished SOP. Then I'd review it, make a few tweaks, and paste it into Notion. Three hours of work replaced what would have been two or three weeks of sporadic documentation.

The team noticed immediately. Questions dropped by about 60%. "How do we do X?" turned into "Let me check the SOP." And when we onboarded a new contractor, we handed them a Notion workspace with every process documented, instead of scheduling five hours of screen-share walkthroughs.

Try This

Pick the one process in your work that you explain to people the most. The thing someone asks you about every week. Open Claude and use the extraction prompt above. Talk through the process messily. Answer Claude's questions. Get the SOP. Then put it somewhere your team can find it.

One SOP, fifteen minutes. Do that once a week and in three months you'll have a complete operations manual for your business. And you'll never have to explain the same process twice again.

Module 6 — Lesson 57

Building Scoring Systems and Trackers by Describing the Logic, Not the Formula

The AI Prompting Course

I needed a lead scoring system. Not a complicated one -- just something that would look at a lead's company size, industry, engagement level, and budget, and spit out a score from 1 to 100 that told my team whether to prioritize them. Simple in concept. But when I sat down to build it in a spreadsheet, I immediately got stuck.

How do you weight company size versus engagement? Is a 500-person company with low engagement better than a 50-person company with high engagement? What about budget? Does that override everything else? I knew the logic intuitively -- I could look at any lead and tell you in two seconds whether they were worth pursuing -- but I couldn't express it as a formula.

So I described it to Claude instead. Not as math. As logic.

Describing Logic in Plain English

Here's the prompt I used:

"I need a lead scoring system. Here's how I think about lead quality: Company size matters a lot -- enterprise companies (500+) are our sweet spot, mid-market (100-500) is good, small companies (under 100) are usually not worth pursuing unless they have a big budget. Industry matters -- SaaS and fintech are our best verticals, healthcare is decent, everything else is lower priority. Engagement matters -- if someone has visited our pricing page, attended a webinar, or replied to an email, they're more serious. Budget is the final filter -- if they've indicated a budget over $50K, that bumps them up significantly. Turn this into a scoring system with a 1-100 scale. Show me the weights, the scoring logic for each factor, and give me the formula I can put in a spreadsheet."

Claude came back with a complete scoring model. Company size weighted at 30%, industry at 20%, engagement at 25%, budget at 25%. Each factor had a sub-scoring scale. For company size: 500+ employees = 10 points, 100-500 = 7 points, 50-100 = 4 points, under 50 = 1 point. Multiply by the weight, sum it up, normalize to 100.

But here's what made it actually useful -- Claude also gave me the spreadsheet formulas. Actual Google Sheets formulas I could paste in. VLOOKUP for the industry scoring, nested IFs for the company size tiers, a weighted sum formula for the final score. I didn't have to translate the logic into formulas. Claude did that translation for me.

Beyond Lead Scoring

I've used this same approach for a bunch of different scoring and tracking systems:

Content performance tracker: I described how I evaluate content -- views matter but engagement matters more, comments are worth more than likes, and shares are worth the most. Claude turned that into a weighted score and gave me the formulas.

Client health score: Response time, project progress, NPS survey results, and payment history all feed into a single number that tells me whether a client relationship is healthy. I described the logic in plain English: "If they're not responding to emails within 48 hours AND their project is behind schedule, that's a red flag. If they're responsive and the project is on track, that's green."

Vendor evaluation rubric: When we were choosing between three different software tools, I described what mattered to us -- price, feature set, integration capability, customer support quality -- and Claude built a comparison scorecard with weighted criteria.

The Key Insight

Here's what I want you to understand: you don't need to think in formulas. You need to think in logic. If you can explain to another person how you make a decision, Claude can turn that explanation into a quantified system.

"I care about X more than Y, unless Z is really high, in which case Z becomes the most important thing." That's messy human logic. And Claude can convert it into clean mathematical logic without you ever needing to understand the math.

I see people all the time who have great intuition about their business but can't turn that intuition into systems. They can eyeball a lead and know if it's good, but they can't build a spreadsheet that does the same thing. The gap isn't intelligence. It's translation. Claude is the translator.

The Iteration Loop

One more thing. Scoring systems aren't set-and-forget. After Claude builds the initial version, I test it against real data. I'll take ten leads I've already qualified manually and run them through the scoring system. If the scores don't match my gut, I go back to Claude:

"I tested the scoring system against real leads. Here are the results: [paste data]. Leads A, D, and G scored much higher than they should have. They're all small companies with high engagement but low budgets. I think we're overweighting engagement relative to budget. Adjust the weights and show me the updated model."

Two or three iterations of this and you have a scoring system that actually reflects your judgment. You've essentially codified your intuition.

Try This

Think about a decision you make regularly that involves weighing multiple factors. Lead quality, content prioritization, vendor selection, task prioritization -- anything where you're balancing competing criteria. Describe your logic to Claude in plain English. Don't worry about being precise. Just explain how you think about it. Then ask Claude to turn it into a scored system with formulas you can use. You'll be amazed at how quickly intuitive logic becomes a usable tool.

Lesson 57 of 100

Module 6 — Lesson 58

Email Sequence Design: Nurture Flows, Waitlist Reactivation, Cold Outbound

The AI Prompting Course

I used to write email sequences one email at a time. I'd write the first email, feel good about it, send it, then two days later sit down to write the follow-up with no real plan for how the whole sequence fit together. The result was emails that individually sounded fine but collectively felt disjointed. Like chapters from different books stitched together.

Now I design entire sequences in one conversation with Claude. Not just the copy -- the strategy. The logic. The narrative arc. The timing. Everything.

The Difference Between Writing Emails and Designing Sequences

Most people use AI to write individual emails. That's fine for one-offs. But a sequence is a system, not a collection of separate messages. Each email has to know what came before it and set up what comes next. The first email needs to establish something. The second needs to build on it. The third needs to shift the energy. There's a progression.

When you ask Claude to "write a 5-email nurture sequence," you get five decent but disconnected emails. When you ask Claude to design the sequence as a strategic system, you get something that actually converts.

Here's how I approach it:

"I need to design a 5-email nurture sequence for people who signed up for our waitlist but haven't converted to a paid account. Here's the context: Our product is [description]. The average time from waitlist signup to conversion is 14 days. The main reasons people don't convert are: they forget about us, they don't understand our key differentiator, or they're waiting for a specific feature. Design the sequence strategy first -- what's the goal of each email, what's the emotional arc, what's the timing between emails. Don't write the copy yet. Just the blueprint."

That last line is important. Don't write the copy yet. Get the strategy right first. Once the blueprint is solid, the copy practically writes itself.

What a Good Blueprint Looks Like

When I did this for Official AI's waitlist reactivation, Claude came back with a sequence blueprint that looked like this:

Email 1 (Day 0 -- The Reminder): Goal is simply to get back on their radar. Tone is casual and low-pressure. Subject line should create curiosity about what's new, not guilt about not converting. Key message: here's what's happened since you signed up.

Email 2 (Day 3 -- The Value Drop): Goal is to demonstrate the key differentiator with a specific use case. Include a mini case study or example. Tone shifts from casual to slightly more authoritative. They should think "oh, I didn't realize it could do that."

Email 3 (Day 7 -- The Objection Handler): Directly address the top three reasons people don't convert. Frame them as "things we hear a lot" rather than "your objections." Each one gets a 2-sentence response. This is the strategic heart of the sequence.

Email 4 (Day 10 -- Social Proof): Stories from real users. Not testimonials -- stories. "Sarah was on the waitlist for two weeks because she wasn't sure if..." Specificity is everything. People don't trust "Our users love it." They trust "Marcus cut his reporting time from 4 hours to 20 minutes."

Email 5 (Day 14 -- The Gentle Close): Clear CTA with a reason to act now. Not a fake countdown timer. A real reason -- a feature launching next week, a pricing change, a limited cohort. Tone is direct but not pushy.

See how each email has a specific job? And how the emotional arc progresses from "hey, remember us?" to "here's why this matters" to "here's what's stopping you" to "here's proof" to "here's why now"? That's a sequence. Not five emails -- a journey.

Designing Cold Outbound

Cold email is a different beast. The sequence is shorter, the stakes are higher per email, and the strategy is fundamentally different. Here's how I prompt it:

"Design a 3-email cold outbound sequence for reaching VPs of Marketing at B2B SaaS companies (50-200 employees). We're selling content strategy services. The first email needs to earn the right to a response -- no pitching. The second is a follow-up with a specific insight about their company. The third is a breakup email. Give me the strategy for each, the timing, and then write the copy. Each email should be under 100 words. No corporate language. Write like a human texting a colleague."

The constraint -- under 100 words, no corporate language -- is where most cold email copy fails. People write 300-word essays full of "I hope this email finds you well" and "I'd love to explore potential synergies." Nobody reads that. Short, specific, human. Claude is good at this when you tell it to be.

The Testing Framework

After Claude designs the sequence, I always ask for the testing plan:

"What should I A/B test in this sequence? Give me the highest-impact variables to test first, and what I should measure for each one."

Claude usually identifies subject line variations for the first email, the CTA format for the close, and the length/format of the social proof email as the highest-leverage tests. It'll even draft the A/B variants for you.

Try This

Pick a sequence you need -- nurture, reactivation, onboarding, cold outbound, whatever is most relevant to your business right now. Use the blueprint-first approach: describe the audience, the context, and the goal, and ask Claude to design the strategy before writing any copy. Review the blueprint, adjust if needed, and then ask for the copy. You'll end up with a sequence that actually tells a coherent story instead of five random emails in a trenchcoat.

Module 6 — Lesson 59

Designing a PLG Onboarding Flow Using Claude as the UX Strategist

The AI Prompting Course

Product-led growth lives or dies in the first five minutes of a user's experience. I learned this the hard way with Official AI. We had solid inbound traffic, decent signup numbers, and then a cliff. People would create an account and just... disappear. Activation rate was dismal. Not because the product was bad, but because the onboarding was.

We didn't have the budget for a UX researcher or a dedicated product designer at that stage. What we had was Claude and a willingness to be honest about what was broken. So I used Claude as our UX strategist, and the results were legitimately transformative.

The Diagnostic Prompt

Before you can fix onboarding, you need to understand where it's failing. I started here:

"I'm going to describe our product's current onboarding flow step by step. After I'm done, I want you to analyze it through the lens of PLG best practices and identify the likely drop-off points. Here's the flow: [User signs up with email > sees a blank dashboard > there's a 'Get Started' button that opens a 4-step setup wizard > Step 1 asks them to connect their data source > Step 2 asks them to invite team members > Step 3 asks them to configure preferences > Step 4 shows a tutorial video]. Our activation metric is 'user creates their first report.' Most users drop off before completing the setup wizard."

Claude's analysis was sharp. It identified three problems immediately. First, the setup wizard front-loaded the hardest step (connecting a data source) before the user had any reason to care. Second, asking users to invite team members during initial onboarding is a conversion killer -- they haven't even seen value yet, why would they invite others? Third, the tutorial video at the end was passive. Users need to do something, not watch something.

These insights weren't revolutionary. Any experienced UX person would have spotted them. But I didn't have that person on my team, and Claude gave me the same analysis in three minutes.

Redesigning the Flow

Next, I asked Claude to redesign the onboarding from scratch:

"Redesign our onboarding flow with these goals: Get the user to their first 'aha moment' (seeing a report with their data) as fast as possible. Remove any step that doesn't directly contribute to that moment. Use progressive disclosure -- don't ask for everything upfront. Show me the new flow step by step, and for each step explain why it's there and what happens if the user tries to skip it."

Claude's redesigned flow was fundamentally different. Instead of a wizard, it used what it called "guided action" -- dropping the user into the product with a single, clear first step.

Step 1: User signs up and immediately sees a sample report built with demo data. They can click around, interact with it, and understand what the product does. This is the aha moment -- delivered in thirty seconds, with zero setup.

Step 2: A banner at the top says "This is demo data. Connect your own data to see real insights." One-click data source connection. The hardest step is now motivated -- they've already seen the value, so they want their own data.

Step 3: Once connected, the system auto-generates their first report. No configuration needed. Sensible defaults for everything. A tooltip says "You can customize this later."

Step 4: After the first report is generated, a subtle prompt suggests inviting a team member to collaborate. Not during setup. After they've already activated.

Every step was justified. Every skip path was defined. And the whole flow was designed around one principle: deliver value before asking for investment.

Getting the Details Right

After the high-level flow, I drilled into the details:

"For each step in this new onboarding flow, give me: the exact copy for the headline, the CTA button text, the microcopy for any explanatory text, and the fallback if the user gets stuck or confused. Write it in a tone that's friendly and confident, not corporate or hand-holdy."

Claude generated microcopy that was specific and natural. Instead of "Welcome to [Product]! Let's get started on your journey," it wrote "Here's what your data could look like. This is a sample -- we'll use your real data in a minute." Direct. Clear. No fluff.

For the CTA buttons, instead of generic "Continue" or "Next Step," Claude used action-specific copy: "Connect my data," "Build my first report," "Share with my team." Each button tells the user exactly what's about to happen.

The Onboarding Email Layer

Onboarding isn't just the in-app experience. It's also the emails that follow. I asked Claude to design the companion email sequence:

"Design a 3-email onboarding sequence that supports the in-app flow. Email 1 goes out immediately after signup. Email 2 goes out if the user hasn't connected their data source within 24 hours. Email 3 goes out after they've created their first report. Each email has one job. Tell me what that job is and draft the copy."

Email 1's job: reinforce the aha moment and provide a direct link back to the sample report. Email 2's job: address the most common reasons people don't connect their data (security concerns, not sure which data source, don't have login credentials handy) and solve each one. Email 3's job: show them one advanced feature that will make them think "okay, this is going to be part of my workflow."

Try This

If you have any kind of product with a signup flow, describe the current onboarding experience to Claude step by step. Be honest about where users drop off. Then ask Claude to redesign it with one goal: get users to the aha moment as fast as possible. The redesigned flow won't be perfect -- you'll need to test and iterate. But it'll be a dramatically better starting point than what most teams come up with in a conference room with a whiteboard.

Module 6 — Lesson 60

Using Claude to Map a Full Org Structure From Messy Notes and Voice Memos

The AI Prompting Course

Last year I was having coffee with a founder friend who was about to close a $3M seed round. He had twelve people on the team, was about to hire ten more, and when I asked him "What does your org structure look like?" he opened the Notes app on his phone and showed me something that looked like a ransom note. Names scattered everywhere. Arrows drawn with text. Half-formed thoughts about who should report to whom. A voice memo transcription that said "maybe Sarah leads marketing but also does some BD?"

That's not unusual. That's how most early-stage org structures exist -- as a mess of thoughts spread across voice memos, Slack messages, whiteboard photos, and the founder's head. The information exists. It's just scattered, contradictory, and unstructured.

This is one of my favorite uses of Claude. Taking that raw, messy input and turning it into a clear, structured org chart with defined roles, reporting lines, and identified gaps.

The Brain Dump Approach

Here's the exact process I use. I dump everything -- and I mean everything -- into one prompt. Voice memo transcriptions, notes, half-baked ideas, all of it. The messier the better. Claude can handle messy input far better than you'd expect.

"I'm going to paste in a bunch of messy notes about our team structure. Some of this is from voice memos, some from Slack messages, some from my own notes. It's contradictory in places and incomplete. I need you to make sense of it and produce a clean org chart. Here's everything I have: [paste in all the messy notes] From this, give me: 1) A clean org chart showing reporting lines, 2) Each person's role and primary responsibilities, 3) Any contradictions you spotted in my notes (places where I said conflicting things), 4) Gaps -- roles or functions that nobody seems to own, 5) Your recommendation for how to resolve the contradictions and fill the gaps."

When I did this for my own team at Mudita Studios, I pasted in about three pages of scattered notes. Things like "Ben handles product stuff and also some client calls" and "we need someone on growth but idk if that's full-time" and a voice memo that said "I keep doing the bookkeeping myself, this is stupid, someone else should own this."

What Claude Produces

From that mess, Claude produced a structured org chart with four layers. It identified that I had two people with overlapping responsibilities in the client management space (which I'd somehow missed). It flagged that nobody owned growth or ops. It pointed out that I'd described Ben as both "leading product" and "supporting sales" in different notes, and asked me which was his primary function.

The contradictions list was the most valuable part. When you're building an org structure from scattered notes over weeks and months, you inevitably contradict yourself. You give someone a responsibility in one note and give it to someone else in another note. You describe a role one way in a voice memo and a different way in a Slack message. Claude catches all of that.

The Gap Analysis

After the initial org chart, I always follow up with this:

"Based on this org structure and the fact that we're a [stage] company doing [revenue/activity level], what functions are critically under-resourced? What's going to break first as we grow? Prioritize the gaps by urgency and give me a rough job description for the most critical hire."

This is where Claude acts as a fractional COO for fifteen minutes. It looks at the structure, identifies that you have no dedicated ops person and your founder is doing three different jobs, and tells you exactly which hire will unlock the most capacity. For my company, it identified that hiring an ops generalist would free up about fifteen hours of my time per week -- time that was being spent on bookkeeping, vendor management, and process documentation that I had no business doing.

Going Deeper: Role Definition

Once the org chart is clean, I use Claude to define each role properly:

"For each role on this org chart, create a role card with: Title, Reports to, Primary responsibilities (3-5 bullet points), Key metrics they own, Decision authority (what can they decide without approval), and Collaboration touchpoints (who they need to work with regularly). Keep each card concise -- this should fit on one page per role."

These role cards become the foundation for everything. Hiring, performance reviews, onboarding, conflict resolution. When someone asks "Whose job is this?" you have an answer. When two people are stepping on each other's toes, you can point to the role cards and clarify ownership. When you're writing a job description, you already have the core of it done.

The Voice Memo Trick

Here's a specific trick I love. If your org thinking lives in voice memos -- which it does for a lot of founders -- record a five-minute stream of consciousness about your team structure. Use your phone's transcription feature or a tool like Otter to turn it into text. Then paste the raw transcription into Claude. Don't clean it up. Don't edit it. The "um"s and tangents and self-corrections are actually useful signal for Claude to understand what you're uncertain about.

I did this once with a seven-minute voice memo where I was talking through the team while driving. It was full of "wait, actually" and "no, that's not right" and "hmm, I need to think about this more." Claude pulled out the key decisions, flagged the parts where I was clearly undecided, and turned it into a structured org plan with a separate section called "Open questions from your notes" that listed the five things I was still mulling over.

Try This

Open your Notes app, your Slack DMs with yourself, your voice memos -- wherever you've been storing thoughts about your team or organization structure. Collect all of it into one messy document. Don't organize it. Just paste it all into Claude with the brain dump prompt above. Let Claude find the structure in your chaos. You'll get a clean org chart, a list of contradictions to resolve, and a gap analysis that tells you exactly what's missing. Twenty minutes of work that would take days to do manually. And the clarity you get from seeing your messy thoughts turned into a clean structure is genuinely hard to describe until you experience it.

Module 7 — Lesson 61

What Vibe Coding Actually Is — And Why Non-Technical Founders Can Ship Real Products

The AI Prompting Course

I need to tell you something that would have sounded insane three years ago. I'm a non-technical founder and I've shipped multiple production applications. Real apps. With databases, authentication, APIs, deployment pipelines. Apps that real users pay money to use. And I've done it without learning to code in any traditional sense.

That's vibe coding. And I want to be very precise about what I mean by that, because the term gets thrown around a lot and most people get it wrong.

What Vibe Coding Actually Is

Vibe coding is not asking ChatGPT to write you a script and then copy-pasting it into a file. That's just using AI as a fancier Stack Overflow. Vibe coding is sitting down with an AI coding agent — Claude Code, Cursor, Lovable, Replit Agent — and building a complete application through conversation. You describe what you want. The agent writes the code, creates the files, sets up the structure. You test it, describe what's wrong or what's next, and the agent keeps building.

You are the product person, the architect, the QA tester, and the project manager. The AI is the engineer. And that division of labor actually works.

Why This Isn't a Gimmick

Here's the thing. When I built CaseSight — a legal AI tool that searches through hundreds of personal injury cases with semantic search — I didn't write the Supabase schema by hand. I didn't write the Next.js components by hand. I described what I needed, reviewed what Claude built, tested it, and iterated. The app works. It has pgvector embeddings, a data pipeline, a clean UI. Real users use it.

Could a senior engineer have built it better? In some ways, sure. Would it have taken them weeks instead of a weekend? Absolutely. And here's what matters: that app exists. It's live. It's generating value. If I had waited until I "learned to code properly," it still wouldn't exist.

That's the fundamental argument for vibe coding. The best product is the one that ships.

What You Actually Need to Know

I'm not going to pretend you need zero knowledge. You need to understand concepts, even if you can't implement them. Here's my honest list of what's required:

  • What a database is and roughly how it works. Tables, rows, columns. Relationships between tables. You don't need to write SQL from scratch, but you need to know what a foreign key is when Claude mentions one.
  • What an API is. One system talking to another. Sending requests, getting responses. You don't need to build one from memory. You need to understand the concept.
  • What frontend vs. backend means. What the user sees vs. what happens on the server. This distinction matters when you're describing bugs or features.
  • How to read an error message. Not fix it — read it. Copy it, paste it to Claude, and say "what does this mean and how do I fix it."

That's genuinely it. Everything else — the specific syntax, the framework conventions, the deployment configuration — that's what the AI handles.

The Real Skill Is Product Thinking

I see this all the time with technical founders vs. non-technical founders using vibe coding. The technical founders often over-engineer things. They want perfect architecture from day one. They debate whether to use Redux or Zustand for state management. They refactor before they ship.

Non-technical founders using vibe coding? They ship ugly MVPs in a weekend. They test with real users on Monday. They iterate based on actual feedback. They move on to the next feature or kill the project quickly. The speed advantage is real, and it comes from not knowing enough to be precious about the code.

Your actual competitive advantage as a non-technical founder doing vibe coding is that you think about the product, not the code. You think about what the user needs, what the screen should show, what the workflow should feel like. And then you describe that in plain English to an AI that turns it into working software.

What Vibe Coding Can't Do (Yet)

I want to be honest about the limitations, because overselling this helps nobody.

Vibe coding struggles with highly complex, interconnected systems. If you're building something with dozens of microservices that all need to talk to each other with complex state management — that's still hard. The AI can lose track of the full picture in a big codebase.

It also struggles with performance optimization at scale. If you need to handle 100,000 concurrent users, you need someone who really understands infrastructure. Vibe-coded apps work great for the first few thousand users. After that, you probably need a real engineer to audit and optimize.

And it's not great for anything safety-critical. Medical devices, financial trading systems, anything where a bug has serious consequences — don't vibe code that.

But for SaaS MVPs, internal tools, data pipelines, AI-powered features, marketing sites, dashboards? Vibe coding is genuinely faster than traditional development for a founder who knows what they want to build.

Try This

Think of one tool or app you've wished existed for your business. Not a massive platform — something small. A dashboard that shows your key metrics. A form that collects data and stores it somewhere useful. A tool that takes an input, processes it through an AI, and gives back a formatted output.

Write a one-paragraph description of what it does. Be specific about who uses it, what they see on the screen, and what happens when they click the main button. That paragraph is the seed for everything we're going to build in this module.

Lesson 61 of 100

Module 7 — Lesson 62

How to Write a Prompt for a Coding Agent (Claude Code, Cursor, Lovable)

The AI Prompting Course

Prompting a coding agent is fundamentally different from prompting a chatbot. When you're talking to Claude in a normal conversation, you're asking for text. When you're talking to Claude Code or Cursor, you're asking it to create files, modify code, run commands, and build real software on your machine. The stakes are different and the prompting style needs to be different too.

I learned this the hard way. Early on, I'd give Cursor the same kind of vague prompts I'd give a chatbot, and I'd end up with a mess — files everywhere, conflicting code, half-built features that broke other features. It took me a few painful weekends to figure out how to actually communicate with a coding agent.

The Anatomy of a Good Coding Prompt

Every good coding prompt has four parts. You don't always need all four, but when things go wrong, it's usually because one of them is missing.

1. What exists right now. The agent needs to know the current state. What's already built? What files are relevant? What's the current behavior? If you're starting from scratch, say so. If you're modifying something, describe what's there.

2. What you want to happen. Be specific about the end state, not the implementation. "I want users to be able to filter the case list by date range and case type" is better than "add some filter functionality."

3. What the constraints are. What should it NOT do? What existing functionality should it leave alone? What libraries or patterns are you already using that it should stick with?

4. What success looks like. How will you test this? What should you see on the screen when it's working? This helps the agent aim for a concrete outcome instead of a theoretical one.

Real Prompts I've Actually Used

Let me show you what I mean with prompts from actual projects I've built.

Bad prompt:

"Add authentication to my app."

This gave me a custom auth system built from scratch with password hashing and session management — when I was already using Supabase, which has auth built in. The agent didn't know the context, so it made assumptions.

Good prompt:

"I'm using Supabase for my backend. Add Supabase Auth to this Next.js app. Use email/password sign-up and login. Create a login page at /login and a signup page at /signup. After successful login, redirect to /dashboard. Protect the /dashboard route so unauthenticated users get redirected to /login. Use the Supabase client that's already initialized in lib/supabase.ts."

See the difference? Same task. But the second prompt tells the agent what stack you're using, what pages to create, what the user flow should be, and what existing code to build on.

The "Build Me a..." Pattern

Here's a prompt pattern I use constantly when starting a new feature:

"Build me a [thing] that [does what]. It should [user-facing behavior]. Use [specific technologies/libraries]. The data should [how data flows]. When the user [action], they should see [result]. Don't modify [existing things to leave alone]."

Example from when I was building a data pipeline:

"Build me a scraper that pulls personal injury case data from [source]. It should extract the case name, date, jurisdiction, verdict amount, and case summary. Use Cheerio for parsing. Store the results in my Supabase table called 'cases' — the schema already exists. Run it as a Node script I can call from the command line. Don't touch any of the existing API routes or frontend code."

That last line — "don't touch any of the existing API routes or frontend code" — is critical. Coding agents love to be helpful, and sometimes that means they'll "improve" things you didn't ask them to touch. Setting boundaries upfront saves you from debugging phantom issues.

When to Use Short Prompts vs. Long Prompts

Not every prompt needs to be a paragraph. Here's my rule of thumb:

  • Short prompts work when you're making small, isolated changes. "Change the button color from blue to green on the landing page." "Fix the typo in the footer — it says 'Conact Us' instead of 'Contact Us'." These are surgical. The agent can't really go wrong.
  • Medium prompts work for adding a single feature or component. A new page, a new API endpoint, a new UI element. Include what it should do and what it connects to.
  • Long prompts are for anything that touches multiple files or has complex logic. Authentication flows, data pipelines, anything with multiple steps or conditional behavior. Take the time to be detailed. It pays off.

The Follow-Up Loop

Here's something nobody talks about: vibe coding is not "one prompt and done." It's a conversation. Your first prompt gets you maybe 70-80% of the way there. Then you test it, see what's off, and follow up.

The follow-up prompts are where the real skill is. They sound like this:

"This is mostly working, but the date filter isn't resetting when I click 'Clear All.' Also, the filter dropdown is overlapping with the header on mobile. Can you fix both of those?"
"The login flow works, but after signup it's showing a blank page for about two seconds before redirecting. Can you add a loading state and make the redirect happen faster?"

Notice the pattern? Describe what's happening, describe what should be happening, and let the agent figure out the fix. You're the QA tester. The AI is the developer. That's the loop.

Try This

Take that app idea from the last lesson. Write the first prompt you'd give a coding agent to start building it. Use the four-part structure: what exists now (nothing, you're starting fresh), what you want it to do, what the constraints are, and what success looks like when you test it. Be specific enough that a developer who's never heard of your idea could start building from your description alone.
