Module 7 — Lesson 63

The One-Session App Build: What's Realistic and How to Scope It

The AI Prompting Course

There's a particular kind of magic to building something in a single session. You sit down on a Saturday morning, you have an idea, and by the afternoon you have a working app. I've done this multiple times and it never stops feeling a little unreal.

But here's what nobody tells you: the apps that actually get finished in one session aren't the ambitious ones. They're the ruthlessly scoped ones. The difference between a one-session success and a one-session disaster is entirely about what you decide to build before you start.

What You Can Actually Build in 3-6 Hours

Let me give you a realistic picture based on what I've actually shipped in single sessions:

  • A single-purpose tool with one main feature. A calculator, a generator, a converter. One input, one process, one output. Think "paste your job description, get interview questions tailored to it."
  • A CRUD app with a simple database. Create, read, update, delete. A contact manager, a project tracker, an inventory list. Basic forms, a table or list view, maybe a detail page.
  • A landing page with a functional backend. A waitlist page that actually stores signups in a database. A lead magnet page that collects emails. A one-page site with a working contact form.
  • An AI-powered feature wrapper. A tool that takes user input, sends it to the Claude API, and displays a formatted response. This is actually the fastest thing to build and often the most valuable.

What you can NOT build in one session: anything with user accounts AND billing AND a dashboard AND multiple user roles AND email notifications. I see people try this all the time. They sit down to build "a full SaaS" in an afternoon. They end up with a half-built auth system and nothing else to show for it.

The Scoping Conversation

Before I write a single prompt to a coding agent, I have a scoping conversation with Claude in regular chat mode. It sounds like this:

"I want to build a tool where users can paste in a legal case description and get back a summary of similar cases with verdict amounts. I have about 4 hours. I'm going to use Next.js and Supabase. What's a realistic MVP I can finish in one session? What should I cut to keep scope tight?"

Claude is excellent at this. It'll tell you which features are essential and which are scope creep. It'll suggest what to build manually vs. what to use a library for. It'll give you a build order so you're not jumping around between features.

This pre-planning conversation usually takes 10-15 minutes and saves you 2-3 hours of going in circles.

The Build Order That Actually Works

I've settled on a build order through painful trial and error. Every one-session build I do now follows this sequence:

Step 1: Get the data layer working first. Set up your database table. Make sure you can write to it and read from it. Don't touch the UI yet. This is the foundation and if it's broken, nothing else matters.

Step 2: Build the simplest possible UI. Not pretty — functional. A form that submits data. A page that displays data. Ugly is fine. You're proving the concept works end to end.

Step 3: Add the core logic. If it's an AI feature, wire up the API call. If it's a data processing tool, build the transform. This is the "does the thing actually do the thing" step.

Step 4: Make it not ugly. Only after everything works do you touch styling. This is where people waste enormous amounts of time if they start here. I've watched people spend two hours getting the landing page gradient perfect while the form doesn't submit.

Step 5: Deploy. Get it live. Even if it's rough. A deployed ugly app is infinitely more useful than a beautiful localhost demo that nobody else can see.

The Scope Creep Trap

Here's the thing about building with AI — it makes scope creep feel free. "Oh, it would be cool to also add a filter here." "Let me just quickly add dark mode." "What if users could export to PDF?"

Each of those requests feels small. But each one introduces potential bugs, increases complexity, and extends your session. The AI can build each feature quickly, but the testing and debugging adds up. Before you know it, you've been "quickly adding" things for three hours and your core feature is still broken.

My rule: write down every "nice to have" idea that pops up during the build. Don't implement any of them until the core feature is deployed and working. Most of them won't matter anyway once you get real user feedback.

A Real Example

Last month I built a tool for generating ad copy variations. Here was my scope:

  • One page
  • A text area for the product description
  • A dropdown for the ad platform (Meta, Google, LinkedIn)
  • A button that sends it to the Claude API
  • Display 5 ad copy variations with headlines and body text
  • A "copy" button next to each one

That's it. No user accounts. No saving history. No team sharing. No analytics on which variations perform best. Just the core value: paste description, get ad copy.

Built it in about 3 hours. Deployed to Vercel. Started using it immediately for client work. Later — weeks later — I went back and added the ability to save favorites. But only after I confirmed the core tool was actually useful.

Try This

Take your app idea and apply the brutal scoping filter. Write down every feature you want. Then cross off everything except the one core action — the single thing the user does that delivers value. That's your one-session build. Everything else is version 2.

Lesson 63 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 7 — Lesson 64

Tech Stack Selection by Describing Your Problem, Not Your Tech Knowledge

The AI Prompting Course

One of the most paralyzing moments for a non-technical founder is when someone asks "What's your tech stack?" and you realize you have no idea what you should be using. Should it be React or Vue? Postgres or MongoDB? AWS or Vercel? The options feel infinite and every blog post recommends something different.

Here's what I've learned: you don't pick a tech stack by comparing technologies. You pick it by describing your problem to Claude and letting it recommend the right tools based on what you're actually building.

How I Used to Do It (Wrong)

When I first started building with AI, I'd go down rabbit holes. I'd read articles about "Best frameworks in 2025." I'd watch YouTube comparisons of Next.js vs. Remix vs. SvelteKit. I'd spend hours researching before writing a single line of code. And then I'd pick something based on which community seemed most active on Twitter, which is genuinely one of the worst ways to make a technical decision.

The problem with this approach is that you're evaluating technologies without understanding what matters for your specific use case. It's like reading every car review ever written when you just need something that fits a car seat and gets good gas mileage.

The Prompt That Changed Everything

Now I start every project with a prompt like this:

"I'm building a tool that lets users search through a database of legal cases using natural language. They type something like 'slip and fall in a grocery store' and get back the most similar cases ranked by relevance, with verdict amounts. I'll have around 500-1,000 cases to start, growing to maybe 10,000. It needs to be a web app, it needs to be fast, and I need to be able to deploy it cheaply. I'm a non-technical founder using AI coding tools — I need a stack that's well-documented and that Claude/Cursor can work with easily. What should I use and why?"

Notice what's in that prompt. The product description. The data scale. The user experience requirements. The deployment constraints. The budget reality. And critically — the fact that I'm building with AI tools, which means I need a stack that AI agents are good at generating code for.

That last part matters more than people realize. Some frameworks have way more training data in AI models than others. Next.js, React, Supabase, Tailwind — Claude has seen millions of examples of these. Pick something obscure and the AI is going to hallucinate more, produce buggier code, and slow you down.

The Stack That Works for 90% of Projects

After building a dozen projects this way, I've converged on a default stack. Not because it's the theoretical best for every situation, but because it's the fastest to build with when you're using AI coding tools:

  • Next.js for the frontend and API routes. It handles both the website and the backend logic in one project. The file-based routing means you just create a file and you have a new page. Claude is incredibly good at writing Next.js code.
  • Supabase for the database and auth. It's Postgres under the hood, which means it's powerful and real. But it has a dashboard you can actually see and click around in. When something goes wrong with your data, you can go look at it in a table view like a spreadsheet.
  • Tailwind CSS for styling. I know designers have opinions about this. But when you're vibe coding, utility classes are a gift. You can describe styling in plain English — "make it a rounded card with a shadow and some padding" — and Claude outputs Tailwind classes that just work.
  • Vercel for deployment. Push your code, it's live. That's it. The entire deployment process is essentially one command.

This isn't the stack for everyone. But it's the stack I recommend for non-technical founders who want to ship fast with AI tools. The ecosystem is deep, the documentation is good, and the AI models have been trained on enormous amounts of code in this stack.

When to Deviate

There are real reasons to pick different tools:

If your app is mostly a mobile experience, consider React Native or Flutter instead. But know that this makes vibe coding harder — mobile development has more edge cases and platform-specific gotchas.

If you're processing large amounts of data, you might need Python on the backend. Claude is excellent at writing Python, and something like FastAPI + a Postgres database is a solid combo for data-heavy applications.

If you need real-time features, like a chat app or live collaboration, Supabase has real-time subscriptions built in, so the default stack still works. But if you need something more complex, you might look at Socket.io or Convex.

The key insight is: describe your specific needs and let Claude recommend. Don't pick tools based on hype cycles.

The Question to Ask Yourself

When someone recommends a technology to you, ask this: "Does Claude generate good code for this? Can I find ten examples of tutorials building similar things with this stack?" If the answer to both is yes, it's a safe choice. If the answer is no, you're going to spend more time debugging AI-generated code than building your product.

Try This

Open Claude and describe your project — not in technical terms, but in product terms. What does it do? Who uses it? How big is your data? What's your budget for hosting? How important is speed? Then ask for a tech stack recommendation and ask Claude to explain each choice in one sentence. You'll end up with a clear, justified technical foundation without having to understand any of the underlying technology debates.

Module 7 — Lesson 65

Supabase + Next.js + Claude API: The Modern AI App Starter Kit

The AI Prompting Course

This is the lesson where we get concrete. I'm going to walk you through exactly how I set up every new AI-powered app, using the same stack I've shipped real products with. Supabase for the database, Next.js for the app, and the Claude API for the intelligence layer. These three pieces snap together like Lego blocks, and once you understand the pattern, you can build almost anything.

Why These Three

Let me be specific about why this combination works so well for vibe coding.

Supabase gives you a full Postgres database with a visual dashboard. This matters when you're not writing SQL by hand. You can see your tables, browse your data, edit rows directly. When something goes wrong, you're not staring at a command line — you're looking at a spreadsheet-like interface. It also gives you auth, file storage, and real-time subscriptions out of the box. One service instead of five.

Next.js lets you build the frontend AND the API in one project. Your pages and your server-side logic live in the same codebase. This is huge for vibe coding because it means Claude is working in one context instead of jumping between a frontend repo and a backend repo. Less context switching for the AI means fewer bugs.

The Claude API is what makes your app actually intelligent. You send it text, it sends back intelligent responses. The API is clean, well-documented, and Claude is obviously very good at writing code that calls its own API.

The Setup Prompt

Here's the exact prompt I use to scaffold a new project. I've refined this over a dozen builds:

"Create a new Next.js app with the following setup: Use the App Router. Install and configure Supabase client using @supabase/supabase-js — create a lib/supabase.ts file that initializes the client using environment variables NEXT_PUBLIC_SUPABASE_URL and NEXT_PUBLIC_SUPABASE_ANON_KEY. Install the Anthropic SDK and create a lib/claude.ts that initializes the client using ANTHROPIC_API_KEY. Set up a basic layout with a header, main content area, and footer. Use Tailwind for styling. Create a .env.local.example file showing all the required environment variables. Give me a clean, minimal starting point I can build on."

This gives me a project where the plumbing is done. The database client is ready. The AI client is ready. The styling system is ready. I'm not wasting the first hour on boilerplate.

The First Feature Pattern

Once the scaffold is up, I always build the same first feature: a simple form that sends user input to Claude and displays the response. This proves the entire chain works — frontend to API to Claude and back.

"Add a page at /generate. It should have a text area where the user types a prompt, and a 'Generate' button. When clicked, it sends the text to an API route at /api/generate. That API route calls the Claude API with the user's input, using claude-sonnet-4-20250514 as the model. Stream the response back to the frontend and display it below the form in a nicely formatted div. Show a loading spinner while waiting. Handle errors gracefully — if the API call fails, show a user-friendly error message."

Once this works, I know everything is wired up correctly. The API key is valid, the routing works, the streaming works. Every subsequent feature builds on this foundation.

Adding the Database Layer

After the AI feature works, I add persistence. The pattern is always the same:

"I want to save each generation to the database. Create a Supabase table called 'generations' with columns: id (uuid, primary key, auto-generated), user_input (text), ai_output (text), created_at (timestamp with timezone, default now). After the Claude API returns a response in the /api/generate route, save the input and output to this table. Add a /history page that fetches all saved generations from Supabase and displays them in reverse chronological order."

Now I have an AI app that remembers things. Users can go back and see what they've generated before. And I have data I can analyze to understand how people are actually using the tool.

The Environment Variables Setup

One thing that trips people up: you need to actually get these API keys and put them in the right place. Here's the plain-English version:

  • Go to supabase.com, create a project, and grab your project URL and anon key from the settings page. They're right there in the dashboard.
  • Go to console.anthropic.com, create an API key. You'll need to add credits — even $5 is enough to build and test an entire app.
  • Put all of these in a file called .env.local in your project root. This file is gitignored by default, so your keys won't end up on GitHub.

If any of this is confusing, literally paste the above paragraph to Claude and say "walk me through this step by step for a Mac." It'll give you exact commands to run.

Try This

Set up the starter kit. Even if you're not building something specific yet, having a working project where you can type something in and get an AI response back — with the result saved to a database — is a foundation you'll use over and over. The entire setup takes about 30 minutes with a coding agent helping you. Once it's done, you have a launchpad for any AI-powered idea you want to test.

Module 7 — Lesson 66

pgvector and Semantic Search: Making Your App Understand Meaning, Not Just Keywords

The AI Prompting Course

This is the feature that made CaseSight actually useful. Before I added semantic search, users had to search for cases using exact keywords. Type "car accident" and you'd get cases with those exact words. But type "vehicle collision" and you'd get nothing, even though it means the same thing. Type "someone slipped on a wet floor at Walmart" and the keyword search had no idea what to do with that.

Semantic search fixed all of that. And the wild part is that adding it was way simpler than I expected.

What Semantic Search Actually Is

Let me explain this without any jargon first. Regular search looks for matching words. Semantic search understands meaning. It knows that "car accident" and "automobile crash" and "motor vehicle collision" are all talking about the same thing, even though they share zero words.

How does it work? Every piece of text gets converted into a long list of numbers called an embedding. Think of it as coordinates in a meaning-space. Texts that mean similar things end up close together in that space, even if they use totally different words. When a user searches, their query gets converted to the same kind of coordinates, and the database finds the closest matches.

That's it. That's the concept. Text goes in, numbers come out, and similar meanings have similar numbers.

pgvector: The Tool That Makes It Easy

pgvector is an extension for Postgres — which is the database under Supabase. It lets you store those number lists (vectors) right alongside your regular data and search through them efficiently. The beautiful thing is that Supabase lets you enable pgvector with one click in their dashboard.

Here's the prompt I used to set it up for CaseSight:

"I need to add semantic search to my Supabase database. Enable the pgvector extension. Add a column called 'embedding' to my 'cases' table — it should be of type vector(1536) since I'm using OpenAI's embedding model. Create a SQL function called 'match_cases' that takes a query embedding as input and returns the top 10 most similar cases, ordered by cosine similarity. Also create an API route at /api/search that takes a user's text query, generates an embedding for it, calls the match_cases function, and returns the results."

That one prompt got me about 80% of the way there. Claude built the database function, the API route, and the embedding generation logic. I had to debug a couple of type mismatches, but the core architecture was solid on the first try.

The Embedding Pipeline

There are two parts to making this work. First, you need to generate embeddings for all your existing data. Second, you need to generate an embedding for each search query in real time.

For the existing data, I used a batch script:

"Write me a Node.js script that reads all cases from my Supabase 'cases' table where the embedding column is null, generates an embedding for each case using the case_summary field, and saves the embedding back to the row. Use OpenAI's text-embedding-ada-002 model. Process them in batches of 20 to avoid rate limits. Log progress so I can see how many cases have been processed."

For the real-time search, the API route handles it. User types a query, the route generates an embedding for that query, passes it to the match_cases function, and returns the results. The whole thing takes about 200-400 milliseconds. Fast enough that it feels instant.

Why This Matters for Your Product

Semantic search isn't just for legal cases. Think about any product where users need to find things based on what they mean rather than exact keywords:

  • A recipe app where users type "something quick with chicken" instead of searching for "chicken stir fry"
  • A knowledge base where support agents describe a customer's problem and get back the most relevant help articles
  • A job board where candidates describe what they want to do and get matched to relevant listings
  • A product catalog where users describe what they need in natural language

The pattern is always the same: embed your content, embed the query, find the closest matches. Once you've built it once, you can apply it to anything.

The Gotchas

A few things I learned the hard way:

Embedding models matter. The quality of your search depends on the quality of your embeddings. OpenAI's embedding models are the most common choice and work well. You can also use Voyage AI or Cohere. Don't use a cheap or outdated model — the search quality will suffer and you'll blame your code when it's actually the embeddings.

What you embed matters. If your cases have a title, summary, and full text, embedding just the title gives you weak search. Embedding the summary usually hits the sweet spot between quality and cost. You can also concatenate multiple fields: "Title: [title]. Summary: [summary]. Jurisdiction: [jurisdiction]."

Cosine similarity vs. Euclidean distance. Don't worry about the math. Just use cosine similarity (which the match_cases function above does) — it works better for text search in practice.

Try This

If you have data that users need to search through — any data at all — try adding semantic search. The setup takes about an hour with a coding agent. Enable pgvector in Supabase, add an embedding column, write a batch script to generate embeddings, and build a search endpoint. The moment a user types a natural language question and gets back the right results, you'll see how powerful this is. It's the feature that makes people say "how did it know what I meant?"

Lesson 66 of 100

Module 7 — Lesson 67

Deploying to Vercel in Plain English: What to Say, What to Expect

The AI Prompting Course

Deployment used to terrify me. I'd build something locally, it would work perfectly on my machine, and then the idea of making it "live on the internet" felt like a completely different discipline. Setting up servers, configuring DNS, dealing with SSL certificates — it sounded like stuff only DevOps engineers did.

Then I deployed to Vercel for the first time and realized it's basically like pushing to GitHub with one extra step. The whole thing took about ten minutes.

What Deployment Actually Means

Let me demystify this. Right now, your app runs on your computer. When you open localhost:3000 in your browser, it's your machine serving that page. Nobody else can see it. Deployment means putting your app on someone else's computer (a server) so that anyone with the URL can access it.

That's literally all it is. Copy your code to a server. The server runs it. People visit a URL. They see your app.

Vercel makes this absurdly simple because it's designed specifically for Next.js apps (they made Next.js, so the integration is seamless). You connect it to your GitHub repo, and every time you push code, it automatically deploys.

The Step-by-Step, in Plain English

Here's what I tell Claude when I'm ready to deploy:

"I want to deploy this Next.js app to Vercel. Walk me through the steps. I have a GitHub account but this code isn't in a repo yet. I have environment variables in .env.local that the app needs. Give me the exact commands and steps."

And here's what it boils down to:

Step 1: Get your code on GitHub. If you're using Claude Code or Cursor, they can do this for you. The prompt is literally: "Initialize a git repository, create a .gitignore for Next.js, and push this code to a new GitHub repository called [your-project-name]." You'll need to authenticate with GitHub the first time, which Claude will walk you through.

Step 2: Connect Vercel to your repo. Go to vercel.com, sign up with your GitHub account, click "Import Project," select your repo. Vercel automatically detects it's a Next.js app and configures the build settings. You don't need to change anything.

Step 3: Add your environment variables. This is the one step people forget. Your .env.local file doesn't get pushed to GitHub (on purpose — you don't want your API keys public). So you need to add those same variables in Vercel's dashboard. There's a section called "Environment Variables" in your project settings. Add each key-value pair there. NEXT_PUBLIC_SUPABASE_URL, NEXT_PUBLIC_SUPABASE_ANON_KEY, ANTHROPIC_API_KEY — whatever your app needs.

Step 4: Deploy. Click "Deploy." Wait about 60-90 seconds. Vercel gives you a URL like your-project-name.vercel.app. That's your live app. On the internet. Right now.

What to Expect (The Gotchas)

Almost every first deployment has at least one issue. Here are the most common ones and what to tell Claude when they happen:

"Build failed." This usually means something works on your machine but breaks during Vercel's build process. The most common cause: TypeScript errors that your local dev server was ignoring. Copy the error from Vercel's build log and paste it to Claude: "My Vercel build failed with this error. How do I fix it?" Nine times out of ten, it's a quick fix.

"It deployed but nothing loads." Usually an environment variable issue. Your app is looking for NEXT_PUBLIC_SUPABASE_URL but you forgot to add it in Vercel. Check that every variable from your .env.local is also in Vercel's environment settings.

"It works but the API calls fail." Your API routes might be hitting rate limits, or the API key you added in Vercel might have a typo, or you might need to add your Vercel domain to an allow-list somewhere. Again — copy the error, paste to Claude, get the fix.

"It deployed but it looks different than localhost." Sometimes CSS behaves differently in production. Usually a caching issue. Try a hard refresh (Cmd+Shift+R on Mac). If that doesn't fix it, ask Claude to check for any development-only CSS or conditional styling.

Custom Domains

Once your app is deployed, you'll have a .vercel.app URL. If you want a custom domain (like yourapp.com), it's straightforward. Buy a domain from Namecheap or Google Domains, go to Vercel's domain settings, add your domain, and update your DNS settings where you bought the domain. Claude can walk you through the DNS part — just tell it where you bought your domain and it'll give you the exact records to add.

The Mental Shift

Here's what I want you to take away from this. Deployment is not the scary part. The hard part was building the app. Deployment is logistics — it's like putting something in an envelope and mailing it. The work was writing what's inside.

Once you've deployed once, the fear goes away completely. Every future project, you'll push to GitHub, import to Vercel, add your env vars, and be live in minutes. It becomes a non-event. And that's exactly what it should be.

Try This

If you have a Next.js app running locally — even the starter kit from Lesson 65 — deploy it right now. Don't wait until it's "ready." Deploy the scaffold. Deploy the ugly version. Get comfortable with the process when there's nothing at stake, so that when you deploy something that matters, it's routine.

Lesson 67 of 100

Module 7 — Lesson 68

When Your Build Breaks: How to Describe the Bug to Claude Without Knowing the Code

The AI Prompting Course

Your app is going to break. I need you to know that upfront, not as a discouragement but as a fact. Every app breaks. Professional apps built by senior engineers break. Your vibe-coded MVP is going to break. The question isn't whether — it's whether you can describe the problem clearly enough for Claude to fix it.

This is honestly the most important skill in vibe coding. Not the building. The debugging. Because building is the fun part — you describe what you want and things appear on the screen. Debugging is where most people give up. Something stops working, they don't understand the error message, they don't know what to tell Claude, and they close the laptop.

I'm going to teach you how to not be that person.

The Three-Part Bug Report

Every time something breaks, you need to communicate three things to Claude. This works whether you're using Claude Code, Cursor, or even just regular Claude chat. The structure is always the same:

1. What were you trying to do? "I clicked the Submit button on the /generate page after typing in a product description."

2. What did you expect to happen? "I expected the AI-generated copy to appear below the form, like it did yesterday."

3. What actually happened? "Nothing happened for about 5 seconds, then I got a red error message that says 'Internal Server Error.' The page didn't crash, but no results appeared."

That's it. That pattern — tried, expected, got — gives Claude enough to start diagnosing. You don't need to know what's wrong. You just need to accurately describe the symptoms.

The Error Message Is Gold

When your app shows an error, that error message is the single most useful piece of information you can give Claude. Don't paraphrase it. Don't describe it from memory. Copy and paste the exact text.

Even better: open your browser's developer tools (right click anywhere on the page, click "Inspect," then click the "Console" tab) and look for red error messages there. Those are usually more detailed than what shows on screen.

Here's a real prompt I've used:

"My app was working fine yesterday, but today when I try to generate ad copy, I get this error in the browser console: 'POST /api/generate 500 (Internal Server Error)' and in the network tab the response body says: '{"error":"Anthropic API error: 401 Unauthorized"}'. What's going on and how do I fix it?"

Claude immediately knew the issue: my API key had expired. It told me to go to the Anthropic console, generate a new key, and update it in both my .env.local file and Vercel's environment variables. Fixed in two minutes.

If I had just said "my app doesn't work anymore," Claude would have had to guess from dozens of possible causes. The specific error message narrows it down to one.

The "It Works Locally But Not in Production" Bug

This is the most common bug in vibe coding and it's almost always one of three things:

  • Missing environment variable in Vercel. You added a new feature that needs a new API key. You added it to .env.local but forgot to add it in Vercel.
  • Hardcoded localhost URL. Somewhere in your code, instead of using a relative URL like /api/generate, there's a hardcoded http://localhost:3000/api/generate. Works on your machine, fails in production.
  • Build-time vs. runtime error. Your code has something that only runs during the build phase on Vercel, and it fails because the environment is different. This is the trickiest one, but Claude is good at diagnosing it if you share the Vercel build logs.

My prompt for this scenario:

"This feature works perfectly on localhost but fails in production on Vercel. Here's what I see when I try it on the deployed site: [description]. Here's the error from Vercel's function logs: [paste the log]. Can you figure out why it works locally but not in production?"

The Infinite Loop Bug

I see this all the time with vibe coding, and it's uniquely frustrating. You ask Claude to fix something. It makes a change. The fix breaks something else. You report the new break. It makes another change. That breaks the first thing again. You're going in circles.

When this happens, stop and try this prompt:

"We've been going back and forth trying to fix this and we keep breaking one thing while fixing another. Let's step back. Here's what the feature should do: [describe the full expected behavior]. Here's what's currently broken: [list everything that's not working]. Instead of patching one thing at a time, can you look at the whole picture and rewrite this section to work correctly?"

Sometimes the fix isn't a patch — it's a rewrite of the component. Claude is good at this when you give it permission to take a broader approach instead of making surgical fixes.

Screenshots and Screen Recordings

If you're using a tool that supports image input, a screenshot is worth a thousand words. Take a screenshot of the broken UI and paste it into the conversation: "This is what the page looks like right now. The dropdown menu is overlapping with the table header and the 'Export' button is pushed off-screen on mobile. Here's what it should look like: [describe or share a mockup]."

Visual bugs are particularly hard to describe in text. A screenshot resolves all ambiguity instantly.

The Bug That Isn't a Bug

Sometimes the app is working exactly as coded, but not doing what you wanted. This isn't a bug — it's a miscommunication from your original prompt. When this happens, don't say "this is broken." Say:

"The filter is working, but it's doing an AND filter where all conditions must match. I actually want an OR filter where any condition matching is enough to show the result. Can you update the filter logic?"

Clear description of what it's doing vs. what you want it to do. That's all Claude needs.

Try This

The next time something breaks in any app you're building, before you do anything else, write out the three-part bug report: what you tried, what you expected, what happened. Open the browser console and copy any red error messages. Then take that whole package and paste it to Claude. You'll be amazed at how fast it resolves when you give it the right information.

Lesson 68 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 7 — Lesson 69

Scraping to Database to API: The Data Pipeline Claude Can Help You Build End-to-End

The AI Prompting Course

Most useful apps are backed by data. Not just user-generated data — curated, collected, structured data that gives the app its value. A job board needs job listings. A comparison tool needs product specs. CaseSight needs legal cases with verdict amounts. And getting that data is often the hardest and least glamorous part of the entire build.

I've built multiple data pipelines with Claude — scraping websites, cleaning messy data, loading it into databases, and serving it through APIs. Here's how the whole process works, start to finish.

Phase 1: Scraping

Scraping means pulling data from websites programmatically. Instead of copying and pasting from a webpage into a spreadsheet one case at a time (which I did for the first 30 cases before I lost my mind), you write a script that does it for you.

Here's a real prompt I used:

"I need to scrape case data from [website]. Each case has a page with the case name, jurisdiction, date, verdict amount, and a summary paragraph. The case listing page shows 20 cases per page with pagination. Write me a Node.js script using Axios for HTTP requests and Cheerio for HTML parsing that: 1) Starts at the first listing page, 2) Extracts the URL for each individual case, 3) Visits each case page and extracts the fields I mentioned, 4) Saves each case as a JSON object, 5) Writes all cases to a file called cases.json, 6) Handles pagination to get all pages, not just the first one. Add a 2-second delay between requests so I don't hammer their server."

That's a meaty prompt, but notice what it does. It describes the source, the data structure, the tools to use, the step-by-step logic, and even the politeness constraint (the delay between requests). Claude can build this entire script in one shot.

Phase 2: Cleaning and Transforming

Scraped data is always messy. Always. Verdict amounts come through as "$1,250,000.00" or "1.25M" or "approximately $1.25 million." Dates are in different formats. Some fields are missing. Some have HTML artifacts.

"I have a cases.json file with scraped data. The verdict_amount field is inconsistent — sometimes it's a clean number, sometimes it has dollar signs and commas, sometimes it's written out like '1.5 million.' Write a cleaning script that: 1) Normalizes all verdict amounts to plain integers (no decimals needed), 2) Parses all dates into ISO format, 3) Trims whitespace from all string fields, 4) Removes any cases where the verdict_amount couldn't be parsed (log those to a separate file so I can review them manually), 5) Outputs a cleaned_cases.json file."

This cleaning step is where a lot of people skip, and then they wonder why their search results look weird or their analytics don't add up. Garbage in, garbage out. Spend the time cleaning.

Phase 3: Loading Into the Database

Now you have clean data in a JSON file and you need it in Supabase.

"Write me a script that reads cleaned_cases.json and inserts each case into my Supabase 'cases' table. The table has columns: id (auto-generated uuid), case_name (text), jurisdiction (text), case_date (date), verdict_amount (integer), summary (text), source_url (text), created_at (auto-generated timestamp). Use upsert based on source_url so I can run this script multiple times without creating duplicates. Process in batches of 50. Log how many cases were inserted and how many were skipped as duplicates."

The upsert detail is important. When you're iterating on your scraper and running it multiple times (you will), you don't want duplicate records. Upsert means "insert if it's new, update if it already exists." That one word saves you hours of debugging.

Phase 4: Serving Through an API

Once data is in Supabase, you need API routes that your frontend can call to retrieve it.

"Create an API route at /api/cases that supports the following: GET /api/cases returns a paginated list of cases, 20 per page, sorted by case_date descending. Support query parameters for filtering: ?jurisdiction=California, ?min_amount=100000, ?max_amount=500000. GET /api/cases/[id] returns a single case by ID. Both endpoints should return JSON and handle errors properly."

Now your frontend can fetch data, filter it, paginate through it — all standard stuff that Claude builds quickly and correctly.

The Full Pipeline in One Picture

Here's what you've built:

  • Scraper pulls raw data from the internet
  • Cleaner normalizes and validates the data
  • Loader puts the clean data into your database
  • API serves the data to your frontend
  • Frontend displays it to users

Each piece is a separate script or route. Each one was built with a single focused prompt. And the whole pipeline can be re-run whenever you want to update your data — run the scraper again, clean, reload. Your app stays fresh.

The Ethics Note

I want to be straightforward about this. Not everything on the internet is okay to scrape. Check the website's robots.txt file and terms of service. Don't scrape data behind a login wall that you don't have permission to access. Don't scrape at a rate that could impact the site's performance. Use public data, be respectful about it, and when in doubt, ask Claude: "Is it legal and ethical to scrape [this type of data] from [this type of site]?"

Try This

Identify one data source that would make your app idea valuable. It could be a public website, a government database, a public API, or even a spreadsheet you already have. Write the scraping prompt (or the import prompt if it's a CSV/spreadsheet). Get that data into Supabase. Once you have real data in a real database, your app idea suddenly feels much more tangible.

Lesson 69 of 100

Module 7 — Lesson 70

Building a Claude-Powered Artifact: Giving Users Their Own AI Inside Your Product

The AI Prompting Course

This is where it gets really fun. Everything we've covered so far — the tech stack, the database, the deployment, the semantic search — it all comes together when you build a feature that gives your users their own AI experience inside your product. Not a chatbot. Not a generic "ask AI anything" box. A constrained, purpose-built AI feature that does one specific thing incredibly well.

I call these Claude-powered artifacts. And they're the single most compelling feature you can add to almost any product right now.

What a Claude-Powered Artifact Looks Like

Let me give you real examples. In CaseSight, the artifact is the case analysis feature. A user selects a few cases from search results, clicks "Analyze," and gets an AI-generated comparison — common themes, how verdict amounts varied by jurisdiction, what factors might have influenced the outcomes. The user didn't type a prompt. They clicked a button. But under the hood, a carefully crafted prompt was sent to Claude with all the case data, and the response was formatted and displayed in a clean UI.

That's the pattern. The user takes an action. Your app constructs a prompt behind the scenes using the user's data and context. Claude generates a response. Your app formats and displays it.

The user never sees the prompt. They never interact with Claude directly. They just see your product doing something smart.

The System Prompt Is Everything

The quality of a Claude-powered artifact depends almost entirely on the system prompt — the hidden instructions that tell Claude how to behave for this specific feature.

Here's how I set up the system prompt for the case analysis feature:

"You are a legal research assistant embedded in a case analysis tool. When given a set of personal injury cases, you provide a structured analysis covering: 1) Common patterns across the cases, 2) Range and average of verdict amounts, 3) How jurisdiction may have influenced outcomes, 4) Key factors that likely drove higher or lower verdicts. Keep your tone professional but accessible — the user may be a lawyer or may be a non-lawyer. Use specific numbers and case names from the data provided. Never make up cases or facts not present in the data. Format your response with clear headings and bullet points."

This system prompt does several critical things. It defines the role. It specifies the output structure. It sets the tone. It establishes guardrails (don't make things up). And it references the actual data the user selected, not generic knowledge.

Building It: The Architecture

Here's the prompt I'd give a coding agent to build a Claude-powered artifact:

"I want to add a 'Generate Analysis' feature to my cases app. When a user selects 2-5 cases using checkboxes and clicks 'Analyze Selected,' it should: 1) Gather the full data for each selected case from Supabase, 2) Send it to an API route at /api/analyze, 3) The API route constructs a prompt that includes the system prompt (stored in a constants file) and the case data formatted as structured context, 4) Call the Claude API with this prompt using claude-sonnet-4-20250514, 5) Stream the response back to the frontend, 6) Display the analysis in a styled card below the case list with proper heading and paragraph formatting. Add a loading state and error handling. Save each analysis to a 'analyses' table in Supabase with the case IDs used and the full output."

That one prompt gets you the full feature. Frontend interaction, data fetching, prompt construction, AI call, response streaming, display, and persistence. It's a lot of functionality, but each piece is straightforward and Claude handles the wiring.

The Secret: Prompt Engineering as Product Engineering

Here's the thing that most builders miss. When you're building a Claude-powered artifact, the system prompt IS the product. The code is just plumbing. The UI is just presentation. The thing that determines whether your users say "whoa, this is good" or "meh, I could've just asked ChatGPT" is the quality of that system prompt.

So treat it like a product feature. Iterate on it. Test it with different inputs. Refine the phrasing. Add examples of good output in the prompt. Constrain it when it's too verbose. Push it when it's too shallow.

I spent more time refining the system prompt for CaseSight's analysis feature than I spent building the entire frontend. That time was well spent. The analysis output went from "generic and obvious" to "genuinely insightful and specific" through prompt iteration alone.

Guardrails Matter

When you give users an AI feature, you need to think about edge cases:

  • What if they submit empty or garbage input? Validate on the frontend before sending to the API. Don't waste API credits on empty requests.
  • What if Claude's response is weird or off-topic? Add a feedback mechanism — a thumbs down button that logs the input and output so you can review and improve the system prompt.
  • What if someone tries to jailbreak it? If your AI feature is public-facing, add a content filter. Your system prompt should include instructions like "Only respond about topics related to personal injury case analysis. If asked about anything else, politely redirect."
  • What about cost? Each API call costs money. Add rate limiting. Track usage per user. Set a daily or monthly cap if you're on a budget. Claude is affordable, but unlimited unthrottled usage can add up fast.

What Makes This Better Than "Just Use ChatGPT"

Anyone can open ChatGPT and type a question. What makes your artifact valuable is the context it provides automatically. The user doesn't have to explain their data, copy-paste their cases, or craft a prompt. They click a button and get an expert-level analysis of their specific data. That's the product value. The AI is the engine. Your app is the experience.

The best Claude-powered artifacts feel like the app is intelligent — not that there's an AI chatbot tacked onto the side. That distinction is everything.

Try This

Think about the one moment in your app where a user would benefit from an AI-generated insight. Not a general chatbot — a specific moment where the user has context (data they've selected, a form they've filled out, a page they're viewing) and the AI can add value by analyzing that context. Write the system prompt for that feature. Test it manually by pasting some sample data into Claude along with your system prompt. Refine it until the output is genuinely good. Then build the API route and UI around it. That's your product's killer feature.

Module 8 — Lesson 71

Using Images as Inputs: What Claude Can and Can't Do With Screenshots and Photos

The AI Prompting Course

I remember the first time I dragged a screenshot into Claude. I'd been going back and forth with a developer on a UI bug, trying to describe what was wrong with the spacing on a card component. I'd typed three paragraphs explaining the issue. Then I thought — wait, let me just show it.

I dropped the screenshot in and typed one sentence: "What's wrong with the spacing on this card compared to standard design patterns?" And Claude nailed it in seconds. Identified the inconsistent padding, the misaligned text, even noticed the border radius was different on one corner. Three paragraphs of description replaced by one image and one sentence.

That was the moment I realized most people are dramatically underusing image inputs. They're still typing everything out when they could just show it.

What Actually Works

Here's the thing — Claude is genuinely good at reading and understanding images, but you need to know what it's good at and where it falls short. Otherwise you'll either avoid using images entirely (missing out on a huge productivity boost) or you'll expect magic and be disappointed.

Screenshots of UIs and websites: This is where image input shines the most. I use this constantly at Mudita Studios. Drop in a screenshot of a landing page and ask for a critique. Drop in a competitor's ad and ask for an analysis. Drop in a wireframe sketch on a whiteboard and ask Claude to turn it into a component description. All of these work incredibly well.

Charts and graphs: Claude can read bar charts, line graphs, pie charts — basically any standard data visualization. I'll screenshot a Google Analytics dashboard and ask "What's the trend here and what should I be concerned about?" It'll read the axes, identify the patterns, and give you a real answer.

Text in images: Screenshots of error messages, code snippets, Slack conversations, email threads — Claude reads text in images reliably. I've pasted in screenshots of terminal errors and gotten debugging help without typing a single line of the error manually.

Photos of physical things: Product photos, whiteboard diagrams, handwritten notes, business cards. It handles all of these. Not perfectly on the handwriting — messy handwriting gets dicey — but printed text and clear handwriting work well.

Where It Struggles

Now for the honest part, because I don't want you wasting time on things that won't work well.

Tiny text in large images: If you screenshot an entire spreadsheet and the cell text is 8px, Claude's going to struggle. Zoom in first, or crop to the relevant section. I learned this the hard way trying to get it to read a full Airtable view — it could read some cells but hallucinated others.

Precise numerical data: If you need exact numbers from a chart, Claude might approximate. It can tell you "revenue went up about 30% in Q3" but if you need it to read that the exact figure was $347,291, you're better off pasting the actual data. Use images for patterns and trends, use text for precise numbers.

Multiple images in sequence: When you drop in five screenshots and say "compare all of these," the quality drops. Claude handles one or two images really well. Three is okay. Five or more and you start getting shallow analysis. Better to do them in pairs.

The Prompting Pattern That Makes Images 10x More Useful

Here's what most people do wrong: they drop an image and say "What do you think?" That's the image equivalent of "Write me an email to a client." Too vague.

The pattern that works is: image + specific question + context about what you're trying to accomplish.

"Here's a screenshot of our current pricing page. We're a B2B SaaS company selling to mid-market HR teams. Our conversion rate on this page is 2.1% which is below our 3.5% target. What specific elements on this page might be causing friction or confusion for a buyer comparing us to competitors?"

See the difference? Same image. But now Claude knows what it's looking at, who it's for, what the problem is, and what kind of feedback you need. The analysis you get back will be surgical instead of generic.

Another one I use all the time:

"Here's a screenshot of an error I'm getting in my terminal. I'm running a Next.js app locally, just pulled from main branch. This started after I added a new API route for user authentication. What's causing this and how do I fix it?"

My Favorite Use Cases

These are the ones I come back to every week:

  • Design feedback: Screenshot a UI, ask Claude to identify hierarchy issues, accessibility problems, or alignment inconsistencies
  • Competitive analysis: Screenshot a competitor's landing page, ask for a breakdown of their messaging strategy and positioning
  • Bug reporting: Screenshot the bug, paste the error, and get debugging suggestions in one shot
  • Meeting notes: Photo of a whiteboard after a brainstorm session, ask Claude to organize the ideas into categories and extract action items
  • Quick data reads: Screenshot a dashboard chart when you don't have time to export the data, get a narrative summary of what's happening

Try This

Right now, think of something on your screen or your desk that you'd normally spend time describing in text. A design you're working on, a chart you're trying to interpret, a page layout you want feedback on. Take a screenshot, drop it into Claude, and pair it with a specific question and two sentences of context about what you're working on. You'll be surprised how much faster you get to a useful answer.

Module 8 — Lesson 72

PDF Analysis: Extracting, Summarizing, and Comparing Documents at Scale

The AI Prompting Course

Last month I got sent a 47-page partnership agreement for a potential deal with Official AI. My lawyer wanted $400/hour to review it. The other party wanted a response by Friday. I had two days, a full sprint schedule, and no legal budget left for the quarter.

So I uploaded the PDF to Claude and said: "Read this entire agreement. I'm the party being offered the deal. Identify every clause that limits my rights, creates financial obligations, or gives the other party unilateral power to change terms. Flag anything unusual compared to standard B2B SaaS partnership agreements."

In about ninety seconds, I had a structured breakdown of every concerning clause, organized by category, with plain-English explanations of what each one actually meant. I still had my lawyer review the final redlines, but instead of paying for six hours of review, I paid for one hour of targeted questions. That single prompt saved me probably $2,000.

That's what PDF analysis with Claude actually looks like when you do it right.

The Basics: How to Feed Claude a PDF

You can upload PDFs directly in Claude's interface — just drag and drop or use the attachment button. Claude reads the text content, and for most standard business documents, it captures everything. Contracts, reports, whitepapers, proposals, invoices, research papers — all fair game.

A few things to know upfront. Scanned PDFs (where the text is actually an image) work less reliably than native text PDFs. If you've got a scanned contract from 2015, the text extraction might be spotty. For those, you're better off running it through an OCR tool first, then pasting the text. But for anything created digitally in the last decade — Word docs saved as PDF, Google Docs exports, most things from DocuSign — you're good.

The Three Modes of PDF Analysis

I think about PDF analysis in three modes, and the prompt structure is different for each.

Mode 1: Extraction. You need specific information pulled out of a long document. This is the "needle in a haystack" use case.

"Read this vendor agreement. Extract every instance of a specific dollar amount, payment term, or financial obligation. List them in a table with the clause number, the obligation, the amount, and the due date."

This is phenomenal for contracts, SOWs, invoices, and any document where you need to find specific data points scattered across dozens of pages. What used to take thirty minutes of Ctrl+F and manual noting takes seconds.

Mode 2: Summarization. You need to understand what a document says without reading every page.

"Summarize this 30-page market research report. I'm the founder of a SaaS company in the HR tech space. Give me: (1) the three most important findings relevant to my market, (2) any data points I could use in a pitch deck, and (3) anything that contradicts the assumption that SMBs are moving toward AI-powered hiring tools."

Notice how I'm not just saying "summarize this." I'm telling Claude who I am and what I care about. The summary it produces is going to be filtered through my specific lens, which makes it actually useful instead of generic.

Mode 3: Comparison. You need to understand how two or more documents relate to each other. We'll go deeper on this in lesson 76, but here's the basic setup.

"I'm uploading two versions of our terms of service. The first is our current live version. The second is the updated draft from our legal team. Compare them section by section and identify every meaningful change. Ignore formatting changes. For each substantive change, explain what it means in plain English and flag anything that changes our liability or customer obligations."

Handling Long Documents

Here's where people run into trouble. Claude has a context window — a limit on how much text it can process at once. For most documents under 50 pages, you're fine. But if you're dealing with a 200-page technical manual or a massive regulatory filing, you need a strategy.

My approach: break it into sections. Upload the first 50 pages, do your analysis, then upload the next 50. At the start of each new section, remind Claude of the context.

"Continuing analysis of the regulatory filing. In the previous section, you identified three areas of concern: [list them]. Now read pages 51-100 and look for additional issues, particularly anything related to data privacy requirements in the EU market."

This works way better than trying to cram everything in at once, because Claude can focus deeply on each section rather than skimming the whole thing.

Real Workflows I Use Regularly

  • Proposal review: Upload a client's RFP, have Claude extract every requirement into a checklist, then use that checklist to draft our response
  • Invoice auditing: Upload a stack of vendor invoices, ask Claude to flag any that are above historical averages or have unusual line items
  • Research synthesis: Upload an industry report, have Claude pull out every statistic with a source citation so I can use them in content
  • Contract negotiation prep: Upload their draft, ask Claude to identify my three strongest negotiation points and suggest specific alternative language

Try This

Find a document you've been meaning to read but haven't gotten to — a contract, a report, a proposal sitting in your downloads folder. Upload it to Claude and don't just say "summarize." Tell Claude who you are, what decision you're trying to make, and what specific information would help you make it. Watch how targeted the output becomes when you frame it around your actual need.

Module 8 — Lesson 73

Uploading Reference Files to Projects So Claude Always Has the Source of Truth

The AI Prompting Course

There was a period a few months ago where I was starting every single Claude conversation the same way. I'd paste in our brand voice guide. Then our product positioning doc. Then the key stats about our user base. Then our current pricing structure. Every. Single. Time.

I was burning five minutes at the start of every conversation just loading context. And worse, sometimes I'd forget to include something, and Claude would give me output that contradicted our actual positioning because it didn't have the full picture. I'd catch it, paste in the missing doc, and start over.

Then I discovered Projects in Claude, and it genuinely changed how I work. Not in a small way — in a "I can't believe I was doing it the other way" kind of way.

What Projects Actually Are

Projects in Claude let you upload reference files that persist across every conversation within that project. Think of it like giving Claude a permanent filing cabinet. Instead of re-explaining who you are and what you're working on every time, you load up the project once with your key documents, and every new conversation starts with Claude already knowing your context.

For Official AI, I have a project with our brand guide, our technical architecture overview, our competitive landscape analysis, our pricing page copy, and our current roadmap. When I start a new conversation in that project, Claude already knows all of it. I can just dive straight into "Write me a cold email for a Series A founder who's evaluating us against Intercom" and the output is immediately on-brand, technically accurate, and aware of our actual differentiators.

No preamble. No context-setting. Just work.

What to Upload (And What Not To)

Here's the thing — not everything belongs in a project. If you dump fifty documents in there, Claude has so much context that it can get confused about what's relevant to your current question. The key is curating what goes in.

What works great as project files:

  • Brand voice guide: Your tone, style, words to use, words to avoid. This is the single highest-ROI file you can upload. Once Claude has your voice guide, every piece of writing it produces sounds like you instead of sounding like AI.
  • Product/company overview: What you do, who you serve, your key differentiators. Keep this to one or two pages max. Dense and factual.
  • Current pricing and packaging: So Claude never suggests something that contradicts your actual offering.
  • Target audience profiles: Who your customers are, what they care about, their pain points. This shapes every piece of output.
  • Key data and metrics: Your current MRR, user count, growth rate, conversion benchmarks. Anything you regularly reference.
  • Style and formatting standards: How you structure emails, blog posts, proposals. Templates and examples of work you've liked.

What doesn't belong:

  • Entire codebases (too noisy, use conversation-level uploads for specific files)
  • Raw data exports (upload these per-conversation when you need analysis)
  • Documents that change weekly (you want relatively stable reference material)
  • Anything confidential you're not comfortable with being in Claude's context

How to Structure Your Project Files

I learned this the hard way: Claude reads project files, but it reads them better when they're well-organized. If you upload a messy Google Doc with random notes, Claude will pick up on the mess. If you upload a clean, structured document with clear headers, Claude uses it much more effectively.

My format for project files:

"COMPANY: Official AI
WHAT WE DO: AI-powered customer intelligence platform for B2B SaaS companies
WHO WE SERVE: Series A to Series C SaaS founders and their go-to-market teams
KEY DIFFERENTIATOR: We analyze customer conversations across all channels to surface buying signals before they become obvious
VOICE: Direct, data-driven, no fluff. We talk like operators, not marketers.
COMPETITORS: Intercom (larger, slower), Drift (more enterprise), Crisp (cheaper, less capable)"

Clear labels. Short statements. No ambiguity. Claude can reference any of this instantly when it needs to.

The Project Instructions Field

This is the part most people miss. Beyond uploading files, each project has a custom instructions field. This is where you tell Claude how to behave in this project — not just what to know, but how to act.

Here's what mine looks like for our marketing project:

"You are helping with marketing content for Official AI. Always write in our brand voice (see uploaded voice guide). Never use the words 'revolutionary,' 'cutting-edge,' or 'leverage.' Default to short paragraphs and direct sentences. When writing emails, assume the recipient is a busy founder who skims. When suggesting copy, give three variations ranked by how aggressive the CTA is. Always reference real product features from the uploaded product doc — never make up features."

This means I never have to repeat these instructions. Every conversation in this project follows these rules automatically.

Multiple Projects for Different Contexts

I don't use one mega-project for everything. I have separate projects for separate contexts:

  • Official AI — Marketing: Brand guide, voice doc, competitor analysis, landing page copy
  • Official AI — Product: Architecture docs, API specs, roadmap, user feedback summaries
  • Mudita Studios — Client Work: Client briefs, SOWs, past deliverables for reference
  • Personal — Writing: My writing samples, newsletter archives, style preferences

Each project is a focused context. When I open the marketing project, Claude thinks like a marketer who knows our brand. When I open the product project, it thinks like a PM who knows our stack.

Try This

Create your first project right now. Start with just three files: a one-page company overview, your brand voice guidelines (even if it's just a few bullet points about how you communicate), and a description of your target customer. Set the custom instructions to include your three biggest pet peeves about AI-generated content. Then start a conversation and ask it to write something you'd normally write yourself. Notice how much closer it gets to your actual voice on the first try.

Module 8 — Lesson 74

Voice-to-Claude Workflows: Dictating, Cleaning, and Sending

The AI Prompting Course

I'm going to tell you something that might sound weird: some of my best prompts never touch a keyboard. I dictate them while walking to get coffee, while driving, while pacing around my apartment trying to think through a problem.

Here's why this matters. When you type, you self-edit in real time. You second-guess your phrasing. You try to make it "sound like a prompt." But when you talk, you just... explain things. And it turns out that natural, spoken explanation is often the best possible input for Claude. You ramble, you give context, you explain the situation the way you'd explain it to a friend. That's exactly what Claude needs.

The problem is that raw dictation is messy. It's full of "um," "like," and half-finished sentences. So you need a workflow that captures the good stuff and cleans up the mess. Let me show you what I mean.

The Basic Voice-to-Claude Pipeline

Here's the workflow I use almost every day:

Step 1: Dictate into your phone's voice notes or any transcription app. I use the built-in voice memo app on my iPhone, but you can use Otter, the whisper API, or even just the voice typing feature in your phone's keyboard. Don't worry about being polished. Just talk through what you need.

Step 2: Get the raw transcript. Most transcription tools give you decent text. It won't be perfect — there'll be filler words, incomplete thoughts, maybe some words it misheard. That's fine.

Step 3: Feed the raw transcript to Claude with a cleaning instruction.

"Here's a raw voice transcript of me explaining what I need. Clean it up into a clear, structured prompt, preserving all my specific details and context. Remove filler words and repetition, but keep my natural tone and all the information. Then execute on whatever I was asking for."

This is the key move. You're using Claude to both clean the input AND act on it in one step. You don't need to manually edit the transcript. Claude is excellent at extracting signal from noisy human speech.

Why This Is Better Than Typing (Sometimes)

I see this all the time — people sit in front of Claude's text box and freeze up. They don't know how to start the prompt. They overthink the structure. They spend five minutes crafting two sentences.

When you dictate, you bypass all of that. You just talk. And here's the counterintuitive part: the "messy" spoken version often contains more useful context than the "clean" typed version. Because when you talk, you naturally include the background, the reasoning, the constraints — all the stuff you'd edit out when typing because you think it's not relevant.

Let me show you a real example. Here's what I might type:

"Write a proposal for a new client project. Web design, $15K budget, 6-week timeline."

Here's what I'd say out loud (transcribed):

"Okay so I just got off a call with this guy Marcus, he runs a DTC skincare brand, they're doing like two million a year, and they need a website redesign because their current site looks like it was built in 2019 and their conversion rate is terrible, I think he said like 1.2 percent. He's got about fifteen grand to spend and he wants it done in six weeks which is tight but doable if we use a template-based approach. He specifically said he doesn't want it to look 'corporate' — his words — he wants it to feel more like Glossier or Aesop. Oh and he mentioned they're launching three new products in Q2 so the site needs to handle that."

That second version is way more useful. It has the client's name, their revenue, the specific problem, the aesthetic reference points, the timeline constraint, and a business event that affects the project scope. All of that came out naturally because I was just talking.

Advanced: Voice Memos as a Thinking Tool

This is something I started doing recently and it's become one of my highest-leverage habits. When I'm working through a complex problem — should we build this feature, how should we position against a new competitor, what should our Q2 priorities be — I'll record myself thinking out loud for five or ten minutes. Just stream of consciousness.

Then I send that transcript to Claude with:

"Here's a transcript of me thinking through a strategic decision. Organize my thoughts into: (1) the core question I'm trying to answer, (2) the arguments for each option I mentioned, (3) the concerns I raised, and (4) any decision criteria I implied but didn't explicitly state. Then give me your recommendation based on my own reasoning."

What you get back is essentially your own thinking, organized and reflected back to you. It's like having a chief of staff who listens to you ramble and then hands you a structured brief. I use this for everything from product strategy to figuring out what to prioritize on a Monday morning.

Tools That Make This Seamless

  • iPhone Voice Memos + copy transcript: Free, built in, and the transcription is solid for clear speech
  • Otter.ai: Records and transcribes in real time, great for capturing longer sessions
  • macOS Dictation (Fn Fn): Hit the function key twice on a Mac and start talking directly into any text field, including Claude
  • Whisper API: If you're technical, OpenAI's Whisper gives you near-perfect transcription you can pipe directly to Claude via the API

Try This

Next time you need to write something — an email, a brief, a project plan — don't type the prompt. Open your voice recorder, explain what you need as if you're talking to a coworker, and talk for 60-90 seconds. Get the transcript, paste it into Claude, and add one line: "Clean this up and do what I'm asking for." Compare the result to what you'd get from your typical typed prompt. I think you'll be surprised which one produces better output.

Module 8 — Lesson 75

Using Claude to Interpret Dashboards and Analytics Screenshots

The AI Prompting Course

Every Monday morning I open about six dashboards. Google Analytics for the marketing site. Stripe for revenue. Our internal product dashboard for usage metrics. Meta Ads Manager. PostHog for feature adoption. And usually whatever Airtable or spreadsheet we're tracking our sales pipeline in.

I used to spend an hour looking at all of this, trying to spot what changed, what matters, and what I should actually do about it. Now I spend about fifteen minutes. The difference is that I screenshot each dashboard and have Claude do the interpretation for me.

Let me show you what I mean, because this is one of those workflows that sounds simple but completely changes your relationship with data.

The Problem With Dashboards

Here's the thing about dashboards — they show you data but they don't tell you what the data means. You see a line going up. Great. Is that good? How much did it go up? Is that rate of increase normal or unusual? Is it because of something you did, or seasonal, or random noise?

Most people look at dashboards and either panic ("traffic is down!") or celebrate ("traffic is up!") without actually understanding the context. And honestly, unless you're a data analyst who does this full-time, you're probably not catching the subtle stuff — the slow trends, the leading indicators, the things that are about to become problems but aren't yet.

Claude is surprisingly good at this kind of pattern interpretation. Not because it's better at math than you — it's not doing calculations from a screenshot. But because it can quickly read the shape of the data, compare it to common patterns, and articulate what might be going on in a way that's actually helpful.

The Right Way to Share a Dashboard Screenshot

I see people screenshot their entire monitor — two dashboards, some browser tabs, their Slack sidebar — and drop it into Claude asking "What do you see?" That's going to get you garbage.

Here's the formula that works:

Crop to one chart or one view at a time. Claude reads one focused image much better than a cluttered screen.

Tell Claude what it's looking at. Don't assume it can read every axis label and title perfectly. Spend one sentence setting the scene.

Tell Claude what you care about. Are you looking for anomalies? Trends? A comparison to last period? Without direction, it'll give you a generic description of the chart.

Here's a real example I use weekly:

"This is a screenshot from our Google Analytics showing organic traffic over the last 90 days, with daily granularity. We published a major content push in mid-February (about 15 new blog posts). I'm trying to understand if that content push is actually driving organic growth or if this traffic pattern is normal seasonal variation. What do you see in this trend?"

Now Claude knows the metric, the timeframe, the granularity, the specific event that might be causing a change, and what question I'm actually trying to answer. The response I get back is genuinely useful analysis, not just "the line goes up in March."

Stacking Multiple Screenshots for a Full Picture

My Monday morning routine works like this. I take three or four screenshots — one from each key dashboard — and share them in sequence with a framing prompt:

"I'm going to share screenshots from three dashboards for my weekly review. After you see all of them, I want you to give me: (1) the single most important positive trend, (2) the single most concerning signal, (3) one thing I should investigate further, and (4) what I should focus on this week based on what the data is showing. Here's the first one — this is our Stripe revenue dashboard showing MRR over the last 60 days."

Then I drop in each screenshot one at a time with a quick label. By the end, Claude gives me a unified brief that connects signals across different data sources. My revenue is up but my trial-to-paid conversion is dropping — that means I'm getting more leads but they're lower quality. That kind of cross-dashboard insight used to take me an hour of manual analysis.

What Claude Catches That You Might Miss

  • Trend direction vs. magnitude: "Your traffic is up, but the rate of growth is actually slowing — you're seeing diminishing returns"
  • Correlation between charts: "Your ad spend increased and your CPA decreased, but your overall conversion rate also dropped — you might be optimizing for clicks rather than qualified traffic"
  • Seasonal context: "This dip is consistent with what most B2B companies see in late December — I wouldn't make strategy changes based on this alone"
  • Anomaly detection: "There's a single-day spike on March 12 that's 4x your daily average. That's probably a referral from a specific source worth investigating"

A Word of Caution

Claude is interpreting visual data, not doing precise computation. Use it for pattern recognition, trend interpretation, and generating hypotheses. Don't use it to calculate exact percentages or verify specific numbers from a chart. For that, export the actual data and paste it as a CSV or table. We'll cover that in lesson 77.

Try This

Open whatever analytics dashboard you check most often. Screenshot the most important chart. Drop it into Claude with three things: what the chart shows, what time period it covers, and the one question you most want answered about it. See how the interpretation compares to your own read of the data. I bet it surfaces at least one angle you hadn't considered.

Lesson 75 of 100
