Module 8 — Lesson 76

Document Comparison: Feeding Two Versions and Asking for a Diff in Plain English

The AI Prompting Course

I used to dread contract revisions. Someone sends back a redlined version of a 20-page agreement, and suddenly I'm spending my afternoon squinting at Track Changes in Word, trying to figure out which edits are cosmetic and which ones just changed my liability from "reasonable efforts" to "guaranteed outcome." Fun stuff.

Now I upload both versions to Claude and get a plain-English breakdown of every meaningful change in about two minutes. Not the formatting tweaks. Not the comma moves. The stuff that actually matters.

This is one of those use cases where Claude goes from "nice to have" to "I genuinely cannot imagine doing this the old way again."

The Basic Setup

The concept is simple: you give Claude two versions of a document and ask it to tell you what changed. But the way you frame the ask makes an enormous difference in the quality of what you get back.

Here's what most people do:

"Compare these two documents and tell me the differences."

And what they get is a line-by-line list of every change, including "changed 'the' to 'a'" and "moved paragraph 3 to paragraph 4." Technically accurate. Completely useless for decision-making.

Here's what I do instead:

"I'm uploading two versions of a consulting agreement. Version 1 is what we sent to the client. Version 2 is what they sent back with their changes. Compare them and give me only the substantive changes — things that affect scope, payment, timeline, liability, termination rights, or IP ownership. Ignore formatting, word choice changes that don't alter meaning, and reordering of sections. For each substantive change, explain: (1) what it was before, (2) what it is now, and (3) whether this change favors us or the client."

Now Claude filters out the noise and gives me a strategic briefing. Every change is contextualized with who it benefits. I can sit down with my team and make decisions in fifteen minutes instead of spending two hours in Track Changes.

Beyond Legal: Where Document Comparison Shines

People immediately think "contracts" when I talk about this, but I use document comparison for all kinds of things.

Proposal revisions: When a team member revises a proposal I drafted, I want to see what they changed and why. Not to micromanage — to learn what they thought needed improving.

"Here's my original project proposal and the version my business partner revised. Identify the changes he made and for each one, explain what problem he was likely trying to solve with that edit."

Policy updates: When a platform updates their terms of service or privacy policy, I feed in the old and new versions.

"Here's Meta's previous advertising policy and their updated version from this month. I run paid ads for e-commerce clients. What changed that affects how I can target, what I can say in ad copy, or what creative formats are now restricted?"

Content drafts: When an editor returns a revised blog post, I can see exactly what they tightened, cut, and restructured.

"Compare these two versions of my blog post. The first is my draft, the second is after my editor's pass. Identify the patterns in what she changed — what does she consistently cut, restructure, or rephrase? I want to learn from her editing style so I can write cleaner first drafts."

That last one is one of my favorites. You're not just comparing documents — you're extracting editorial intelligence from the comparison.

How to Format the Upload

You have a few options for getting two documents into Claude:

Option 1: Two file uploads. Upload both PDFs or documents. Label them clearly in your prompt — "The first attachment is Version A, the second is Version B."

Option 2: Paste both into the conversation. For shorter documents, you can paste the text directly. Use clear delimiters:

"=== VERSION A (Original) ===
[paste text]
=== VERSION B (Revised) ===
[paste text]"

Option 3: One file, one paste. If one version is a PDF and the other is text you have handy, upload the PDF and paste the other version in the prompt. Just be explicit about which is which.

The Output Format That Works Best

I always ask for a structured format. Free-form paragraph comparison is hard to scan. Here's what I request:

"Present each change as:
- Section/Clause: [where it is]
- Was: [original language]
- Now: [new language]
- Impact: [plain English explanation of what this means]
- Favors: [which party benefits]"

This turns a document comparison into a decision-ready brief. I can scan it in two minutes, star the items that need discussion, and skip everything else.

Try This

Find two versions of any document you've worked on recently — a contract, a proposal, a blog post, even two versions of a landing page. Upload both to Claude and ask specifically for substantive changes only, with plain-English explanations of impact. If you've never done this before, the amount of time it saves will genuinely surprise you. And you'll catch things you would have missed reading them side by side.

Module 8 — Lesson 77

Combining Structured Data (CSV) With Claude for Instant Analysis

The AI Prompting Course

A couple months ago, Ben and I were trying to figure out which of our free trial users were most likely to convert to paid. We had the data — a CSV export from our database with about 2,000 rows. Login frequency, features used, time on platform, company size, source channel. All of it sitting in a spreadsheet.

In the old world, I'd have either spent half a day in Google Sheets writing formulas and making pivot tables, or I'd have asked a data person to run the analysis. Instead, I pasted the CSV into Claude and asked a question in plain English. Three minutes later, I had a segmentation analysis that identified our three highest-converting user profiles and the specific behaviors that predicted conversion.

That's what this lesson is about. Not replacing your data tools — but using Claude as the fastest path from raw data to actionable insight when you don't need a full analytics pipeline.

How to Feed Claude a CSV

You have three options, and which one you use depends on the size of the data.

Small datasets (under 100 rows): Just paste it directly into the conversation. Copy the CSV from your spreadsheet or text file and drop it in. Claude reads tabular data natively. You don't need to format it or convert it — raw CSV with commas works fine.

Medium datasets (100-1,000 rows): Upload it as a file. Claude can read CSV and Excel files directly. This is cleaner than pasting and works better for larger tables because Claude can reference specific rows and columns without the text getting cluttered in the conversation.

Large datasets (1,000+ rows): This is where you need to be strategic. Claude can handle large files, but the analysis quality depends on what you're asking. For straightforward queries ("what's the average order value by channel?"), it handles large datasets fine. For nuanced pattern recognition, you might want to pre-filter in your spreadsheet and give Claude a focused subset.

The Prompt Structure That Gets Real Analysis

Here's where most people go wrong. They paste in data and say "Analyze this." That's like handing someone a phone book and saying "Tell me something interesting." You'll get a surface-level summary that restates the obvious.

The structure that works:

"Here's a CSV export of our customer transactions from Q1. Each row is one transaction. Columns are: customer_id, date, product, amount, channel, and region.

Context: We're trying to decide whether to double down on our West Coast sales team or invest more in our digital ads channel. We have budget for one but not both.

Analyze this data and tell me: (1) Revenue by channel and region, (2) Average deal size by channel, (3) Which channel is growing faster month-over-month, (4) Which channel has better unit economics when you factor in the number of transactions needed to hit the same revenue, and (5) Your recommendation given that we can only invest in one."

See what I'm doing? I'm giving Claude the schema, the business context, the specific decision I'm facing, and the exact questions I need answered. The analysis I get back is directly tied to a real decision, not just a data summary.

Things Claude Does Shockingly Well With Data

Segmentation: "Group these customers into segments based on their behavior patterns and give each segment a descriptive name." Claude will identify clusters you might not have thought to look for.

Anomaly detection: "Flag any rows where the values seem unusual compared to the rest of the dataset." It'll catch the $50,000 transaction in a dataset where the average is $500, or the customer who logged in 200 times in one week.

Narrative summaries: "Write a one-paragraph executive summary of what this data shows, aimed at a board member who has five minutes." Claude translates numbers into story better than most analysts I've worked with.

Cross-referencing: "Here's our customer list and here's our support ticket data. Match them and tell me which customers have the highest support burden relative to their contract value." Two datasets, one question, instant insight.

What Claude Doesn't Do Well (Yet)

Let me be straight with you. Claude is interpreting data, not running it through a statistical engine. It's great at approximate analysis, pattern recognition, and generating hypotheses. It's not the right tool for:

  • Precise statistical tests (p-values, regression coefficients) — use Python or R for that
  • Visualizations — Claude can describe what a chart should look like, but you need another tool to actually make it
  • Real-time data that changes frequently — Claude analyzes a snapshot, it doesn't connect to your database

Think of Claude as the analyst who looks at the data and tells you what it means. Think of your spreadsheet or database tool as the calculator that gives you exact numbers. Use both.

My Go-To Data Analysis Prompts

"Look at this sales data and tell me which deals I'm most likely to close this month based on patterns from deals that closed in previous months."
"Here's our expense report for Q1. Categorize every line item and flag anything that looks like it could be cut without affecting operations."
"Analyze this NPS survey data. Don't just give me the average score — identify the themes in the open-ended responses that correlate with high and low scores."

Try This

Export a CSV from whatever tool you use most — your CRM, your analytics platform, your accounting software. Something you'd normally analyze in a spreadsheet. Paste it into Claude with a specific business question you're trying to answer. Not "analyze this data" but "I'm deciding whether to [specific decision] — what does this data suggest?" The combination of raw data plus a real question is where the magic happens.

Module 8 — Lesson 78

How to Feed Claude a Competitor's Website and Get a Strategic Breakdown

The AI Prompting Course

When we were positioning Official AI against established players in our space, I spent a ridiculous amount of time on competitor websites. Reading their landing pages, their pricing pages, their about pages, their blog. Trying to decode their positioning, figure out who they were targeting, and find the gaps we could exploit.

It was valuable work, but it was slow. Hours of reading and note-taking for each competitor. Then I realized I could have Claude do the heavy lifting — not to replace my strategic thinking, but to accelerate the information gathering and pattern recognition so I could spend my time on the actual decision-making.

Here's exactly how I do it now.

Capturing the Website Content

Claude can't browse the internet on its own, so you need to get the content to it. There are a few approaches that work:

Screenshot approach: Take screenshots of the competitor's key pages — homepage, pricing page, features page, about page. Drop them into Claude one at a time. This is fastest and captures the visual design, not just the words. You get analysis of layout, hierarchy, and visual messaging in addition to the copy itself.

Copy-paste approach: Select all the text from their key pages and paste it into Claude. This loses the visual design but gives Claude the complete text to work with, including small print and footer content that screenshots might cut off.

Hybrid approach (what I usually do): Screenshot the homepage for visual analysis, then copy-paste the text from the pricing page and features page for detailed comparison. Best of both worlds.

The Prompt That Gets You a Real Strategic Breakdown

Don't just ask "What do you think about this website?" That's going to get you a design critique, not competitive intelligence.

Here's what I use:

"I'm analyzing a competitor's website. My company is [one sentence about what you do]. I'm uploading their homepage and pricing page.

Give me a strategic breakdown:
1. WHO are they targeting? (specific buyer persona, company size, industry)
2. WHAT is their core positioning? (how do they want to be perceived?)
3. HOW are they differentiating? (what do they claim makes them different?)
4. What OBJECTIONS are they preemptively addressing in their copy?
5. What PRICING PSYCHOLOGY are they using? (anchoring, decoys, tier structure)
6. What GAPS do you see? (what audience needs are they NOT addressing, what claims feel weak or unsubstantiated?)
7. Based on all of this, what positioning angle could I take that would directly counter their strongest claims?"

This prompt works because each question builds on the last. By the time Claude gets to question 7, it's synthesized a complete picture of the competitor and is thinking strategically about how you fit in.

Going Deeper: Messaging Teardown

For our most direct competitors, I go even deeper on the messaging. Here's a follow-up prompt I use after the initial breakdown:

"Now focus specifically on their messaging hierarchy. What's the first thing a visitor reads? What's the primary value proposition above the fold? What's the supporting proof? What emotional triggers are they using? Are they selling on fear, aspiration, or pain? Identify the three strongest lines of copy on this page and explain why they work. Then identify the three weakest and explain what's wrong with them."

This turns Claude into a copywriter who's reverse-engineering the competitor's playbook. I've used this to identify messaging angles that competitors were leaving on the table — pain points they weren't addressing, proof points they weren't leveraging, audience segments they were ignoring.

The Competitive Matrix Prompt

When I need to compare multiple competitors at once, I build what I call a competitive intelligence matrix. I'll analyze two or three competitors in the same conversation:

"I've given you content from three competitor websites: Competitor A, Competitor B, and Competitor C. Create a competitive matrix comparing them across these dimensions: target audience, core value proposition, pricing model, primary differentiator, messaging tone, biggest weakness. Then add a row for my company with your recommendation for how I should position in each dimension to stand out from all three."

What comes back is essentially the competitive analysis slide for your pitch deck, built in three minutes instead of three days.

What to Do With This Intelligence

Analysis without action is just trivia. Here's how I turn competitive breakdowns into actual decisions:

  • Update your landing page copy to address the gaps you found in competitors' messaging
  • Train your sales team on the objections competitors are preemptively addressing — those are the concerns your prospects have
  • Adjust your pricing page based on the psychology you decoded from their tier structure
  • Create content targeting the audience segments competitors are ignoring
  • Refine your pitch deck with positioning that directly counters their strongest claims

One More Thing

I do this analysis quarterly. Competitors change their websites. They pivot their messaging. They launch new features. What was true about their positioning six months ago might not be true today. Set a calendar reminder to re-run this analysis every quarter. Takes fifteen minutes each time and keeps you from making strategic decisions based on stale intelligence.

Try This

Pick your most direct competitor. Screenshot their homepage and pricing page. Drop both into Claude with the strategic breakdown prompt from above, customized with a one-sentence description of your own company. Read the output and I guarantee you'll find at least one positioning angle you hadn't considered and at least one weakness you can exploit. Then do it again with your second biggest competitor. The patterns across multiple competitors are often more valuable than any single analysis.

Lesson 78 of 100

Module 8 — Lesson 79

Using Claude With a Transcript to Extract Decisions, Action Items, and Next Steps

The AI Prompting Course

I have a confession. For the first year of running Mudita Studios, I was terrible at meeting follow-ups. I'd hop on a call with a client or my co-founder Ben, we'd talk for forty-five minutes, make a bunch of decisions, agree on next steps, and then... nothing got written down. Two days later I'd be trying to remember — did we decide to go with Option A or Option B? Who was supposed to send the brief? When was the deadline?

This is such a common problem. Meetings happen, good ideas get discussed, decisions get made — and then they evaporate because nobody captured them properly. Meeting notes, when they exist, are either so detailed that nobody reads them or so vague that they're useless.

Now every important meeting gets recorded, transcribed, and processed through Claude. And the output isn't "meeting notes." It's a structured decision log that tells everyone exactly what was agreed and who's doing what.

Getting the Transcript

First, you need a transcript. A few options:

  • Zoom/Google Meet transcripts: Both platforms generate automatic transcripts. Quality is decent. Download the transcript after the call.
  • Otter.ai or Fireflies: These integrate with your video conferencing tool and transcribe in real time. Better quality than built-in options.
  • Manual recording + transcription: Record the audio, run it through Whisper or another transcription tool.
  • Voice memos for in-person meetings: If you're meeting in person, open a voice recording app with permission. Transcribe after.

Don't worry about transcript quality being perfect. Claude is remarkably good at understanding messy transcripts — it handles cross-talk, filler words, incomplete sentences, and even moderate transcription errors without issues.

The Extraction Prompt

Here's where the magic happens. Don't just say "Summarize this meeting." That gets you a generic recap. Instead, tell Claude exactly what you need extracted:

"Here's the transcript from a 45-minute product planning meeting between me (Kayan, founder) and Ben (co-founder, technical lead). Extract the following:

1. DECISIONS MADE: Every explicit decision, stated as a clear sentence. Include who made or proposed the decision.
2. ACTION ITEMS: Every task that someone agreed to do, with the responsible person and any deadline mentioned.
3. OPEN QUESTIONS: Things that were discussed but not resolved — questions that still need answers.
4. KEY INSIGHTS: Any important observations or ideas that came up that aren't decisions or action items but should be remembered.
5. DISAGREEMENTS: Any points where we had different opinions, even if we resolved them. I want to track these."

That last category — disagreements — is something most meeting summaries miss entirely. But it's incredibly valuable. Understanding where your team has friction helps you anticipate problems before they become blockers.

Why This Is Better Than Taking Notes During the Meeting

Here's the thing. When someone is taking notes during a meeting, they're splitting their attention between listening and writing. They miss nuance. They capture what they think is important in the moment, which isn't always what turns out to be important later.

When Claude processes the full transcript after the fact, it has everything. Every side comment, every "oh, and one more thing," every clarification that happened twenty minutes into the call. I've had Claude surface action items from offhand comments that I definitely would have missed if I was taking notes in real time.

Real example: in a recent client call, there was a moment where the client said, almost as an aside, "Yeah, and our CEO is going to want to see this before we can sign off." Nobody flagged that in the meeting. It wasn't on anyone's action item list. But Claude caught it and listed it under "Key Insights: Client requires CEO approval for sign-off, which may extend the timeline." That one insight saved us from being blindsided by a two-week delay.

The Follow-Up Email Generator

Once I have the extracted decisions and action items, I use a second prompt to turn them into a follow-up email:

"Based on the decisions and action items you extracted, write a follow-up email to all meeting participants. Keep it brief — bullet points, not paragraphs. Tone should be friendly but clear. Start with the decisions, then list action items with owners and deadlines, then flag any open questions that need to be resolved before our next check-in. End with the date and time of our next meeting if one was mentioned in the transcript."

This entire pipeline — transcript to extraction to follow-up email — takes me about five minutes. It used to take thirty minutes of note-reviewing and email-drafting, and the output is better because nothing falls through the cracks.

For Sales Calls Specifically

If you're processing sales call transcripts, use a different extraction framework:

"This is a sales call transcript with a prospective client. Extract: (1) Their stated pain points, (2) Their budget and timeline signals, (3) Their decision-making process and who else is involved, (4) Objections they raised (explicit or implied), (5) What they seemed most excited about, (6) Suggested follow-up actions to move this deal forward."

This gives your sales team a structured deal intelligence brief instead of a generic call summary. I've seen this single workflow improve follow-up quality and close rates because reps are acting on actual buyer signals instead of memory.

Try This

Record your next meeting (with permission). Get the transcript afterward. Paste it into Claude with the five-part extraction prompt from above. Then compare what Claude extracted to what you remembered from the meeting. I'm willing to bet Claude catches at least two action items and one decision that you'd forgotten about by the time the meeting ended. That's the value — it's not just documentation, it's organizational memory.

Lesson 79 of 100

Module 8 — Lesson 80

Multi-Document Synthesis: Feeding 5 Sources and Asking for a Single Coherent Narrative

The AI Prompting Course

This is the lesson where everything in Module 8 comes together. We've talked about individual inputs — images, PDFs, CSVs, transcripts, reference files. Now I want to show you what happens when you combine multiple sources into a single analysis. Because that's where AI stops being a productivity tool and starts being something closer to a thinking partner.

Let me give you a real example. A few weeks ago, I needed to put together a board update for Official AI. I needed to synthesize: (1) our Stripe revenue dashboard, (2) our product usage analytics from PostHog, (3) notes from three customer calls, (4) a competitive landscape analysis I'd written the previous quarter, and (5) our current roadmap document.

In the old world, I'd spend an entire day reading through all of this, pulling threads together, and writing a coherent narrative. With Claude, I fed in all five sources and asked it to weave them into a single story. The first draft took about four minutes. The final version, after two rounds of refinement, took twenty minutes total. And honestly? It was better than what I would have written alone, because Claude caught connections between the data sources that I hadn't seen.

Why Multi-Document Synthesis Is Hard (For Humans)

Here's the thing about working with multiple sources. Each one lives in its own format, its own context, its own frame of reference. Revenue data is in numbers. Customer call notes are in conversational language. Your roadmap is in strategic terms. Your competitive analysis is in market language.

The hard work isn't reading any one of them. The hard work is connecting them. Seeing that the revenue dip in Week 3 correlates with the feature complaint from Customer Call #2, which relates to the roadmap gap you identified, which your competitor just shipped. That cross-source pattern recognition is what turns raw information into insight.

Humans are actually good at this — but only when the volume is manageable. When you're dealing with five, ten, twenty sources, the cognitive load overwhelms you and you start cherry-picking the data that confirms what you already believe. Claude doesn't have that bias. It reads everything with equal attention.

The Setup: How to Structure a Multi-Source Prompt

The biggest mistake I see is people dumping all five sources into Claude with no structure and saying "Synthesize this." What they get back is a random mashup of information from different sources. Technically it addresses everything. Practically it's a mess.

Here's the structure that works:

Step 1: Label your sources clearly.

"I'm going to give you five sources of information. Read all of them before responding.

SOURCE 1 — Revenue Data: [paste or upload]
SOURCE 2 — Product Usage Metrics: [paste or upload]
SOURCE 3 — Customer Call Notes: [paste or upload]
SOURCE 4 — Competitive Analysis: [paste or upload]
SOURCE 5 — Product Roadmap: [paste or upload]"

Step 2: Define the output you want. Don't let Claude decide how to organize this. Tell it.

"Now synthesize these into a board update narrative with the following structure:
- Executive Summary (3 sentences max)
- Revenue and Growth: What's happening with our numbers and why
- Product and Usage: What customers are actually doing and how that connects to revenue
- Voice of the Customer: What customers are saying and how it aligns (or doesn't) with our roadmap
- Competitive Landscape: How the market is shifting and how we're positioned
- Risks and Concerns: Anything across these sources that should worry us
- Recommended Priorities: Based on ALL of this data, what should we focus on next quarter"

Step 3: Specify the connections you want Claude to make. This is the secret sauce.

"As you write, explicitly connect insights across sources. If revenue data shows a trend that customer feedback explains, link them. If our roadmap addresses something a competitor just launched, note it. I don't want five separate summaries — I want one integrated narrative where the sources talk to each other."

That third instruction is what transforms the output from a summary into a synthesis. Without it, you get five mini-summaries stapled together. With it, you get genuine cross-source insight.

Managing the Context Window

Five documents is a lot of text. Here's how I manage it practically:

  • Pre-summarize large sources: If one of your sources is a 40-page report, don't upload the whole thing. Summarize it in a separate conversation first, then use the summary as the source.
  • Use the most relevant sections: You don't need the entire competitive analysis — just the executive summary and the section about your direct competitors.
  • Upload key documents, paste smaller data: For the mix of formats, upload PDFs and CSVs as files, and paste shorter text (like call notes) directly in the prompt.
  • Prioritize your sources: Tell Claude which sources are most important. "Source 1 (revenue data) and Source 3 (customer feedback) are the most critical. Sources 2, 4, and 5 provide supporting context."

Use Cases Beyond Board Updates

I use multi-document synthesis for all kinds of things:

Client strategy presentations: Combine market research + client's business data + competitive intel + past campaign results into a single strategic recommendation.

Content research: Feed in five articles on the same topic and ask Claude to identify the consensus view, the contrarian takes, the gaps nobody is covering, and the unique angle you should write about.

Due diligence: When evaluating a potential partner or vendor, feed in their website content, customer reviews, social media presence, financial data, and any press coverage. Ask for a unified assessment.

Quarterly planning: Combine last quarter's OKR scorecard, team retrospective notes, customer feedback summary, revenue data, and market trends into a single "here's what happened, here's what it means, here's what we should do" document.

The Quality Check

One word of caution. When Claude synthesizes multiple sources, it can sometimes smooth over contradictions rather than highlighting them. A customer might say one thing and the data might show the opposite, and Claude might present a narrative that kind of papers over the conflict.

To prevent this, I always add:

"If any of these sources contradict each other, call that out explicitly. Don't resolve the contradiction — flag it so I can investigate."

The contradictions are often the most valuable findings. They tell you where your assumptions might be wrong.

Try This

Think of a decision you're currently trying to make — could be a business strategy call, a product priority, a content direction. Gather three to five relevant sources: data exports, documents, notes, competitor info, whatever's relevant. Feed them all to Claude using the labeling and structuring format from this lesson. Ask for a single integrated narrative that connects the sources and ends with a recommendation. That final document is something that would take you hours to write from scratch. Claude gives you a draft in minutes — and often a better one, because it's connecting dots across sources without the cognitive fatigue that makes humans miss patterns.

Lesson 80 of 100

Module 9 — Lesson 81

The PCT Framework: Building Problem/Credibility/Transformation Narratives With Claude

The AI Prompting Course

Every piece of marketing that has ever worked — every landing page, every ad, every pitch deck — follows the same basic structure. It identifies a problem, establishes why you should be trusted to solve it, and then paints the picture of what life looks like after. Problem. Credibility. Transformation. PCT.

I didn't come up with this. It's basically the backbone of every direct response copywriter who's ever existed. But here's what I did figure out: Claude is absurdly good at building these narratives once you teach it the framework explicitly. And most people never bother to do that.

They just say "write me some marketing copy" and hope for the best. That's like handing someone a guitar and saying "play something good" without telling them the key, the tempo, or the genre.

How PCT Actually Works

Let me break this down with a real example. When we were building the positioning for Official AI, I needed copy that spoke to operators — people running businesses who were drowning in manual work but skeptical of AI tools because they'd been burned by overpromising before.

Problem: You're spending 15+ hours a week on tasks that should take minutes. Your team is growing but your processes aren't scaling. You've tried automation tools and they broke the moment something unexpected happened.

Credibility: We've built AI systems for companies processing 10,000+ customer interactions daily. Our founder ran an agency and felt this exact pain before building the solution.

Transformation: Imagine your ops team focusing on strategy instead of data entry. Imagine onboarding a new client in hours, not weeks. That's what happens when the boring stuff runs itself.

See how each section does a different job? The problem creates recognition — the reader thinks "that's me." The credibility creates trust — "okay, these people get it." The transformation creates desire — "I want that."

Teaching Claude the Framework

Here's where it gets powerful. Instead of writing this from scratch every time, you teach Claude the PCT structure and then feed it raw material. The prompt looks like this:

"I need you to build a PCT narrative — Problem, Credibility, Transformation — for the following product. Here's the framework: Problem section: Identify the 2-3 most painful problems our target audience faces. Use specific, visceral language. The reader should feel seen — like we've been watching their Tuesday afternoon. No generic pain points. Credibility section: Use concrete proof points — numbers, experience, specific results. No vague claims like 'industry-leading.' Real things that make someone think 'okay, they've actually done this.' Transformation section: Paint the after picture. Not features — outcomes. What does their day look like? What stress disappears? What becomes possible that wasn't before? Here's the raw material: [paste your product info, customer research, testimonials, whatever you have] Target audience: [who specifically] Write three versions — one that leads heavy on emotion, one that leads heavy on logic, one that balances both."

That prompt does more work than most people's entire marketing process. You're not just asking for copy. You're giving Claude a strategic framework, raw inputs, audience context, and variation parameters all at once.

Why Three Versions Matter

I always ask for at least three. Here's why. You don't know which angle will resonate until you see them side by side. The emotional version might nail the problem section but feel weak on credibility. The logical version might have incredible proof points but read like a case study instead of marketing copy. The balanced one might be the winner, or it might be a Frankenstein of the best parts of the other two.

The point is, Claude generates the range and you make the editorial decision. That's the right division of labor.

The Mistake People Make

I see this all the time — people feed Claude a PCT prompt but give it zero raw material. They'll say "write a PCT narrative for a project management SaaS" and expect magic. Claude will produce something, sure. But it'll be generic because you gave it generic inputs.

The quality of the PCT output is directly proportional to the quality of the raw material you feed in. Customer quotes. Support tickets. Sales call transcripts. Product specs. Competitor weaknesses. The more real data Claude has, the more specific and sharp the narrative becomes.

When I built the positioning for Mudita Studios, I pasted in actual client feedback, real project timelines, specific results we'd delivered. The PCT narrative Claude built from that was better than what most copywriters would produce — because it had real evidence to work with, not just prompting magic.

Layering PCT Into Everything

Here's the thing most people miss. PCT isn't just for landing pages. Once you have your core PCT narrative, it becomes the DNA for everything. Your email sequences? PCT in miniature. Your ad hooks? The problem section, compressed. Your investor pitch? PCT with credibility turned up to eleven.

I keep a master PCT document for every product I work on. When I need any piece of marketing copy, I feed that document into Claude along with the specific format I need. The narrative stays consistent because the source material stays consistent.

Try This

Take whatever you're selling right now — a product, a service, yourself for a job, whatever. Open Claude and give it the PCT framework I described above. But before you do, spend ten minutes gathering raw material. Customer feedback, your own experience, specific numbers, real stories. Paste all of it in.

Ask for three versions. Read them. Highlight the lines that make you think "yes, that's exactly right." Those lines are your marketing foundation. Everything else builds from there.

Module 9 — Lesson 82

Battle Cry Hook Generation: 40 Variants, Ranked by CTR Prediction

The AI Prompting Course

The hook is the only thing that matters in the first two seconds. I know that sounds dramatic but it's literally true. In a paid ad, an email subject line, a LinkedIn post, a landing page hero — if the hook doesn't land, nothing else you wrote matters because nobody's reading it.

I used to write hooks one at a time. Sit there, stare at the screen, try to be clever. Maybe I'd get three or four good ones in an hour. Now I generate forty in about ninety seconds and spend my time picking the winners instead of trying to invent them from nothing.

What a Battle Cry Hook Actually Is

A battle cry hook isn't just a catchy headline. It's a statement that makes your target audience stop scrolling because it articulates something they feel but haven't been able to put into words. It's confrontational, specific, and impossible to ignore if you're the right person.

"Stop wasting money on ads that don't convert" is not a battle cry. It's generic. Everyone's heard it. It blends into the noise.

"You're spending $3,000/month on Meta ads and can't name your top-performing creative from last week" — that's a battle cry. It's specific. It stings. It makes someone think "wait, that's me."

The 40-Variant Prompt

Here's the exact prompt structure I use. I've refined this over probably a hundred rounds of ad creative generation:

"Generate 40 hook variants for the following product/offer. Each hook should be one sentence, max 15 words, and should stop the target audience mid-scroll. Product: [what you're selling] Target audience: [who, specifically — their role, their pain, their day-to-day] Core value prop: [the main thing you deliver] Emotional trigger: [what keeps them up at night about this problem] Generate the hooks in these categories (8 hooks each): 1. Pain-agitation hooks — make the problem feel urgent 2. Curiosity gap hooks — create an information gap they need to close 3. Social proof hooks — imply others are already winning 4. Contrarian hooks — challenge something they believe 5. Direct benefit hooks — state the outcome plainly After generating all 40, rank your top 10 by predicted click-through rate and explain your reasoning for each ranking."

That last part is crucial. When you ask Claude to rank and explain, it forces a second pass of critical thinking on its own output. It's like having a copywriter write the hooks and then having a media buyer review them — except it's the same brain doing both passes.

Why Categories Matter

I didn't always use categories. I used to just say "give me 40 hooks" and what I'd get back was basically the same hook written forty different ways. Slight word variations, same underlying angle. Useless.

The categories force diversity. A pain-agitation hook works completely differently than a curiosity gap hook. They appeal to different psychological triggers. By forcing Claude to spread across five categories, you get genuine variety instead of forty versions of the same sentence.

And here's what I've found from actually running these: the winners almost never come from the category you'd expect. I'll go in thinking "the pain hooks will crush it" and then a contrarian hook outperforms everything by 3x. You can't predict it. But you can make sure you have options in every lane.

The Ranking Conversation

After Claude gives me the initial 40 with rankings, I push back. This is where the real value comes from:

"Your top 10 ranking skews heavily toward direct benefit hooks. In my experience running Meta ads for this audience, curiosity gaps tend to outperform on cold traffic. Re-rank with that context. Also, hooks 7 and 23 feel too similar — pick the stronger one and replace the weaker with something from a category that's underrepresented in the top 10."

Now we're having a conversation. Claude isn't just generating — it's iterating based on your real-world data. This is the difference between using AI as a copy machine and using it as a strategic collaborator.

What I Actually Do With the Output

I take the final top 10, drop them into a spreadsheet, and test 3-5 as primary text in Meta ads against the same creative. Same audience, same image or video, different hooks. Within 48 hours I usually have a clear winner, and that winner becomes the template for the next round of creative.

Then I go back to Claude with the performance data:

"Hook A got a 2.1% CTR, Hook B got 1.4%, Hook C got 3.8%. Hook C was a curiosity gap: '[the winning hook].' Generate 15 more variants that follow the same structural pattern as Hook C but explore different angles of the same core message."

That's the loop. Generate, test, feed back results, generate more. Each cycle gets sharper because Claude has actual performance data to learn from, not just theory.

The Constraint That Makes It Work

The 15-word max constraint in the original prompt is non-negotiable for me. Without it, Claude will give you beautiful, flowing sentences that work great in a blog post and die on a Meta ad. Hooks need to be compressed. Punchy. The kind of thing someone can absorb in a glance while their thumb is already moving.

If you're writing for a different format — email subject lines, maybe 7 words. YouTube thumbnails, 5 words. LinkedIn posts, the first line can be longer but the hook still needs to hit in under 10 words. Adjust the constraint to the medium.

Try This

Pick a product or offer you're working on right now. Run the 40-variant prompt with all five categories. Don't skip the ranking step — that's where you learn how Claude thinks about hook quality. Read all 40, mark your own top 10, and compare them to Claude's rankings. Where you disagree is where the interesting strategic conversation starts.

Module 9 — Lesson 83

Split-Audience Messaging: Writing the Same Thing for Investors vs. Operators

The AI Prompting Course

One of the hardest things in marketing — and honestly in business communication in general — is saying the same thing to two completely different audiences. I deal with this constantly. When I'm talking about Official AI, the story I tell an investor is fundamentally different from the story I tell an operator who's going to actually use the product. Same company. Same product. Completely different narratives.

And here's the thing: most people try to find one message that works for everyone and end up with a message that works for no one. It's the classic "if you're talking to everyone, you're talking to no one" problem. But rewriting everything from scratch for each audience is brutally time-consuming.

This is where Claude becomes genuinely powerful. Not as a copywriter — as a translator between worldviews.

The Core Concept

An investor and an operator look at the same product and see completely different things. The investor sees market size, defensibility, growth trajectory, unit economics. The operator sees workflow improvement, time saved, pain removed, integration with their existing stack.

The product hasn't changed. The lens has.

Your job when writing split-audience messaging is to take one truth about your product and refract it through each audience's lens. And you need to do it without dumbing anything down or making either audience feel like they're reading someone else's marketing.

The Prompt That Makes This Work

Here's how I set this up in Claude:

"I need to communicate the same core value proposition to two different audiences. Here's the raw truth about what we do: [Paste your core product/company description — be specific and honest, not marketing-speak] Audience A: Early-stage investors (Series A). They care about market size, competitive moats, growth metrics, and founder-market fit. They're pattern-matching against other successful investments. They're skeptical of hype and want to see evidence of traction. Audience B: Operations leaders at mid-market companies (50-500 employees). They care about implementation time, integration with existing tools, team adoption, and measurable ROI within 90 days. They've been burned by tools that looked great in demos and fell apart in practice. For each audience, write: 1. A one-paragraph positioning statement 2. Three key messages (one sentence each) 3. The proof points that would matter most to them 4. The objection they're most likely to have, and the response Keep both versions honest and consistent — they should clearly be about the same product. But the emphasis, language, and framing should be completely different."

What comes back is usually remarkably good. The investor version will lead with market dynamics and traction metrics. The operator version will lead with "here's what your Monday morning looks like before and after." Same product, refracted correctly.

Why This Is Hard Without AI

I used to try to do this manually and I'd always drift. I'd start writing the investor version and accidentally slip into operator language because that's how I actually think about the product day-to-day. Or I'd write the operator version and it would start sounding like a pitch deck because I'd just finished working on fundraising materials.

Claude doesn't have that drift problem. When you define the two audiences clearly, it can maintain separation between them perfectly. It holds both frames in mind simultaneously in a way that's genuinely hard for a single human brain to do — especially when you're the founder and you're emotionally attached to every angle of the product.

The Real-World Application

Let me give you a concrete example. When we were positioning Mudita Studios, I needed messaging for two audiences: potential clients who needed AI solutions built, and potential talent who we wanted to recruit.

Same company. But a client wants to hear "we'll build the thing that makes your business run better." A recruiter candidate wants to hear "you'll work on interesting problems with smart people and ship things that matter."

I fed Claude our company background, recent projects, team culture, and technical capabilities. Then I asked for split messaging. What came back was a client-facing version that emphasized reliability, delivery speed, and measurable outcomes — and a recruiting version that emphasized technical challenge, creative autonomy, and impact. Both were true. Neither would have worked for the other audience.

Going Beyond Two Audiences

Once you get comfortable with split messaging for two audiences, try three or four. I've done this for product launches where I needed messaging for:

  • End users (the people clicking buttons)
  • Technical decision makers (the CTO evaluating your API docs)
  • Budget holders (the VP who signs the check)
  • Champions (the internal advocate who has to sell it up the chain)

Each of those people needs to hear something different. The end user needs simplicity. The CTO needs technical depth. The budget holder needs ROI. The champion needs ammunition — talking points they can use in an internal meeting.

Claude handles this beautifully when you define each audience clearly and give it real material to work with. The key is being as specific as possible about what each audience cares about and what their decision-making criteria actually look like.

The Consistency Check

After generating split messaging, I always run one more prompt:

"Read the messaging for all audiences side by side. Flag any inconsistencies — places where the product is described differently in ways that would be confusing if someone from Audience A talked to someone from Audience B. Make sure the core truth is the same, even though the emphasis is different."

This matters more than you think. If your investor messaging says "we're a platform" and your operator messaging says "we're a tool," someone's going to notice. Claude catches these inconsistencies faster than I do because it can hold all the versions in context simultaneously.

Try This

Pick your product or company and identify two audiences that matter to you right now. Not hypothetical ones — the two groups you're actually trying to reach this month. Run the split-messaging prompt above with real details about each audience. When you read the output, pay attention to which version feels more natural to you — that's probably the audience you understand best. The other version is where Claude is adding the most value.

Module 9 — Lesson 84

Landing Page Copy at Scale: One Brief → 41 Variants

The AI Prompting Course

I'm going to walk you through something I actually did last quarter that saved me probably forty hours of work. I had one product brief and I needed landing page copy for forty-one different audience segments. Not slightly different. Meaningfully different. Different pain points, different proof points, different CTAs. Forty-one versions.

Before AI, that's a month of copywriting work. With Claude, it was an afternoon. And the quality wasn't "good enough" — several versions outperformed the copy our team had written manually for the main page.

The Setup: Your Master Brief

Everything starts with the brief. And I mean a real brief, not a sentence. This is the document that Claude will reference for every single variant, so it needs to be comprehensive. Here's what I include:

  • Product description — what it does, how it works, what makes it different
  • Core value propositions — the 3-5 main reasons someone would buy
  • Proof points — numbers, case studies, testimonials, anything concrete
  • Brand voice guidelines — how we sound, what we never say
  • Page structure — hero section, problem section, solution section, proof section, CTA
  • Conversion goal — what specific action we want people to take

I paste all of this into Claude at the start of the conversation and say:

"This is the master brief for a landing page. I'm going to ask you to generate multiple variants of this page for different audience segments. For each variant, the page structure stays the same but the messaging, examples, and emphasis should be tailored to the specific audience. Don't change the product — change how we talk about it. Confirm you understand the brief before we start."

That confirmation step matters. It forces Claude to process the brief rather than just acknowledge it. I usually get back a summary of what it understood, and I can correct anything before we start generating.

The Variant Generation Loop

Then I feed it audience segments one at a time, or in batches of three to five:

"Variant 1: E-commerce founders doing $1-5M annual revenue who are running Meta ads themselves because they can't afford an agency. They're overwhelmed by the complexity and afraid of wasting money. They've probably tried boosting posts and gotten burned. Write the full landing page copy for this segment following the master brief structure."

Each variant gets its own detailed audience description. The more specific you are about the audience, the more differentiated the copy becomes. If you just say "e-commerce founders," you'll get generic copy. If you describe their Tuesday afternoon — staying up late tweaking ad sets, watching ROAS fluctuate, wondering if they should just hire someone — Claude writes to that person specifically.

The Quality Control Pass

Here's where most people stop, and here's where I keep going. After generating every five or ten variants, I run a quality control prompt:

"Review the last five variants. For each one: 1. Is the messaging genuinely differentiated from the others, or are we falling into repetitive patterns? 2. Does the hero section hook match the specific audience's primary pain point? 3. Are the proof points relevant to this segment or generic? 4. Would this page feel personalized if the target reader landed on it, or does it feel like a template with words swapped out? Be honest. If any variant is lazy, flag it and I'll give you more audience context to work with."

This is the secret sauce. Without this step, variant twenty starts to blur into variant five. Claude drifts toward its comfort zone and starts recycling phrases. The quality check catches this and forces it back into genuine differentiation.

Why 41 and Not 5

You might be thinking "why do I need forty-one variants?" Fair question. You might not. But here's my reasoning.

If you're running paid traffic, every audience segment you target should land on a page that speaks directly to them. A SaaS founder and a freelance designer have completely different relationships to your product, even if they both need it. When you match the message to the segment, conversion rates go up — I've seen 30-60% lifts from message matching alone.

At scale, this is the difference between one landing page that converts at 2% across all traffic and forty-one pages that each convert at 4-6% for their specific segment. The math is obvious. The bottleneck was always the cost of producing that much copy. AI removes that bottleneck.

The Template Trap

One thing to watch out for. It's tempting to create a "template" with brackets — "Welcome, [audience type]! Are you struggling with [pain point]?" — and just fill in the blanks. Don't do this. It produces copy that reads like a mail merge. Humans can smell it immediately and it kills trust.

Each variant should read like it was written specifically for that audience from scratch. That means the sentence structures should vary, the metaphors should be different, the proof points should be chosen for relevance rather than just included because they're in the brief. Claude can do this — but only if you explicitly tell it not to use template-style substitution.

The Post-Generation Workflow

After I have all variants, I do three things:

First, I read the hero sections of all forty-one back to back. If any two feel too similar, I regenerate the weaker one with more specific audience context.

Second, I check CTA alignment. Every CTA should feel like the natural conclusion of that specific page's narrative, not a generic "Get Started" bolted on at the end.

Third, I pick the five strongest variants and have Claude write three A/B test headlines for each. Those go into the testing queue first while the remaining variants roll out with their primary copy.

Try This

You don't need to start with forty-one. Start with three. Pick three meaningfully different audience segments for something you're selling. Write a thorough master brief, feed it to Claude, and generate three variants. Read them side by side. If they feel genuinely different — like three different writers who each understand their specific reader — you've got the process down. Scale from there.

Module 9 — Lesson 85

Subheadline Compression: The "5 Words Max, Make It Punch" Iteration Loop

The AI Prompting Course

There's a specific kind of writing that AI struggles with by default, and it's the kind that matters most in marketing: extremely compressed copy. Five words or fewer. A subheadline that stops you. A button label that converts. A tagline that sticks.

Claude's natural tendency is to explain. It wants to give you the full picture, the nuance, the complete thought. That's great for a lot of things. It's terrible for a subheadline that needs to land in under two seconds.

But here's what I've figured out: you can build an iteration loop that compresses Claude's output down to something tight and punchy. It just takes a specific process.

The Problem With Asking Directly

If you say "write me a subheadline, five words max" — Claude will give you something grammatically correct and completely forgettable. "Simplify your work today." "Better results, less effort." These are word-count compliant but they're empty. They could be for literally any product.

The issue is that compression without context produces generic output. You need to give Claude the full thought first, and then systematically compress it.

The Expansion-Compression Loop

Here's the exact process I use. Four steps.

Step 1: The full thought. Start by asking Claude to articulate the value proposition in two or three full sentences. No word limit. Let it breathe.

"Explain the core value proposition of [product] for [audience] in 2-3 sentences. Be specific and concrete. Focus on the single most compelling benefit."

You'll get something like: "Operations teams spend an average of 12 hours per week on manual data entry that could be fully automated. Our platform eliminates that work entirely, giving your team back time to focus on strategic decisions that actually grow the business."

Step 2: Compress to one sentence.

"Compress that into one sentence. Keep the most emotionally resonant element. Lose everything else."

Now you get: "Get 12 hours back every week by automating the work your team hates."

Step 3: Compress to a phrase.

"Now compress to 5 words or fewer. This is a subheadline — it needs to punch, not explain. Give me 10 options."

Now you get things like: "Kill the busywork." "12 hours back. Weekly." "Automate the grind." "Your team deserves better." "Manual work, eliminated."

Step 4: Pressure test.

"Which of these 10 would make someone stop scrolling? Be ruthless. Pick the top 3 and explain why they work better than the others. Then generate 5 more that are even sharper."

Why the Loop Matters

Each step in this loop does something specific. The expansion step ensures Claude fully understands what needs to be communicated. The first compression forces prioritization — what's the one thing? The second compression forces linguistic creativity — how do you say that one thing in five words that actually hit? The pressure test forces quality control.

If you skip straight to "give me a five-word subheadline," you're asking Claude to do all four steps internally, and it'll cut corners. By making each step explicit, you get dramatically better output.

I've tested this. Same product, same audience. Direct ask produces generic filler. The four-step loop produces lines I'd actually put on a landing page. The difference is night and day.

The Secret: Rhythm and Sound

At five words or fewer, copy isn't just about meaning anymore. It's about sound. Rhythm. How the words feel when you read them — even silently. There's a physicality to great compressed copy.

"Just do it" works because of the rhythm. Three monosyllabic words, building momentum. "Think different" works because the slight grammatical incorrectness creates friction — your brain snags on it.

After the compression loop, I add one more prompt:

"Read these finalists out loud. Which ones have the best rhythm? Which ones create a slight pause or surprise? If any feel flat or predictable when spoken aloud, replace them with versions that have more sonic texture."

This sounds precious but it works. Claude actually considers the phonetic qualities of the words and produces alternatives with harder consonants, unexpected pauses, or rhythmic variation. Copy that sounds good performs better. Period.

Where I Use This

I run this loop for subheadlines on landing pages, obviously. But also for:

  • CTA button text (the difference between "Get Started" and "Start building" is real)
  • Email subject lines that need to work in a mobile preview
  • Section headers in long-form sales pages
  • Ad headlines in the Meta Ads character-limited formats
  • Product feature names in the UI

Anywhere you have a tiny amount of space and need every word to carry weight, this loop applies.

Try This

Take your current landing page or product homepage. Find the subheadline — the line directly under the main headline. Run it through the four-step loop starting from scratch. Don't anchor on what you already have. Let Claude expand the full thought, then compress through the stages. Compare the final output to what you currently have. I bet at least two of the options Claude generates are stronger than what's live right now.

Module 9 — Lesson 86

Ad Copy That Avoids Negative Framing for the Buyer — The Sensitivity Constraint

The AI Prompting Course

There's a line in advertising that most people cross without realizing it. The line between agitating a problem and making the reader feel bad about themselves. And the difference matters way more than you think — both ethically and in terms of performance.

I learned this the hard way running ads for a client in the health and wellness space. We had hooks that agitated the pain hard: "Tired of feeling sluggish and out of shape?" "Still struggling to lose those last 10 pounds?" The CTR was decent. The conversion rate was terrible. People would click, get to the page, and bounce. Something felt off.

When we rewrote the same ads with a sensitivity constraint — no negative framing directed at the buyer — conversions doubled. People don't buy when they feel attacked. They buy when they feel understood.

What Negative Framing Looks Like

Negative framing is when the ad copy makes the reader the problem. It points at them and says "you're failing" or "you're doing it wrong" or "you're behind." Here are real examples I've seen:

  • "Stop making these embarrassing email mistakes"
  • "Why your marketing strategy is broken"
  • "You're leaving money on the table (and you probably don't even know it)"
  • "Are you still doing [thing] the old way?"

Each of these makes the reader feel dumb, behind, or inadequate. And yes, some of these hooks get clicks. But clicks aren't conversions. And even when they do convert, you've started the customer relationship on a foundation of "you were screwing up and we saved you" — which is a terrible foundation for retention and loyalty.

The Sensitivity Constraint in Prompts

When I generate ad copy with Claude now, I always include what I call the sensitivity constraint. It looks like this:

"Write ad copy for [product/audience]. Important constraint: never make the reader feel bad about themselves, their current situation, or their past decisions. You can acknowledge the problem exists — you cannot imply it's their fault or that they should have solved it already. Frame the problem as something that happens TO smart, capable people — not as evidence of a personal failing. The reader should feel seen and validated, not judged. Good: 'Managing a team's workload shouldn't require a PhD in spreadsheet gymnastics.' Bad: 'If you're still managing your team with spreadsheets, you're falling behind.' Both acknowledge the same problem. One makes the reader laugh and nod. The other makes them defensive."

That good/bad example pair in the prompt is doing a lot of heavy lifting. It gives Claude a concrete reference for the line you don't want it to cross. Without it, Claude will default to fairly aggressive pain-agitation because most marketing copy in its training data uses that approach.

Why This Works Better

Here's the psychology. When someone feels attacked, their guard goes up. They become skeptical, defensive, resistant. Even if your product is perfect for them, they're now processing your offer through a filter of "this company just insulted me."

When someone feels understood — when the copy says "hey, this thing is hard, it's not your fault, and there's a better way" — their guard drops. They lean in. They think "finally, someone gets it." And from that emotional state, they're far more likely to take action.

I've run this experiment multiple times across different niches. The sensitivity-constrained version outperforms the aggressive version on conversion rate almost every time. CTR is sometimes slightly lower — the edgy hooks get more clicks. But the math doesn't lie. Lower CTR with higher conversion rate beats higher CTR with lower conversion rate every time.

The Tricky Middle Ground

Here's where it gets nuanced. You still need to agitate the problem. If your ad copy is all sunshine and solutions, it's boring. Nobody engages with "Everything's fine but here's how to make it slightly better." People respond to tension.

The trick is to direct the tension at the situation, not the person. Let me show you what I mean:

Negative framing (directed at the person): "You're wasting 10 hours a week on manual reporting."

Sensitivity-constrained framing (directed at the situation): "Manual reporting eats 10 hours a week that your team will never get back."

Same fact. Same urgency. But the first one says "you're wasting" — it's your fault. The second one says "manual reporting eats" — the process is the villain, not the reader.

This is a subtle shift but it completely changes how the copy lands. And Claude is excellent at making this kind of adjustment once you explain the principle clearly.

Running It at Scale

When I'm generating batches of ad creative, I include the sensitivity constraint in the system-level instructions so it applies to every variant:

"For all ad copy in this session, apply the following sensitivity constraint: the reader is smart, capable, and doing their best. The problem exists because of systemic complexity, not personal failure. Never use 'you' + negative verb ('you're wasting,' 'you're missing,' 'you're failing'). Always externalize the problem. This is non-negotiable."

That "non-negotiable" at the end matters. Without it, Claude will occasionally slip back into standard marketing language that crosses the line. Being firm about the constraint keeps the output clean.

Try This

Pull up your current ad copy or landing page. Search for every instance of "you" followed by a negative — "you're losing," "you're missing," "you're stuck," "you're still." Rewrite each one with the problem externalized. Then ask Claude to generate five more hooks for the same offer with the sensitivity constraint applied. Compare the tone. You'll feel the difference immediately, and your audience will too.

Lesson 86 of 100

Module 9 — Lesson 87

Using Claude to Predict Which Hooks Will Outperform Before You Test Them

The AI Prompting Course

Let me be upfront about something: Claude cannot actually predict the future. It doesn't have access to your ad account data, it can't see your audience's behavior in real-time, and it doesn't know what's happening in the cultural moment that might make one hook land and another miss.

But here's what Claude can do — and this is genuinely useful — it can analyze hooks against well-established principles of persuasion, behavioral psychology, and advertising performance patterns. And when you give it your historical data on top of that, its predictions become surprisingly accurate. Not perfect. But good enough to cut your testing budget in half by eliminating the obvious losers before they ever spend a dollar.

The Baseline Prediction Prompt

Here's how I use this when I don't have historical performance data to work with — like when I'm launching a new product or entering a new market:

"I have 20 hook variants for a Meta ad campaign. Target audience is [specific description]. The offer is [specific offer]. Predict the relative performance of each hook, ranked from most likely to least likely to achieve a high CTR on cold traffic. For each hook, evaluate: - Specificity (does it contain a concrete detail or is it generic?) - Pattern interrupt (does it break the scroll or blend into the feed?) - Emotional trigger (what specific emotion does it activate?) - Clarity (can someone understand the value in under 3 seconds?) - Audience match (how closely does this speak to the stated target?) Score each dimension 1-5 and provide an overall prediction. Here are the hooks: [paste your 20 hooks]"

What comes back is a structured analysis that forces you to think about each hook through multiple lenses instead of just gut feel. And Claude's reasoning is usually sound — it'll flag that Hook 7 is specific but lacks a pattern interrupt, or that Hook 13 has great emotional resonance but is so vague it could be for any product.

The Historical Data Supercharge

Now here's where it gets really powerful. When I have past performance data, the predictions get significantly better:

"Here are the results from our last three rounds of hook testing: Round 1: [Hook A - 2.1% CTR, Hook B - 1.4% CTR, Hook C - 3.2% CTR] Round 2: [Hook D - 1.8% CTR, Hook E - 4.1% CTR, Hook F - 2.7% CTR] Round 3: [Hook G - 3.5% CTR, Hook H - 1.1% CTR, Hook I - 2.9% CTR] Analyze the patterns. What do the top performers have in common structurally, tonally, and emotionally? What do the underperformers share? Now, using those patterns, predict the performance ranking of these 15 new hooks for the same audience: [paste new hooks]"

This is where Claude starts functioning less like a copywriting tool and more like a data analyst. It'll identify that your winners tend to use numbers, or that they lead with a question rather than a statement, or that they reference a specific pain point while the losers are more general. Then it applies those patterns to the new set.

I've tracked Claude's predictions against actual performance across about fifty hooks over the last few months. It correctly identifies the top 3 performers about 60% of the time. That's not clairvoyance — but it's dramatically better than random, and it means you can prioritize your testing queue intelligently.

The Elimination Round

I don't actually use Claude to pick winners. I use it to eliminate losers. There's a meaningful difference.

From a batch of 40 hooks, Claude can usually identify the 10-15 that are weakest — generic, unclear, off-audience, or missing a pattern interrupt. Those go straight to the trash without spending a dollar. The remaining 25-30 go into the testing queue, and real audience behavior determines the winner.

This approach plays to Claude's strengths. It's great at spotting structural weaknesses. It's less reliable at predicting which specific angle will catch fire — that depends on market timing, creative fatigue, platform algorithm shifts, and a dozen other things Claude has no visibility into. But clearing the deadwood? It's excellent at that.

The Second-Order Prediction

There's one more layer I run that most people don't think of. After the initial prediction, I ask:

"For your predicted top 5 hooks, now predict which would have the highest conversion rate AFTER the click — not just CTR. A hook can get clicks through curiosity but fail to convert if the promise doesn't align with the landing page. Which of these hooks set up the best expectation for what the reader will find when they click?"

This catches a common trap. Curiosity gap hooks often get the highest CTR but the lowest conversion rate because the landing page can't deliver on the implicit promise. By asking Claude to evaluate the full funnel — click AND convert — you get recommendations that optimize for revenue, not vanity metrics.

The Honest Limitations

I want to be straight with you about what this can't do. Claude's predictions break down when:

  • Your audience is highly niche and doesn't follow general patterns
  • The creative (image/video) matters more than the copy for your specific format
  • You're in a rapidly shifting market where last month's data is already stale
  • Platform-specific factors (Meta's algorithm preferences, time of year, competitive landscape) are driving performance more than copy quality

Don't use Claude's predictions as a substitute for testing. Use them as a filter that makes your testing faster and cheaper. That's the right frame.

Try This

Take ten hooks you've written or generated. Run them through the baseline prediction prompt with the five evaluation dimensions. Read Claude's analysis. I guarantee it'll flag at least two or three hooks that you were planning to test but that have obvious structural weaknesses you didn't notice. Kill those, replace them with stronger options, and test the rest. Your testing budget just got more efficient.

Lesson 87 of 100

Module 9 — Lesson 88

Turning a Product Concept Into a Full SEO Content Architecture

The AI Prompting Course

Most people think about SEO as writing blog posts with keywords in them. That's like thinking about architecture as choosing paint colors. The blog post is the last step. Before that, you need a content architecture — a strategic map of what you're going to write, why, and how each piece connects to drive the outcomes you care about.

Building a content architecture used to take me weeks. Keyword research, competitive analysis, topic clustering, mapping search intent, planning internal linking structures. Now I do it in a single Claude session. Not because I'm cutting corners — because Claude can hold the entire strategic picture in context simultaneously in a way that's really hard to do manually with spreadsheets and fifteen browser tabs open.

Starting With the Product, Not Keywords

Here's where most people go wrong. They start with keyword tools. They find high-volume keywords and write posts targeting them. That's backwards. You should start with your product and work outward to the search landscape.

The first prompt in my process:

"I'm building an SEO content architecture for [product]. Here's what it does: [detailed description]. Here's who it's for: [target audience with specifics]. Here's what they care about: [their problems, goals, decision criteria]. Before we look at any keywords, map out the topics this audience would search for during their buyer journey. Group them into: 1. Problem-aware searches (they know they have a problem, don't know solutions exist) 2. Solution-aware searches (they know solutions exist, comparing options) 3. Product-aware searches (they know about us or competitors specifically) 4. Adjacent searches (topics they care about that relate to our space but aren't directly about our product)"

This gives me a strategic map before I touch a single keyword. The output usually includes fifty to a hundred topic ideas organized by buyer journey stage. Some I'd never have thought of. The adjacent searches are especially valuable — they're topics where you can build authority and capture attention before someone is even in buying mode.

Building the Topic Clusters

Next, I have Claude organize these topics into clusters — groups of related content that support a central pillar page:

"Group these topics into 5-7 content clusters. Each cluster should have: - One pillar page topic (comprehensive, 2000+ word guide) - 5-8 supporting article topics (specific, focused, 800-1200 words each) - Internal linking logic (how the supporting articles connect to the pillar and to each other) The clusters should cover the full buyer journey. At least one cluster should be problem-aware (top of funnel), at least two should be solution-aware (middle of funnel), and at least one should be product-aware (bottom of funnel)."

What comes back is essentially a content strategy that would take a content marketing agency two weeks to deliver. And because Claude can see all the topics at once, the clusters are logically organized and the internal linking makes sense — there's no overlap, no gaps, and each cluster supports the others.

The Keyword Layer

Now — and only now — I add keywords. I'll take the topic clusters to a keyword tool (Ahrefs, SEMrush, whatever you use), find the actual search volumes and difficulty scores, and then bring that data back to Claude:

"Here are the keyword metrics for our content clusters. [paste data] Based on this data, prioritize our content calendar. We can publish 4 articles per month. Sequence them to maximize early wins — start with lower difficulty keywords that have decent volume, build topical authority, then attack the harder keywords once we have supporting content in place."

This gives me a prioritized six-month content calendar that's strategically sequenced for maximum impact. Not just "write the highest volume keyword first" — that's naive. The sequencing accounts for topical authority building, internal linking dependencies, and competitive difficulty.

The Real-World Example

When I was building the content strategy for a legal tech project, I started with the product concept and used this exact process. Claude mapped out topic clusters around case research, settlement data analysis, legal market trends, and practice area specific content. The adjacent topic cluster — "how personal injury firms evaluate case value" — turned out to be our highest-performing content because it captured an audience at the exact moment they needed what we built, even though it wasn't directly about our product.

I never would have identified that angle staring at a keyword tool. It came from starting with the audience's buyer journey instead of search volume data.

The Content Brief Generator

The final step is turning each topic in the calendar into a content brief:

"For the first four articles in our content calendar, generate a detailed content brief for each: - Target keyword and secondary keywords - Search intent (what the reader wants when they search this) - Recommended structure (H2s and H3s) - Key points to cover - Internal links to include (from our planned content architecture) - Differentiation angle (what makes our take on this topic better than what's already ranking) - CTA that aligns with the reader's position in the buyer journey"

Each brief is essentially a writing assignment that anyone on your team — or Claude itself — can execute against. The strategic thinking is done. The execution becomes repeatable.

Try This

Pick a product or service you're working on. Run the first prompt — the buyer journey topic mapping — without any keyword data. Just your product, your audience, and Claude's reasoning about what they'd search for. Count how many topic ideas you get that you hadn't considered. That list alone is probably worth more than whatever you'd get from spending an hour in a keyword tool, because it's organized around human intent rather than search volume.
