Module 9 — Lesson 89

Cold Outreach Sequence Design by Buyer Persona

The AI Prompting Course

Cold outreach is where most people's relationship with AI goes wrong. They use it to generate mass emails that sound like every other mass email. Hi [First Name], I noticed [Company] is doing great things in [Industry]. I'd love to show you how we can help. Delete.

That's not cold outreach. That's spam with a mail merge. And Claude will happily generate thousands of those if you ask it to. The skill isn't in generating volume — it's in designing sequences that actually get responses from people who get fifty of these a day.

I've used Claude to build cold outreach sequences that consistently get 15-25% reply rates. Not open rates. Reply rates. Here's how.

Start With the Persona, Not the Product

The fundamental mistake in cold outreach is starting with what you want to sell. Nobody cares what you want to sell. They care about their own problems, their own goals, their own Tuesday afternoon.

Before I write a single email, I build the persona in Claude:

"I'm building a cold outreach sequence targeting [specific title] at [specific type of company]. Before we write any emails, help me build a detailed persona: - What does their day look like? - What are their top 3 priorities this quarter? - What keeps them up at night professionally? - What tools are they already using that relate to our space? - What's their relationship with cold emails — what makes them reply vs. delete? - What language do they use to describe their problems? (Not marketing language — how they'd describe it to a coworker over coffee) - What would make them forward an email to their boss or team? Be specific and realistic. This is a [role] at a [size] [type] company, not a generic persona."

This gives me a working model of the person I'm writing to. And that model informs everything about the sequence — the tone, the length, the angle, the ask, the timing.

Designing the Sequence Architecture

A cold outreach sequence isn't just "email 1, then email 2, then email 3." Each email has a specific job and a specific psychological strategy. Here's the framework I give Claude:

"Design a 5-email cold outreach sequence for this persona. Each email must have a distinct strategic purpose: Email 1: The pattern interrupt. Short. Unexpected. Not a pitch. Goal: get them to read email 2. Email 2: The value demonstration. Share something genuinely useful — an insight, data point, or observation about their business. Goal: establish credibility. Email 3: The specific connection. Reference something concrete about their company, role, or recent activity. Goal: show this isn't a mass email. Email 4: The soft ask. Propose something low-commitment — a 10-minute call, a shared resource, a specific question. Goal: get a reply, any reply. Email 5: The breakup email. Last touch. Honest, direct, slightly self-deprecating. Goal: trigger the loss aversion response. Spacing: 3-4 days between emails 1-2, 5 days between 2-3, 4 days between 3-4, 7 days between 4-5. Each email must be under 100 words. No corporate language. Write like a real person who's busy and direct."

The Persona-Specific Variation

Here's the thing that makes this actually powerful. Once you have this sequence architecture, you can generate variations for different personas. The CTO gets a different sequence than the VP of Sales, even if you're selling the same product.

The CTO version might lead with a technical insight in Email 2. The VP of Sales version might lead with a revenue metric. The CEO version might be even shorter and lead with a strategic question. Same product, same five-email framework, completely different execution.

I typically build three to five persona-specific sequences and then ask Claude:

"Compare the five sequences side by side. For each persona, confirm that the tone matches how that role actually communicates. A CTO email shouldn't sound like a sales email. A CEO email shouldn't sound like a technical email. Flag any places where the persona voice slips."

The Personalization Layer

After the sequences are built, I add genuine personalization — and this is where Claude really shines. I'll feed it information about a specific prospect and ask it to customize the sequence:

"Here's the base sequence for the CTO persona. Now customize it for [Name], CTO at [Company]. Here's what I know about them: [LinkedIn summary, recent posts, company news, tech stack info, anything you've found]. Work genuine references to their situation into the emails without making it feel stalkerish. The personalization should feel like 'this person did their homework' not 'this person has been watching me.' One specific reference per email, max."

That last constraint is important. Over-personalization is creepy. One well-placed reference shows you did your research. Five references in one email shows you've been obsessing over their LinkedIn profile. Claude can easily overdo it unless you cap it.

What I've Learned From the Results

After running sequences like this for months, a few patterns have become clear. Email 1 and Email 5 get the most replies. Email 1 works because the pattern interrupt catches attention. Email 5 works because of loss aversion — people reply to breakup emails more than any other type. Emails 2-4 build the relationship that makes the reply to Email 5 actually productive.

The sequences that perform best are the ones where Claude nails the persona voice. When a CTO reads an email that sounds like it was written by another technical person who gets their world, they engage. When they read something that sounds like a BDR following a script, they delete.

Try This

Pick one buyer persona you're actively trying to reach. Build the persona profile in Claude using the prompt above — really push for specificity. Then generate a five-email sequence following the framework. Read each email and ask yourself: would I reply to this? If the answer is no, tell Claude why and iterate. The goal isn't perfection on the first pass — it's a sequence that sounds like a real human who happens to have exactly what the reader needs.

Lesson 89 of 100

Module 9 — Lesson 90

Building a Content Strategy From a Single Positioning Statement

The AI Prompting Course

Everything in your marketing should trace back to one sentence. One positioning statement that defines who you are, who you're for, and why you're different. If you can't say it in one sentence, you don't have positioning — you have a collection of features looking for a narrative.

Here's what's wild about this lesson: Claude can take that single sentence and extrapolate an entire content strategy from it. Not a random collection of blog post ideas. A coherent, strategic, multi-channel content plan where every piece of content reinforces the same positioning from a different angle. I've done this for my own companies and for clients, and it consistently produces strategies that are more cohesive than what most marketing teams build manually over months.

The Positioning Statement

Before we do anything with Claude, you need the statement. And most people's positioning statements are terrible. They're either too vague ("We help businesses grow with AI") or too long ("We provide enterprise-grade artificial intelligence solutions that enable mid-market companies to automate their operational workflows while maintaining compliance with industry-specific regulatory frameworks"). Both are useless.

A good positioning statement follows this structure: For [specific audience], [product] is the [category] that [key differentiator] unlike [alternative] which [limitation].

Here's an example from when we were positioning Official AI: "For operations-heavy businesses doing $5-50M in revenue, Official AI is the automation platform that learns from your existing workflows instead of forcing you to rebuild them — unlike traditional automation tools that require months of custom development."

That's a sentence I can build an entire content strategy from. It contains an audience, a category, a differentiator, and a competitive frame. Everything Claude needs.

The Expansion Prompt

Here's how I unpack that single statement into a full content strategy:

"Here is our positioning statement: [paste it] From this single statement, build a complete content strategy. Extract every implicit claim, audience assumption, and competitive angle, then map content to each one: 1. Core themes — What are the 4-5 major themes embedded in this positioning? (e.g., 'learns from existing workflows' implies themes around implementation speed, workflow preservation, change management) 2. Content pillars — For each theme, define a content pillar with: - Pillar description - Target audience segment this pillar serves - Stage of buyer journey it targets - 10 specific content pieces (articles, guides, case studies, social posts) 3. Narrative threads — How do these pillars connect to each other? What's the story someone follows if they consume content across multiple pillars? 4. Competitive content — What content directly addresses the 'unlike [alternative]' portion of the positioning? What comparison, migration, and alternative content should we create? 5. Channel distribution — For each pillar, which channels make sense and why? (blog, LinkedIn, email, YouTube, podcasts, etc.) Be specific. I don't want generic content ideas. I want ideas that could ONLY come from this positioning statement."

That last line is the most important sentence in the entire prompt. Without it, Claude will give you generic marketing content ideas that could apply to any company. With it, Claude is forced to derive every piece of content directly from your unique positioning. The output becomes specific to you in a way that generic content calendars never are.

What Comes Back

When I ran this for Official AI, Claude identified themes I hadn't consciously articulated. "Learns from existing workflows" implied a content theme around "the hidden cost of rip-and-replace" — exploring why companies fail at automation when tools force them to change how they work. That became an entire pillar with ten articles, a LinkedIn series, and a downloadable guide.

The competitive content section was especially valuable. From "unlike traditional automation tools that require months of custom development," Claude generated comparison content, a "migration from [competitor] to us" guide, and a series of articles about the hidden costs of custom development. All of those pieces directly reinforce our positioning every time someone encounters them.

The 90-Day Calendar

After the strategy is mapped, I compress it into an actionable calendar:

"Turn this content strategy into a 90-day publishing calendar. Constraints: - 2 long-form articles per week - 5 LinkedIn posts per week - 1 email newsletter per week - 1 lead magnet or downloadable per month Sequence the content so that early pieces build foundational awareness and later pieces can reference and link to earlier ones. The first month should focus on the problem-aware themes. Month two should shift to solution-aware. Month three should be product-aware and competitive. For each piece, include: title, pillar it belongs to, buyer journey stage, primary keyword target, and a one-sentence description of the angle."

The output is a complete, executable content plan that a single person or a small team can run with. Every piece traces back to the positioning statement. Nothing is random. Nothing is there just because "we should have a blog post about that."

The Consistency Test

Here's my final quality check. After the strategy is built, I ask Claude to stress-test it:

"Read through the entire content strategy. If someone consumed all of this content over 90 days, would they walk away with a clear, consistent understanding of our positioning? Or would any pieces muddy the message, create confusion, or pull in a direction that doesn't reinforce the core statement? Flag anything that drifts."

This catches the pieces that sound good individually but don't serve the larger narrative. Maybe an article idea is interesting but targets an audience that's outside your positioning. Maybe a social post angle contradicts the competitive frame. Claude catches these because it can hold the entire strategy in context and evaluate coherence across dozens of content pieces simultaneously.

Why One Statement Changes Everything

I see this all the time with founders and marketing teams. They have ten different messages floating around. Their website says one thing, their LinkedIn says another, their sales deck tells a third story. It's not that any single piece is bad — it's that together they create noise instead of signal.

Starting from a single positioning statement and using Claude to extrapolate everything from it forces coherence. Every piece of content is an expression of the same core idea, just viewed from a different angle or at a different stage of the buyer journey. That consistency compounds over time. Prospects who encounter you across multiple channels get the same message reinforced, not a fragmented picture.

Try This

Write your positioning statement. One sentence. Use the structure I outlined: audience, category, differentiator, competitive frame. If you can't do it in one sentence, that's actually the most valuable thing this exercise will reveal — it means your positioning isn't clear yet. Once you have it, run the expansion prompt. What comes back will either confirm that your positioning is strong enough to build on, or it'll expose the cracks. Both outcomes are worth the ten minutes it takes.

Lesson 90 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 10 — Lesson 91

Thinking of Claude as a Senior Collaborator, Not a Search Engine

The AI Prompting Course

I want to tell you about a moment that changed how I work. I was building out the product strategy for Official AI with my co-founder Ben, and we were stuck on positioning. Not stuck like "we don't have ideas" stuck. Stuck like "we have twelve ideas and can't figure out which one is right" stuck.

I opened Claude and almost typed "what's a good positioning strategy for an AI startup." Almost. That's the search engine instinct. Instead, I spent about three minutes dumping everything: the competitive landscape, what we'd heard in customer calls, the two positioning directions we were torn between, the specific objections we'd gotten from early users, and our constraints around launch timing.

What came back wasn't an answer. It was a strategic analysis that pointed out a tension in our thinking that Ben and I had completely missed. Claude noticed that our two positioning options were actually solving for different customer segments, and the real question wasn't which positioning was better — it was which customer we wanted to serve first.

That's not a search engine output. That's what a senior strategist gives you after a good briefing.

The Mental Model Shift

Here's the thing most people never get past: they interact with AI the way they interact with Google. Type a question, get an answer, move on. And that works fine for simple stuff. "What's the capital of Portugal" doesn't need a collaboration framework.

But the moment your work involves ambiguity, tradeoffs, judgment, or creative thinking — which is basically everything that matters in business — the search engine model falls apart. You need a different mental model entirely.

Think about the best senior person you've ever worked with. Maybe a mentor, a sharp colleague, a consultant who actually earned their fee. What made them valuable wasn't that they knew more facts than you. It was that they could take messy, incomplete information about your specific situation and help you think through it. They'd push back on your assumptions. They'd spot patterns you were too close to see. They'd ask questions that reframed the problem.

That's what Claude can do. But only if you treat it that way.

What Changes When You Shift

When you stop treating AI as a search engine and start treating it as a senior collaborator, three things change immediately.

You give more context. You wouldn't walk into a meeting with a senior advisor and say "help me with marketing." You'd spend the first five minutes explaining where things stand, what you've tried, what's working, what isn't. You'd give them the lay of the land. When you do this with Claude, the quality of output jumps dramatically.

You have conversations, not transactions. A search engine interaction is one-and-done. A collaboration is iterative. You share something, they respond, you push back, they refine. The best work I've gotten from Claude has never come from a single prompt — it's come from the fourth or fifth exchange in a conversation where we've been narrowing in on something together.

You ask harder questions. People ask search engines for facts. They ask collaborators for judgment. "What do you think the biggest risk is with this approach?" "Where are the holes in this argument?" "If you were advising a competitor, how would you attack our position?" These questions unlock Claude's actual capabilities in a way that factual queries never will.

How I Actually Do This

Let me show you what a typical "senior collaborator" session looks like for me. I was working on a pricing model for a new service at Mudita Studios. Instead of asking "what's a good pricing model for a digital agency," I started like this:

"I run a small creative studio that does brand strategy and AI-integrated marketing for B2B SaaS companies. We're launching a new productized service — an AI audit that evaluates how a company is using AI in their marketing ops and gives them a roadmap for improvement. Our typical client is a SaaS company doing $5-30M ARR. I'm trying to figure out pricing. Here's my tension: I want it to be accessible enough that companies will try it without a long sales cycle, but premium enough that they take the output seriously. We've been kicking around $3,500 for a one-week engagement or $7,500 for a more comprehensive three-week version. What am I not thinking about?"

That last question is the key. "What am I not thinking about?" That's a collaborator question. And Claude came back with three things I genuinely hadn't considered: the signaling effect of pricing on perceived quality, the risk of the lower tier cannibalizing the higher tier, and a suggestion to anchor with the premium option and offer the lighter version as a "quick-start" rather than a standalone product.

Was every suggestion perfect? No. But the conversation that followed — where I pushed back on some points and dug deeper on others — gave me more clarity in twenty minutes than two days of going back and forth in my own head.

The Prompts That Unlock Collaboration

There are specific kinds of questions that shift Claude from "information retrieval mode" to "thinking partner mode." Here are the ones I use constantly:

  • "What are the tradeoffs between X and Y given my situation?" — Forces comparative analysis grounded in your context.
  • "Poke holes in this plan." — Invites critical thinking instead of agreement.
  • "What would you prioritize if you were in my position?" — Asks for judgment, not just information.
  • "What's the strongest counterargument to this?" — Gets you the thinking you need to stress-test your ideas.
  • "I'm leaning toward X. Talk me out of it — or tell me why my instinct is right." — Creates a structured thinking exercise.

Notice what these all have in common: they assume Claude has something to contribute beyond looking things up. They treat the interaction as a thinking exercise, not a retrieval task.

Try This

Take a real decision you're facing right now — something with genuine ambiguity. Not "what should I name my dog" but something where there are real tradeoffs, real stakes, real uncertainty.

Open Claude and brief it like you'd brief a sharp advisor. Give it the full picture: what you're trying to decide, what the options are, what you've already considered, what your constraints are, what your gut is telling you. Then ask it a collaborator question. Not "what should I do?" but "what am I not seeing?" or "where are the risks in my current thinking?"

The difference in output will be striking. And once you feel it, you won't go back to search engine mode.

Lesson 91 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 10 — Lesson 92

When NOT to Use AI — Protecting Decisions That Require Judgment

The AI Prompting Course

This might be the most important lesson in the entire course, and it's about the thing nobody in the AI space wants to talk about: when to close the laptop and think for yourself.

I use AI more than almost anyone I know. I build products with it, I run parts of my business through it, I've spent hundreds of hours in conversation with Claude. And precisely because of that experience, I've developed a very clear sense of where AI should not be the decision-maker. Not because the technology is bad. Because some decisions require something AI literally cannot provide.

The Judgment Line

Here's the distinction I've landed on after running Mudita Studios and building Official AI: there's a line between decisions that benefit from more information and better analysis, and decisions that ultimately come down to human judgment about values, relationships, and risk tolerance.

AI is extraordinary at the first category. It can process more information than you, find patterns you'd miss, and present options you haven't considered. I use it for this constantly.

But the second category — the decisions that are fundamentally about what kind of person you are, what kind of company you want to build, how you treat people — those aren't information problems. They're judgment problems. And outsourcing your judgment is different from outsourcing your analysis.

The Situations Where I Don't Use AI

Hiring and firing decisions about specific people. I'll use Claude to help me think through what role I need, what the job description should look like, what interview questions to ask. But the actual decision of whether to hire or let go of a specific person? That's mine. It involves reading someone's character, understanding team dynamics, weighing things that can't be captured in a prompt. I've seen people paste performance reviews into ChatGPT and ask "should I fire this person?" That scares me.

Relationship-critical communications where the other person would feel betrayed if they knew AI wrote it. If I'm writing a condolence note to a colleague, navigating a personal conflict with a friend, or responding to someone who's vulnerable and trusting me with something real — I write that myself. Even if Claude could produce something eloquent, the point of those communications isn't eloquence. It's authenticity. The person on the other end deserves your actual words, not a machine's approximation of your empathy.

Ethical decisions where you need to feel the weight of the choice. Should we take on a client whose values don't align with ours, even though we need the revenue? Should we pivot the company in a direction that means letting go of part of the team? These decisions need to cost you something emotionally. They need to keep you up at night. That discomfort is a feature, not a bug — it's what makes you actually reckon with the consequences.

Creative vision and taste-level decisions. I'll use Claude to generate options, brainstorm angles, pressure-test ideas. But the final call on "this is what our brand looks like" or "this is the story we're telling" — that comes from taste, intuition, and lived experience. AI can show you fifty directions. Only you can choose the one that feels right for reasons you might not be able to fully articulate.

The Danger of Convenient Answers

Here's the thing nobody warns you about. AI makes it very easy to feel like you've thought something through when you haven't. You paste in a problem, you get back a structured analysis with pros and cons and a recommendation, and there's a sense of resolution. "Well, Claude said Option B is better, so I guess we'll go with that."

But that feeling of resolution is artificial. You haven't actually done the hard thinking. You've outsourced it and mistaken the output for your own deliberation. That's dangerous not because the analysis is wrong — it might be perfectly solid — but because you've weakened your own judgment muscle. You've stopped practicing the thing that makes you good at your job.

I see this all the time with founders I talk to. They're making increasingly complex decisions with decreasingly deep personal engagement. They prompt their way through strategy, hiring, investor communications, product direction. And they start to feel like something is slipping. They can't quite articulate it, but the decisions feel hollow. That's because they are.

The Framework I Use

I have a simple test for whether I should use AI on a decision. I ask myself two questions:

Question one: If this decision goes wrong, will I be able to look the affected people in the eye and explain my reasoning? If the answer is "I asked Claude and went with its recommendation," that's not good enough for high-stakes human decisions. I need to own the reasoning. AI can inform it, but I need to be able to defend it in my own words, from my own thinking.

Question two: Is the value of AI here in making a better decision, or in making the decision easier? Better is good. Easier is a trap. If I'm reaching for AI because the decision is uncomfortable and I want someone else to carry the weight of it, that's exactly when I should not be using it.

Where I Do Use AI for Hard Decisions

This isn't a lesson about being a Luddite. I absolutely use Claude when facing tough decisions — just not to make them for me. Here's the difference:

"Here's the situation with our biggest client. They want us to do X, which goes against our stated values around Y. I'm leaning toward declining the work but I'm worried about the revenue impact. Help me think through this — what are the second-order consequences of each path? What am I potentially not considering?"

That's using AI as a thinking aid. The analysis is valuable. The framework Claude provides might surface something I missed. But the decision is still mine. I'm using AI to see more clearly, not to avoid looking.

Try This

Look at the last ten decisions you used AI to help with. Sort them into two categories: ones where AI helped you think better, and ones where AI helped you think less. Be honest with yourself.

For anything in that second category, ask yourself: what was I avoiding? What would it have looked like to sit with the discomfort and work through it myself? The goal isn't to stop using AI. It's to use it with your eyes open about what you're gaining and what you might be giving up.

The people who will be most successful with AI in the long run aren't the ones who use it for everything. They're the ones who have a clear, principled understanding of when it makes them better and when it makes them lazy. Build that discernment now.

Lesson 92 of 100

Module 10 — Lesson 93

How to Catch Hallucinations Before They Cost You

The AI Prompting Course

Let me tell you about the time I almost sent an investor update with a completely fabricated statistic in it. I was using Claude to help me draft a market analysis section, and it included a very specific data point — something like "the AI marketing tools market is projected to reach $107.5 billion by 2028, according to a report by Grand View Research." It was specific. It had a source. It felt authoritative.

It was also made up. Not the general direction — the AI marketing tools market is growing fast. But that specific number, that specific source, that specific year? Claude generated it because the pattern fit, not because it was pulling from a real document. If I'd sent that to investors without checking, I'd have built part of my credibility on fiction.

This is the hallucination problem. And if you're going to use AI seriously — for business, for research, for anything that matters — you need a system for catching it.

Why Hallucinations Happen

I'm not going to get deeply technical, but the basic intuition matters. Language models generate text by predicting the most likely next word based on patterns in their training data. They're not looking things up in a database. They're not retrieving facts from a filing cabinet. They're generating plausible language.

Most of the time, plausible language and accurate language overlap heavily. That's why AI is so useful — it's usually right, or at least directionally correct. But "usually" isn't "always," and the gap between the two is where people get burned.

The tricky part is that hallucinations don't come with warning labels. Claude doesn't say "I'm guessing here" or "I made this up." The fabricated statistic looks exactly like a real one. The fake case law citation has the same format as a real one. The invented company name sounds plausible. The confidence level in the prose is identical whether the information is accurate or completely fictional.

The High-Risk Zones

Through a lot of trial and error, I've mapped out where hallucinations are most likely. This is important because it lets you calibrate your skepticism appropriately instead of either trusting everything or verifying everything (which defeats the purpose of using AI).

Specific statistics and numbers. If Claude gives you a precise number — a market size, a percentage, a revenue figure — verify it. General trends are usually reliable. Specific data points are where things go sideways.

Citations and sources. This is the biggest one. AI will generate fake citations that look perfectly real. Fake URLs, fake paper titles, fake author names. I see this all the time when people use AI for research. The source looks legitimate, the format is correct, and the paper simply does not exist.

Historical facts with specific details. AI is good with well-known history and bad with niche details. It might tell you a specific Supreme Court case supports your argument when that case is either about something different or doesn't exist at all.

Technical specifications. Exact API parameters, specific software version capabilities, precise regulatory requirements. The general knowledge is usually right; the details can be fabricated.

Anything about recent events. Claude's training data has a cutoff. It doesn't always know what it doesn't know, so it might generate plausible-sounding information about things that happened after its training or things that are rapidly evolving.

My Verification System

I don't verify every single thing Claude tells me. That would be insane and would erase most of the productivity benefit. Instead, I run a tiered system based on stakes and risk zones.

Tier 1 — Publish-grade content. Anything that will be seen by investors, clients, the public, or used in legal or regulatory contexts. Every factual claim gets verified. Every statistic gets sourced independently. Every citation gets checked. No exceptions. This is the price of using AI for serious work — you verify what matters.

Tier 2 — Internal decision-making. Strategy docs, internal analyses, brainstorming. I'll verify claims that directly influence the decision, but I'm more relaxed about general framing and directional statements. If Claude says "most B2B SaaS companies see higher churn in the first 90 days," I'm not going to hunt for the source because the general point is well-established and I'm using it for internal thinking.

Tier 3 — Draft and iteration. First drafts, brainstorming sessions, exploratory thinking. I'm not verifying much here because nothing is leaving my desk in this form. The point is to generate ideas and refine them, not to produce publication-ready content.

Practical Catching Techniques

Beyond the tiered system, here are specific techniques I use to catch hallucinations in real-time:

Ask Claude to flag its own uncertainty. This actually works better than most people think.

"Give me an overview of the competitive landscape for AI-powered legal research tools. For any specific claims, statistics, or company details, flag your confidence level — tell me what you're sure about and where you're less certain."

Claude won't catch everything, but it will often flag areas where it's working from less certain knowledge. This gives you a map of where to focus your verification effort.

Ask for the reasoning, not just the answer. When Claude gives you a factual claim, ask "how do you know that?" or "what's that based on?" If the reasoning is vague or circular, that's a red flag. If it can walk you through a clear logic chain, the claim is more likely reliable.

Cross-reference the specific with the general. If Claude gives you a specific statistic, check whether it's in the right ballpark. You can often catch fabricated numbers by just checking whether they make logical sense, even without finding the exact source.

Use the "two-source rule" for anything important. If a claim matters, find it in at least one independent source before relying on it. This is just good practice regardless of whether AI is involved.

Try This

Next time you get a response from Claude that includes specific statistics, sources, or factual claims, try this: ask Claude "For each factual claim in your response, rate your confidence from 1-5 and explain why." Then pick the two lowest-confidence items and verify them independently. You'll start to develop an instinct for what's reliable and what needs checking. That instinct is one of the most valuable skills you can build as an AI power user.

Lesson 93 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 10 — Lesson 94

Epistemic Calibration: Knowing When Claude Is Confident vs. Guessing

The AI Prompting Course

Last lesson we talked about hallucinations — when AI is flat-out wrong. This lesson is about something subtler and, in some ways, more dangerous: the space between confident and guessing, and how to tell the difference.

I call this epistemic calibration. Fancy term, simple concept: can you tell how much Claude actually "knows" about what it's telling you versus how much it's pattern-matching its way to a plausible-sounding answer?

This matters because most of what AI gets wrong isn't completely fabricated. It's partially right, confidently stated, and subtly off in ways that are hard to catch. The statistic is in the right ballpark but not precise. The legal concept is real but doesn't apply the way Claude described. The business advice is generally sound but misses a crucial nuance about your specific industry. This is the stuff that actually costs you, because you're less likely to verify things that sound mostly right.

The Confidence Spectrum

Through a lot of use, I've started to recognize a rough spectrum of how reliable Claude's outputs are across different types of knowledge. This isn't scientific — it's based on my experience across thousands of interactions.

High confidence — widely documented, stable knowledge: How programming languages work. The general principles of marketing and business strategy. Well-established scientific concepts. The content of widely-read books. The basics of law, finance, and medicine. For this stuff, Claude is extremely reliable — as reliable as a well-read, well-educated person.

Medium confidence — domain-specific, nuanced, or evolving: Best practices in specific industries. The details of particular frameworks or methodologies. Current competitive landscapes. Implementation details of specific technologies. Here Claude is like a smart generalist who's read a lot but hasn't worked in the specific field. Directionally right, potentially wrong on specifics.

Lower confidence — niche, recent, or requires real-world experience: What's actually working right now in a specific market. The internal dynamics of a specific organization. How a particular tool behaves in edge cases. Pricing for specific services in specific geographies. This is where Claude is most likely to generate plausible-sounding content that doesn't match reality.

Reading the Signals

Here's the practical skill I want you to develop. Claude gives off subtle signals about its own certainty, even when it doesn't explicitly flag it. You can learn to read these.

Signal: hedging language. When Claude says "generally," "typically," "in many cases," or "it's worth noting that" — pay attention. That hedging often means it's working from general patterns rather than specific knowledge. It's not always uncertain when it hedges, but the hedging is a prompt to ask yourself: do I need this to be precise, or is "generally" good enough?

Signal: overly detailed specifics in unfamiliar territory. Counterintuitively, when Claude suddenly gets very specific about something niche — exact percentages, specific company details, precise timelines — that can be a red flag rather than a green one. Specificity feels authoritative, but in areas where Claude is less certain, specificity is sometimes compensating for actual knowledge. Real experts often hedge more, not less, because they understand nuance.

Signal: generic frameworks applied to specific questions. If you ask Claude a very specific question about your situation and it responds with a general framework rather than a specific answer, that's actually an honest response. It's telling you, implicitly, "I don't know enough about your specific case to give you a precise answer, so here's a thinking tool instead." That's more trustworthy than a false-specific answer.

How to Actively Calibrate

Beyond reading the signals, you can actively prompt Claude to help you calibrate. This is one of the most underused techniques I've found, and I use it constantly.

The domain expertise check. Before diving into a complex topic, ask:

"How well do you understand the nuances of B2B SaaS pricing for vertical markets? What aspects would you be most and least confident advising on?"

Claude will usually give you a surprisingly honest self-assessment. It'll tell you where its knowledge is strong and where you'd be better off consulting a domain expert. This saves you from getting deep into a conversation where Claude is quietly out of its depth.

The assumption check. After Claude gives you advice or analysis, ask:

"What assumptions are you making in this analysis? Which of those assumptions are you least sure about?"

This forces Claude to surface the foundation of its reasoning. If the assumptions are shaky, the conclusions built on them are too — regardless of how confidently they're stated.

The "what would change your answer" test. This is my favorite:

"What additional information, if I could provide it, would most change or refine your recommendation?"

This reveals what Claude knows it doesn't know. The areas where additional information would change the answer are the areas where the current answer is most likely to be imprecise or wrong. It's like asking a doctor "what tests would you run before being sure?" — it shows you where the uncertainty lives.

Why This Skill Matters More Than Prompting

I see this all the time in the AI space. People obsess over writing better prompts but never develop the skill of evaluating outputs. They get better at asking questions but not at assessing the quality of answers. That's like getting better at asking for directions but never developing the ability to tell when someone is confidently sending you the wrong way.

Epistemic calibration is the skill that separates people who use AI effectively from people who use AI dangerously. Both groups get the same outputs. The first group knows which parts to trust, which to verify, and which to discard. The second group treats it all the same.

Try This

Pick a topic you're genuinely expert in — something you know deeply from real-world experience. Ask Claude a series of increasingly specific questions about it. Start broad and go narrow. Notice where its answers start to diverge from what you know to be true. Notice the language it uses when it's confident versus when it's pattern-matching. That gap — between where Claude is reliable and where it starts to drift — is what you need to be calibrated to across every domain you use it in.

Then take that same calibration instinct and apply it to a domain you don't know well. You'll find that the same signals — the hedging, the false specificity, the generic frameworks — show up. Once you know what uncertainty looks like in AI output, you can spot it everywhere.

Lesson 94 of 100

Module 10 — Lesson 95

Intellectual Property and AI: What You Can and Can't Safely Generate

The AI Prompting Course

I'm going to be upfront: I'm not a lawyer, and this lesson is not legal advice. What this is, is a practical framework from someone who runs a company that uses AI-generated content every single day and has had to figure out how to do it without getting into trouble.

The IP landscape around AI is evolving fast. What I'm sharing here is how I think about it right now, based on conversations with actual lawyers, real business decisions I've had to make, and the current state of the law as I understand it. You should absolutely consult a lawyer for your specific situation, especially if the stakes are high.

That said — most people aren't thinking about this at all, which means even an imperfect framework puts you ahead of ninety percent of AI users.

The Core Question

The question most people ask is "Can I own what AI generates?" The more useful question is: "What can I safely use, and where are the risk zones?"

Here's the current landscape as I navigate it. In the U.S., the Copyright Office has taken the position that purely AI-generated content — with no meaningful human creative input — cannot be copyrighted. But content that involves substantial human authorship, with AI as a tool, can be. The line between those two is blurry, and that's where the practical challenge lives.

When I use Claude to help me write marketing copy, I'm heavily involved in the process. I'm providing the strategy, the positioning, the voice, the structure. I'm editing, refining, sometimes rewriting entirely. Claude is a tool in my creative process, not a replacement for it. That's a very different situation than typing "write me a blog post about X" and publishing whatever comes back.

The Risk Zones

Generating content that closely mimics a specific creator's style. This is where I see people get into the most trouble, practically if not legally. Prompting Claude to "write in the style of [specific author/brand]" and then publishing it is ethically murky and potentially legally risky. The more specific and recognizable the style you're imitating, the bigger the risk. "Write in a conversational, punchy tone" is fine. "Write exactly like [specific famous writer] in their [specific famous work]" is asking for problems.

Generating code and using it commercially. Code generated by AI is in an especially gray area. There have been lawsuits about AI training on open-source code and whether the outputs constitute derivative works. My practical approach: for core, differentiated product code, I use AI to accelerate but always review, modify, and understand every line. For standard boilerplate — setting up an Express server, writing a basic SQL query — the risk is lower because there are only so many ways to write that code.

Generating visual content. Image generation has its own set of IP considerations that are distinct from text. Artists have filed lawsuits arguing that AI image generators trained on their work produce derivative copies. This is being litigated right now. For Mudita Studios, we made the decision to use AI-generated images for internal brainstorming and mood boarding but to rely on original photography, licensed stock, or work from actual designers and illustrators for client-facing deliverables.

Generating content in regulated industries. If you're in healthcare, finance, legal, or any heavily regulated space, the AI-generated content question intersects with compliance requirements. A financial advisor using AI to generate client communications still has to ensure those communications meet regulatory standards. The AI doesn't change your compliance obligations.

My Practical Framework

Here's how I actually handle this at Mudita Studios and with Official AI. It's not perfect, but it's systematic.

Principle 1: Always add substantial human creative input. I never publish AI output as-is. Everything goes through meaningful human editing, refinement, and creative direction. This isn't just about IP protection — it's about quality. But it has the added benefit of strengthening any copyright claim by ensuring substantial human authorship.

Principle 2: Don't try to replicate specific protected works. I never prompt Claude to reproduce or closely imitate a specific copyrighted work. "Help me write a case study in a clear, narrative style" is different from "Rewrite this specific article from [publication] in slightly different words." The first is using AI as a creative tool. The second is using it as a plagiarism tool.

Principle 3: Be transparent where it matters. With clients, I'm upfront that we use AI in our workflow. Most clients don't care — they care about the quality of the output and the results it produces. But they deserve to know, and being transparent protects you from the awkward conversation later if it comes out.

Principle 4: Keep records of your creative process. I save my prompt histories for any important creative work. If there's ever a question about the level of human involvement, I can show the back-and-forth — the strategy I defined, the iterations I directed, the editing I did. This is probably overkill right now, but as the legal landscape develops, having a clear record of your creative process won't hurt.

What's Likely Coming

The law is going to catch up. There are major cases working through the courts right now that will establish clearer precedents. My read — and again, not a lawyer — is that we'll end up somewhere reasonable: AI as a tool in human creative processes will be fine, purely AI-generated content used commercially without meaningful human involvement will face more restrictions, and there will be specific rules around training data and attribution.

The best thing you can do right now is build habits that will hold up under whatever rules emerge. Use AI as a genuine creative partner, not a content factory. Add real value through your own thinking and editing. Be transparent with stakeholders. And when the stakes are high, ask a lawyer.

Try This

Audit your current AI workflow. For each type of content you generate with AI, ask yourself: how much of the final product reflects my creative input versus raw AI output? If the answer is "I basically publish what Claude gives me with minor edits," consider whether you need to increase your involvement — not just for IP reasons, but because the quality will be better and the content will actually sound like you.

Lesson 95 of 100

Module 10 — Lesson 96

Using Claude for Sensitive Situations: Internal Politics, Layoffs, Legal-Adjacent

The AI Prompting Course

There's a category of work that nobody talks about in AI courses because it's uncomfortable: the stuff you need help with but can't easily ask another person. Internal politics. Layoff communications. Difficult conversations with co-founders. Messages that are one wrong word away from a legal issue.

I've used Claude for all of these. And I think it's one of AI's most underappreciated use cases — the confidential thinking partner for situations where you can't just poll your network or ask a colleague.

But it requires a specific approach, because the stakes are high and the margin for error is thin.

Why AI Is Actually Good for This

Here's the counterintuitive thing. For sensitive workplace situations, AI has some genuine advantages over human advisors.

Zero judgment. When I needed to figure out how to have a conversation with a team member about underperformance, I could tell Claude every detail without worrying about how it would change anyone's perception of me, the team member, or the company. No social consequences. No gossip risk. No "well, between us..." leaking into the organization.

No political agenda. A human advisor — even a well-intentioned one — has their own relationships, biases, and political position in the organization. They might tell you what you want to hear, or what serves their own interests, or what aligns with their personal loyalties. Claude has none of that. It's looking at the situation you describe and nothing else.

Available at 2 AM. Sensitive situations don't happen on a schedule. The moment you find out you need to let someone go, or you get an email that could have legal implications, or a board member says something that needs a careful response — that's when you need to think it through. Not during your next scheduled meeting with a mentor.

How I Approach Sensitive Prompts

The key principle for sensitive work is this: give Claude the full picture, but never include identifying information you wouldn't want associated with this conversation.

Let me show you what I mean. Here's how I might prompt Claude for help with a difficult personnel situation:

"I need to have a performance conversation with a senior team member. Here's the context: they were a strong contributor for the first year but their output has declined significantly over the last three months. They've missed deadlines on two major projects, and their communication with the rest of the team has become inconsistent. I've had one informal conversation about it that didn't lead to improvement. I genuinely like this person and value what they've contributed, but the current trajectory isn't sustainable. I need to have a direct conversation that's compassionate but clear, sets specific expectations with a timeline, and documents the conversation appropriately. Help me think through the structure of this conversation and what to say."

Notice what I included: the full context, the emotional reality, the specifics of the performance issues, what I've already tried, and what I need from the conversation. Notice what I didn't include: the person's name, the company name, identifying details that would make this traceable to a specific individual.

This approach gives Claude everything it needs to help while maintaining appropriate confidentiality.

The Specific Sensitive Scenarios

Layoff communications. This is one where I've seen Claude be genuinely excellent. The challenge with layoff communications is that they need to be empathetic, clear, legally careful, and consistent — all at once. That's a hard combination to hit when you're emotionally drained from making the decision in the first place. I use Claude to draft the communication, then I rewrite it in my own voice, then I have a lawyer review anything that touches on legal specifics like severance terms or benefits continuation.

Responding to legal-adjacent situations. Someone threatens a lawsuit. A former employee posts something damaging online. A client claims you violated a contract term. Your instinct is to respond immediately, and that instinct is almost always wrong. I use Claude to draft a measured response, to think through the implications of different approaches, and to identify where I need actual legal counsel versus where I can handle it myself. But — and this is critical — Claude is not a lawyer and any output related to legal situations is a starting point for professional legal review, not a final answer.

Internal politics and stakeholder management. This is maybe the most common sensitive use case. You need to send an email to a difficult board member. You need to navigate a disagreement between two team leads without taking sides. You need to push back on a client's unreasonable demand without losing the account. These all require careful word choice and emotional intelligence. Claude can help you think through the dynamics, draft options, and pressure-test your approach before you commit to it.

The Rules I Follow

Rule 1: Never let AI be the final voice on sensitive communications. Claude drafts. I finalize. Every word that goes out in a sensitive situation goes out in my voice, after my review, with my judgment applied. AI is the thinking partner, not the author.

Rule 2: Get professional review when the stakes are high. If there are legal implications, have a lawyer look at it. If there are HR implications, involve your HR advisor. AI can help you prepare, but it doesn't replace professional expertise in high-stakes situations.

Rule 3: Use Claude for preparation, not just drafting. Some of the best value I get is in preparing for difficult conversations, not just writing difficult emails. "Here are the likely objections this person will raise and how I plan to respond. What am I missing?" That prep work makes the actual conversation dramatically better.

Rule 4: Be honest in the prompt. The value of using AI for sensitive situations is that you can be completely candid. Don't sanitize the situation to make yourself look better. Give Claude the real picture — including where you might be in the wrong — so it can give you genuinely useful guidance. Nobody else will see the conversation.

Try This

Think of a sensitive situation you're currently navigating or one you handled recently. Open Claude and describe it fully — all the context, all the dynamics, all the things you can't say out loud in the office. Ask Claude to help you think through your approach, identify blind spots, and draft a plan. You'll likely find that just the process of articulating the situation to Claude helps you think more clearly about it, even before you read the response.

Module 10 — Lesson 97

The One-Session Concept-to-Pitch-Deck Workflow

The AI Prompting Course

I'm going to walk you through something I've actually done, multiple times, that still kind of blows my mind. Taking a raw idea — sometimes just a sentence or two — and turning it into a complete, presentable pitch deck in a single working session with Claude. Not a crappy one. One that I've actually used in real meetings.

When Ben and I were refining the Official AI pitch, I used this workflow to go from "here's what we're thinking" to "here's a deck we can walk an investor through" in about three hours. Previously, that process would have taken me a week — writing, designing, rewriting, restructuring, agonizing over slide order.

Let me break down exactly how I do this.

Phase 1: The Brain Dump (15 minutes)

I start by telling Claude everything. Not a polished pitch. Not a structured brief. A raw brain dump of what the idea is, who it's for, why I think it matters, what the market looks like, and what I'm trying to accomplish with the deck.

"I'm building a pitch deck for [concept]. Here's everything I know so far: [brain dump of 300-500 words covering the idea, the problem it solves, who the customer is, how it makes money, what the competitive landscape looks like, and what stage we're at]. The deck is for [audience — investors, clients, internal team, partners]. The tone should be [describe]. I want someone to walk away from this deck thinking [specific takeaway]."

This is the most important step. The quality of your brain dump determines the quality of everything that follows. Don't hold back, don't organize, don't edit yourself. Just get it all out. Claude can help you organize later — its job right now is to absorb the raw material.

Phase 2: The Narrative Architecture (20 minutes)

Next, I ask Claude to help me build the story structure before we touch any individual slide.

"Based on everything I just shared, propose a narrative arc for this deck. Not individual slides yet — the story. What's the emotional journey I'm taking the audience on? What do they believe at the beginning, and what do I need them to believe by the end? What's the key tension that makes this compelling?"

This step is crucial and it's what separates a good deck from a bad one. Most pitch decks fail not because the individual slides are bad, but because there's no story connecting them. They're just a collection of information in an arbitrary order.

Claude will usually give me a narrative structure like: establish the problem and make the audience feel its urgency, reveal why existing solutions fall short, introduce the new approach, prove it can work, show the opportunity size, explain why this team can execute, and close with a clear ask. But it'll be customized to my specific concept and audience, not generic.

I'll go back and forth here — adjusting the narrative, reordering elements, pushing Claude on which points deserve more emphasis. This is a conversation, not a single prompt.

Phase 3: Slide-by-Slide Content (60-90 minutes)

Once the narrative arc is solid, I work through slides one at a time. For each slide, I tell Claude what this slide needs to accomplish in the story, and I ask for the content.

"Slide 3 is where we reveal the problem. The audience is [type of investor]. They need to feel like this problem is urgent and large, but I don't want to be dramatic about it — I want to use a specific, concrete example that makes the problem tangible. Here's the real example I have: [specific example]. Write the headline and 3-4 bullet points for this slide. Keep the language sharp and specific — no filler phrases."

I do this for every slide. It sounds slow, but it's actually fast because each prompt is focused and the output is targeted. I'm not asking Claude to generate an entire deck at once (that produces generic mush). I'm collaborating on each piece of the story individually, with full context about how it fits into the whole.

For data-heavy slides, I give Claude the actual numbers and ask it to frame them compellingly. For team slides, I provide the real bios and ask Claude to highlight the most relevant experience for this particular audience. For competitive landscape slides, I describe the competitors and Claude helps me build a positioning framework that makes our differentiation clear.

Phase 4: Polish and Pressure-Test (30-45 minutes)

Once all the slide content exists, I do two things.

First, I ask Claude to review the whole deck as if it were the target audience:

"Here's the full deck content in order. Read through it as if you're [the target audience persona]. What questions would you have after seeing this? Where do you lose interest? Where are the logical gaps? What's the single weakest slide?"

This feedback loop is incredibly valuable. Claude will usually identify two or three things that need work — a slide that's redundant, an argument that's not fully supported, a transition that doesn't flow. I fix those in real-time.

Second, I pressure-test the hardest parts:

"What's the most likely objection an investor would raise after seeing this deck? Draft a version of slide 7 that pre-emptively addresses that objection."

By the end of this phase, I have a complete set of slide content that tells a cohesive story, is tailored to my audience, and has been pressure-tested against likely objections.

Phase 5: Design Transfer (30 minutes, outside Claude)

The one thing Claude can't do well is visual design. I take the content and build the actual slides in my tool of choice. But because the content is so well-structured, this step is mostly layout work — not creative work. The hard thinking is done. I'm just making it look good.

Why This Works

This workflow works because it mirrors how a great strategist would actually build a deck. Story first, slides second. Audience-aware at every step. Iterative, not one-shot. And because you're providing all the raw material and making all the strategic decisions, the output sounds like you — not like a template.

Try This

Take an idea you've been meaning to pitch — to your boss, a client, an investor, anyone. Set aside three hours. Follow this workflow step by step. Don't try to shortcut it by asking Claude to generate the whole deck at once. Do the brain dump. Build the narrative. Go slide by slide. Polish and pressure-test. I think you'll be genuinely surprised at what you produce in a single session.

Lesson 97 of 100

Module 10 — Lesson 98

How to Build an AI Operating System for Your Company — Not Just Ad Hoc Prompts

The AI Prompting Course

Here's the pattern I see at most companies right now. A few individuals are using AI well. Maybe one person on the marketing team has figured out how to use Claude for content. Someone in ops uses it for data analysis. A developer on the engineering team uses it for code review. They're each getting great results in their little pockets.

But it's all ad hoc. There's no system. No shared knowledge. No way for one person's breakthroughs to benefit everyone else. The company is getting 10% of the value it could be getting from AI because the usage is scattered, inconsistent, and entirely dependent on individual initiative.

I learned this the hard way at Mudita Studios. For months, I was the only one using AI effectively, and the team would come to me whenever they needed "the Claude thing done." That's not scalable, and it's not a competitive advantage. A competitive advantage is when the whole organization thinks with AI, not just the founder.

What an AI Operating System Actually Looks Like

When I say "AI operating system," I don't mean a piece of software. I mean a structured approach to how your company uses AI — documented, repeatable, and available to everyone. Think of it like your company's playbook, but for AI-assisted work.

Here are the components:

Prompt libraries for recurring tasks. Every company has tasks that happen repeatedly: writing proposals, summarizing meeting notes, creating client reports, drafting job descriptions, responding to support tickets. For each of these, you should have a documented prompt template that anyone on the team can use. Not a rigid script — a starting framework that captures the context and approach that produces good results.

At Mudita Studios, we have a shared doc with prompt templates for: client onboarding briefs, creative brief development, competitive analyses, weekly status reports, and proposal drafts. Each template includes the prompt itself, notes on what to customize, and examples of good outputs.

Context documents that feed into prompts. This is the part most companies miss. Your prompts are only as good as the context they contain. So build and maintain context documents that can be referenced or pasted into conversations: your brand voice guide, your ideal customer profiles, your product positioning, your competitive landscape overview, your company values and communication principles.

When someone on my team needs to write client-facing content, they don't start from scratch. They paste in our brand voice document and the specific client's context doc, then use the relevant prompt template. The output is consistently high quality because the inputs are consistently rich.

Quality standards and review processes. Not everything AI produces is ready to ship. Your operating system needs clear standards for what requires human review, what can go out with light editing, and what needs to be rewritten from scratch. At Mudita Studios, our rule is simple: anything client-facing gets reviewed by a human. Anything with factual claims gets verified. Internal documents can go out with lighter review.

A feedback loop for improving prompts. When someone on the team uses a prompt template and gets a great result, they note what worked. When they get a mediocre result, they note what they changed to fix it. Over time, the prompt library gets better. This is the difference between a static collection of prompts and a living system that improves with use.

How to Build This — The Practical Steps

Step 1: Audit your current AI usage. Spend a week tracking how your team uses AI. What tasks? What tools? What prompts? What results? You'll probably find that people are reinventing the wheel constantly — writing similar prompts for similar tasks without sharing what they've learned.

Step 2: Identify your top ten recurring AI tasks. These are the tasks that happen most frequently and would benefit most from standardization. Don't try to systematize everything at once. Start with the ten tasks that will deliver the most value if you get them right consistently.

Step 3: Build prompt templates for each. For each task, develop a prompt template that captures the key context, constraints, and approach. Test it with multiple team members. Refine it until it consistently produces good results across different users.

Step 4: Create your context library. Build the foundational documents that get reused across prompts. Brand voice, customer personas, product descriptions, competitive intelligence. These are the building blocks that make every prompt better.

Step 5: Train the team. Not a one-time workshop. An ongoing practice of sharing what works, reviewing outputs together, and building shared prompting instincts. I do a monthly "AI wins" session at Mudita Studios where people share their best prompt-output combinations from the past month. It takes thirty minutes and has probably been the single highest-ROI meeting on our calendar.

The Competitive Advantage

Here's why this matters beyond just efficiency. Right now, every company has access to the same AI tools. Claude, GPT, Gemini — they're available to everyone. The technology is not the differentiator. The system around the technology is.

A company with a well-built AI operating system will consistently outperform a company with better individual prompters but no system. Because the system scales. Individual skill doesn't — at least not without a system to capture and distribute it.

Try This

Start small. Pick the single most common task your team uses AI for. Build a prompt template for it — include the context, the structure, the constraints, and examples of good output. Share it with your team. Use it for two weeks. Then gather feedback and improve it. You've just built the first component of your AI operating system. Now do it nine more times.

Lesson 98 of 100
← Previous Next →
Module 10 — Lesson 99

The Feedback Loop: Training Your Prompting Instincts by Reviewing What Worked

The AI Prompting Course

I want to share something that made me meaningfully better at working with AI, and it's embarrassingly simple. I started saving my best conversations.

Not bookmarking them. Not vaguely remembering that I got a good result that one time. Actually saving them — the prompt, the output, and a quick note about why it worked. I've been doing this for over a year now, and the compound effect has been enormous. My prompts are better, my instincts are sharper, and I waste dramatically less time on approaches that don't work.

Most people treat every AI interaction as a standalone event. They prompt, they get a result, they use it or they don't, and they move on. Whatever they learned from that interaction evaporates. Next time they face a similar task, they start from zero again.

That's like cooking dinner every night but never remembering which recipes worked. You'd never get better.

Why Deliberate Review Matters

Here's the thing about getting good at prompting: it's a skill, and like all skills, it improves through deliberate practice. But deliberate practice requires feedback. You need to know what worked, what didn't, and why.

The problem is that most people never create that feedback loop. They have a vague sense of "I'm getting better at this" but couldn't tell you specifically what changed or what principles they've internalized. That's not deliberate practice. That's just accumulating hours.

When I started actively reviewing my best and worst AI interactions, I noticed patterns I'd completely missed during the interactions themselves.

For example, I realized that my best outputs almost always came from prompts where I included a specific example of what I wanted — not just describing the output, but showing a sample. "Here's a paragraph from our last successful proposal. I want this section to have the same tone and level of detail." That one insight, which I only noticed by reviewing multiple interactions side by side, improved my success rate dramatically.

I also noticed that my worst outputs correlated strongly with prompts where I was vague about the audience. I'd describe the task in detail but forget to mention who would be reading the output. Every time I added audience context, the quality jumped. I knew this intellectually from early lessons in this course, but reviewing my own patterns made it visceral and automatic.

My Review System

I keep it simple because anything complex won't survive contact with a real workday. Here's my system.

The save. When I get an output that's notably good or notably bad, I copy the prompt and the key part of the output into a running document. Takes about thirty seconds. I tag it with the task type — writing, strategy, analysis, coding, brainstorming — and whether the result was good or bad.

The weekly glance. Once a week, usually Friday afternoon, I spend ten minutes scrolling through what I saved that week. I'm looking for patterns. What did the good ones have in common? What did the bad ones share? Is there a technique I used once that worked really well and I should use more often?

The monthly review. Once a month, I do a deeper review. I look at the whole month's collection and ask myself: what's the single biggest thing I've learned? Is there a prompt template I should create from a one-off success? Is there a mistake I keep making that I need to consciously correct?

That's it. Thirty seconds per save, ten minutes per week, thirty minutes per month. The return on that time investment has been absurd.

What I've Learned From My Reviews

Let me share some of the specific insights I've pulled from this process, because they'll probably help you too.

Longer prompts aren't always better — but more specific prompts almost always are. I had a phase where I was writing very long prompts, thinking more was better. My review showed that length didn't correlate with quality. Specificity did. A three-sentence prompt with precise context outperformed a three-paragraph prompt with vague instructions almost every time.

The first prompt in a conversation sets the ceiling. I noticed that when I nailed the opening prompt — rich context, clear objective, right tone — the whole conversation was productive. When I started with a mediocre prompt, even extensive follow-up couldn't fully recover. It's like the first ten minutes of a meeting. If you set the right frame, everything flows. If you don't, you're fighting upstream the whole time.

Constraint prompts produce more creative results than open-ended prompts. This was counterintuitive. I assumed that giving Claude more freedom would produce more creative output. My review showed the opposite. "Give me ten tagline options — each under six words, none using the word 'innovative,' and each targeting a different emotional angle" produced wildly more interesting results than "brainstorm some taglines for us."

"What's wrong with this?" is more valuable than "Is this good?" When I asked Claude to evaluate my work, asking "what's wrong with this approach?" consistently surfaced more useful feedback than "what do you think of this?" The first question gives permission to be critical. The second invites agreement.

Building Your Own Feedback Loop

You don't need to copy my system exactly. But you do need some system. Here's the minimum viable version:

Start a document — Google Doc, Notion page, Apple Notes, whatever you'll actually use. Title it "AI Prompt Log." Every time you get a result that's notably good or notably bad, paste the prompt and make a one-line note about what made it work or fail. Do this for two weeks, then spend twenty minutes reviewing the collection.

I promise you'll find at least one insight that materially improves your prompting. Probably more.

Try This

Right now — not later, not tomorrow — open a new note and title it "AI Prompt Log." Then go back to your most recent Claude or ChatGPT conversation. Find the prompt that produced the best result. Copy it into your log. Write one sentence about why it worked. You've just started your feedback loop. The hardest part is starting. Now keep it going for two weeks and see what you learn about your own patterns.
